the weather variables 382xxix Foreword Water management in agriculture is increasingly critical as the world faces escalating challenges from climate change, water scarcity, and the demands of an increasing global population. The FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 56, “Crop Evapotranspiration—Guidelines for Computing Crop Water Requirements”, has been a cornerstone of agricultural water management since its first edition in 1998. The standardization of methodologies and the adoption of the Penman-Monteith ETo equation for computing the reference evapotranspiration and then estimating crop evapotranspiration enabled agronomists, researchers, policymakers, and farmers to effectively manage water resources, optimize irrigation practices, and promote sustainable agricultural systems. Its influence is globally reflected in thousands of citations and numerous studies validating its overall accuracy. In response to significant progress in both science and technology over the past two decades, FAO, in collaboration with key partners, has revised these guidelines. The revamped version not only reaffirms the value of the original guidelines but also integrates cutting-edge techniques such as remote sensing, the Internet of Things (IoT), and improved energy balance methods. These tools enhance the accuracy and applicability of the methods included in the guidelines, addressing the dynamic and complex needs of today’s agricultural systems. Moreover, the new version includes new approaches and datasets, making it adaptable to diverse regional conditions, especially in arid and semi-arid areas. The revised guidelines are also key for addressing the pressing challenges posed by climate change. Rising temperatures and shifting precipitation patterns reinforce the need for more precise information on crop water requirements and more efficient water use in agriculture. This revamped version introduces refined methodologies for optimizing water allocation, incorporating water-saving strategies, and advancing crop coefficient estimation and irrigation scheduling. These enhancements enable stakeholders to tailor their irrigation practices to local conditions, preserve water, and support food security while minimizing environmental impacts. By integrating the latest scientific knowledge with practical tools, the revised guidelines remain an indispensableresourceforagriculturetoeffectivelyrespondtochangingclimateandwateravailability conditions, and to become more efficient, inclusive, resilient and sustainable in the future. Lifeng Li Director, FAO Land and Water Divisionxxx

Introduction to evapotranspiration Introduction to evapotranspiration This chapter introduces the evapotranspiration process and explains the concepts of and the differences between reference crop evapotranspiration (ETo ) and crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions (ETc ) and various management and environmental conditions(ETc act ).Itexamines the factors that affect evapotranspiration, the units in which it is normally expressed and the way in which it can be determined. It also describes the field methods used to determine the ETc act , as well as the main remote sensing ET methods. 1.1 EVAPOTRANSPIRATION PROCESS The combination of two separate processes whereby water is transferred to the atmosphere, on the one hand from the soil surface by evaporation and, on the other hand, from the crop by transpiration, is referred to as evapotranspiration (ET). 1.1.1 Evaporation Evaporation is the process whereby liquid water is converted to water vapour (vaporization) and removed from the evaporating surface (vapour removal). Water evaporatesfromavarietyofsurfaces,suchaslakes,rivers,pavements,soilsandvegetation. Energy is required to change the state of the molecules of water from liquid to vapour. Direct solar radiation and, to a lesser extent, the ambient temperature of the air provide this energy. The driving force to remove water vapour from the evaporating surface is the difference between the water vapour pressure at the evaporating surface and that of the surrounding atmosphere. As evaporation proceeds, the surrounding air becomes gradually saturated and the process will slow down, and might stop if the wet air is not transferred to the atmosphere. The replacement of the saturated air with drier air depends greatly on wind speed. Hence, solar radiation, air temperature, air humidity and wind speed are the essential climatological parameters to consider when assessing the evaporation process. Where the evaporating surface is the soil surface, the degree of shading by the crop canopy and the amount of water available at the evaporating surface are other factors that affect the evaporation process. Frequent rains, irrigation and water transported upwards in a soil from a shallow water table wet the soil surface. Where the soil is able to supply water fast enough to satisfy the evaporation demand, the evaporation from the soil is determined only by the meteorological conditions. However, where the interval between rains and irrigation becomes large and the ability of the soil to conduct moisture to near the surface is small, the water content in the topsoil drops and the soil surface dries out. Under these circumstances, the limited availability of water exerts a controlling influence on soil evaporation. In the absence of any supply of water to the soil surface, evaporation decreases rapidly and may cease almost completely within a few days. 1.1.2 Transpiration Transpiration consists of the vaporization of liquid water contained in plant tissues and the vapour removal to the atmosphere. Crops predominately lose their water through stomata. These are small openings on the plant leaf through which gases and Chapter 12 Crop evapotranspiration water vapour pass (Figure 1.1). The water, together with some nutrients, is taken up by the roots and transported through the plant. The vaporization occurs within the leaf, namely in the intercellular spaces, and the vapour exchange with the atmosphere is controlled by the stomatal aperture. Nearly all water taken up is lost by transpiration and only a tiny fraction is used within the plant. FIGURE 1.1 Schematic representation of a stoma Atmosphere water vapour water Leaf cuticula epidermal cells mesophyll cells intercellular space Source: FAO56, 1998 Transpiration, like direct evaporation, depends on the energy supply, vapour pressure gradient and wind. Hence, radiation, air temperature, air humidity and wind terms should be considered when assessing transpiration. The soil water content and the ability of the soil to conduct water to the roots also determine the transpiration rate, as do waterlogging and soil water salinity. The transpiration rate is also influenced by crop characteristics, environmental aspects and cultivation practices. Different kinds of plants may have different transpiration rates. Not only the type of crop, but also the crop development stage, environment and management should be considered when assessing transpiration. 1.1.3 Evapotranspiration (ET) Evaporation and transpiration occur simultaneously and there is no easy way of distinguishing between the two processes unless specific instruments are used. Apart from the water availability in the topsoil, the evaporation from a cropped soil is mainly determined by the fraction of the solar radiation reaching the soil surface. This fraction decreases over the growing period as the crop develops and the crop canopy shades more and more of the ground area. When the crop is small, water is predominately lost by soil evaporation, but once the crop is well developed and completely covers the soil, transpiration becomes the main process. In Figure 1.2,3 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration the partitioning of evapotranspiration into evaporation and transpiration is plotted in correspondence to leaf area per unit surface of soil below it. At sowing, nearly 100 percent of ET comes from evaporation; while at full crop cover, more than 90 percent of ET comes from transpiration. FIGURE 1.2 The partitioning of evapotranspiration into evaporation and transpiration over the growing period for an annual field crop 100% 80% 60% 40% 20% 0% sowing harvest time partitioning of evapotranspiration leaf area index (LAI) LAI crop transpiration soil evaporation Source: FAO56, 1998 1.2 UNITS The evapotranspiration rate is normally expressed in millimetres (mm) per unit time. The rate expresses the amount of water lost from a cropped surface in units of water depth. The time unit can be an hour or fraction, a day, decade, month or even an entire growing period or year. As one hectare has a surface of 10 000 m2 and 1 mm is equal to 0.001 m, a loss of 1 mm of water corresponds to a loss of 10 m3 of water per hectare. In other words, 1 mm day-1 is equivalent to 10 m3 ha-1 day-1 (or 1 litre per square meter). Water depths can also be expressed in terms of energy received per unit area, i.e. the energy or heat required to vaporize free water. This energy, known as the latent heat of vaporization (λ), is a function of the water temperature. For example, at 20 °C, λ is about 2.45 MJ kg-1 . In other words, 2.45 MJ is needed to vaporize 1 kg or 0.001 m3 of water. Hence, an energy input of 2.45 MJ per m2 is able to vaporize 0.001 m or 1 mm of water; therefore, 1 mm of water is equivalent to 2.45 MJ m-2 (Example 1.1). The evapotranspiration rate when expressed in units of MJ m-2 day-1 is represented by λET, the latent heat flux. Table 1.1. summarizes the units used to express the evapotranspiration rate and the conversion factors.4 Crop evapotranspiration TABLE 1.1 Conversion factors for evapotranspiration depth volume per unit area energy per unit area* MJ m-2 day-1 mm day-1 m3 ha-1 day-1 l s-1 ha-1 1 mm day-1 1 10 0.116 2.45 1 m3 ha-1 day-1 0.1 1 0.012 0.245 1 l s-1 ha-1 8.640 86.40 1 21.17 1 MJ m-2 day-1 0.408 4.082 0.047 1 * For water with a density of 1 000 kg m-3 and at 20 ºC EXAMPLE 1.1 Converting evaporation from one unit to another On a summer day, net solar energy received at a lake reaches 15 MJ per square meter per day. If 80 percent of the energy is used to vaporize water, how large could the depth of evaporation be? From Table 1.1: Therefore: 1 MJ m-2 day-1 = 0.8 x 15 MJ m-2 day-1 = 0.8 x 15 x 0.408 mm day-1 = 0.408 4.9 mm day-1 mm day-1 The evaporation rate could be 4.9 mm day-1 1.3 FACTORS AFFECTING EVAPOTRANSPIRATION Weather parameters, crop characteristics, management and environmental aspects are factors affecting evaporation and transpiration. The related ET concepts presented in Figure 1.3 are discussed in the section on evapotranspiration concepts. 1.3.1 Weather parameters The principal weather parameters affecting evapotranspiration are radiation, air temperature, humidity and wind speed. Several procedures have been developed to assess the evaporation rate from these parameters. The evaporation power of the atmosphere is expressed by the reference crop evapotranspiration (ETo ). The reference crop evapotranspiration represents the evapotranspiration from a standardized vegetated surface. The ETo is described in detail later in this Chapter and in Chapters 2 and 4. 1.3.2 Influencing crop factors The crop type, variety and development stage should be considered when assessing the evapotranspiration from crops grown in large, well-managed fields. Differences in resistance to transpiration, crop height, crop roughness, reflection, ground cover and crop rooting characteristics result in different ET levels in different types of crops under identical environmental conditions. Crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions (ETc ) refers to the evaporating demand from crops that are grown in large fields under optimum soil water, excellent management and environmental conditions, and achieve full production under the given climatic conditions. The ETc is detailed in Chapters 6 and 7.5 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration FIGURE 1.3 Factors affecting evapotranspiration with indication of related ET concepts ET ET ° ETc ETc act T E weather parameters crop characteristics management environmental factors Source: FAO56, 1998 1.3.3 Management and environmental conditions Factors such as soil salinity, poor land fertility, limited application of fertilizers, the presence of hard or impenetrable soil horizons, the absence of control of diseases and pests and poor soil management may limit crop development and reduce evapotranspiration. Other factors to be considered when assessing ET are ground cover, plant density and the soil water content. The effect of soil water content on ET is conditioned primarily by the magnitude of the water deficit and the type of soil. On the other hand, too much water will result in waterlogging which might damage the root and limit root water uptake by inhibiting respiration. When assessing the ET rate, additional consideration should be given to the range of management practices that act on the climatic and crop factors affecting the ET process. Cultivationpracticesandthetypeofirrigationmethodscanalterthemicroclimate,affect the crop characteristics or affect the wetting of the soil and crop surface. A windbreak reduces wind velocities and decreases the ET rate of the field directly downwind of the barrier. The effect can be significant especially in windy, warm and dry conditions although evapotranspiration from the trees themselves may offset any reduction in the field. Soil evaporation in a young orchard, where trees are widely spaced, can be reduced by using a well-designed drip or trickle irrigation system. The drippers apply water directly to the soil near trees, thereby leaving the major part of the soil surface dry, and limiting the evaporation losses. The use of mulches, especially when the crop is small, is another way of substantially reducing soil evaporation. Anti-transpirants, such as stomata-closing, film-forming or reflecting material, reduce the water losses from the crop and hence the transpiration rate. ETc act is discussed in Chapters 8, 9 and 10. Wherefieldconditionsdifferfromthestandardconditions,correctionfactorsarerequired to adjust ETc to actual conditions, i.e. ETc act . The adjustment reflects the effect on crop evapotranspiration by the environmental and management conditions of the field.6 Crop evapotranspiration 1.4 EVAPOTRANSPIRATION CONCEPTS Distinctions are made (Figure 1.4) between reference crop evapotranspiration (ETo ), crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions (ETc ) and crop evapotranspiration under non-standard conditions, i.e. actual conditions (ETc act ). ETo is a climatic parameter expressing the evaporation power of the atmosphere. ETc refers to the evapotranspiration from excellently managed and disease-free, large, well-watered fields that achieve full production under the given climatic conditions. Due to sub optimal crop management and environmental constraints that affect crop growth and yield and any presence of water stress, evapotranspiration may be limited, and therefore the actual ETc under non-standard conditions, ETc act , requires a correction. 1.4.1 Reference crop evapotranspiration (ETo ) The evapotranspiration rate from a crop reference surface, not short of water, is called the reference crop evapotranspiration or reference evapotranspiration and is denoted as ETo . The reference surface is a hypothetical grass reference crop with specific characteristics. The use of other denominations and terms such as potential ET is strongly discouraged due to ambiguities in their definitions. The concept of reference evapotranspiration was introduced to study the evaporative demandoftheatmosphereindependentofcroptype,cropdevelopmentandmanagement practices. As water is abundantly available at the reference evapotranspiring surface, soil factors do not affect ETo . Relating ETo to a specific surface provides a reference to which ET from other surfaces can be related. It obviates the need to define a separate ET equation or level for each crop and stage of growth. ETo values measured or calculated at different locations or in different seasons are comparable as they refer to the ET from the same reference surface. The only factors affecting ETo are climatic parameters. Consequently, ETo is a climatic parameter and must be computed from weather data. ETo expresses the evaporating power of the atmosphere at a specific location and time of the year and does not consider crop characteristics and soil factors. Given the specific characteristics of the standardized hypothetical grass reference crop, that are not possible to consistently and accurately reproduce in a lysimeter, ETo , in general, cannot be easily estimated in an operational manner from lysimetric observations. Thus, the FAO Penman-Monteith method (FAO-PM ETo ) is recommended as the sole method for determining ETo . The method has been selected because it closely approximates grass ETo at the locations evaluated, is physically based, and explicitly incorporates both physiological and aerodynamic parameters. Moreover, procedures have been developed for estimating missing climatic parameters. Typical ranges for ETo values for different agroclimatic regions are given in Table 1.2. These values are intended to familiarize users with typical ranges that may be compared with locally computed values and are not intended for direct application. The calculation of the reference crop evapotranspiration is discussed in Part A of these guidelines (Box 1.1).7 1. The FAO-PM method is recommended as the method fordeterminingreferenceETo .Themethod,itsderivation,therequiredmeteorological data; the corresponding definition of the reference surface are described in this chapter. Also included is the correction of data collected over non-reference weather sites and application of QAQC to measured meteorological data. Chapter 3 - Meteorological variables and parameters: The FAO-PM ETo method requires radiation, air temperature, air humidity, and wind speed data. Calculation procedures to derive climatic parameters from the meteorological data are presented. Procedures to estimate missing meteorological variables required for calculating ETo are outlined. This Chapter discussed the source, measurement and computation of all data required for the calculation of the reference evapotranspiration (ETo ) by means of the FAO-PM method under circumstances, even in the case of missing climatic data or, for example, having only air temperature data. Different examples illustrate the various calculation procedures. Chapter 4 - Determination of ETo : The calculation of ETo by means of the FAO-PM ETo equation, with different time steps, from the principal weather parameters and with missing data is described. The determination of ETo from pan evaporation is also presented. 1.4.2 Crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions (ETc ) The crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions, denoted as ETc , is the evapotranspiration from disease-free, well-fertilized crops, grown in large fields, under optimum soil water conditions, and achieving full production under the given climatic conditions. It must be noted that the target is achieving full production and not necessarily attaining maximum evapotranspiration. In addition, any reductions to be made to ETc to exercise limitations specified by water use policy must be applied outside of the ETc calculation process. Theamountofwaterrequiredtosatisfytheevapotranspirationfromthecroppedfieldis defined as the crop water requirement. Although the values for crop evapotranspiration and crop water requirement are identical, crop water requirement refers to the amount of water that needs to be supplied naturally or through irrigation, while crop evapotranspiration refers to the amount of water that is transferred to the atmosphere through evapotranspiration. The irrigation water requirement basically represents the difference between the crop water requirement and effective precipitation. The irrigation water requirement also includes additional water for the leaching of salts and to compensate for the non-uniformity of water application. Cropevapotranspirationcanbecalculatedfromclimaticdataandbyintegratingdirectly the crop resistance, albedo and air resistance factors for the standardized reference crop into the Penman-Monteith combination equation. As there is still a considerable lack of information for different crops, the Penman-Monteith method is used for the estimation of the standard reference crop to determine its evapotranspiration rate, i.e.9 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration ETo . Experimentally, determined ratios of ETc /ETo , called crop coefficients (Kc ), are used to relate ETc to ETo as ETc = Kc ETo . Differences in leaf anatomy, stomatal characteristics, aerodynamic properties and even albedo cause the crop evapotranspiration to differ from the reference crop evapotranspiration under the same climatic conditions. Due to variations in the crop characteristics throughout its growing season, Kc for a given crop changes from sowing to harvest. The calculation of crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions (ETc ) is discussed in Part B of this guidelines (Box 1.2). BOX 1.2 Chapters concerning the calculation of crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions (ETc ) PART B Chapter 5 - Introduction to crop evapotranspiration (ETc ) and its measurement: This chapter introduces the user to the “Kc ETo ” approach for calculating crop evapotranspiration. The effects of characteristics that distinguish field crops from the reference grass crop are integrated into the crop coefficient Kc . Differences in evaporation and transpiration between field crops and the reference grass surface can be integrated into a single crop coefficient (Kc ) or separated into two coefficients: a basal crop (Kcb ) and a soil evaporation coefficient (Ke ), i.e. Kc = Kcb + Ke . The approach to follow should be selected as a function of the purpose of the calculation, the accuracy required and the data available. A review of ETc measurement using ground and remote observations and sensors is added. Chapter 6 - ETc and single crop coefficients (Kc ): This chapter deals with the calculation of crop evapotranspiration (ETc ) under standard, pristine, non-stressed cropping conditions. ETc is determined by the single crop coefficient approach whereby the effects of the various weather conditions are incorporated into ETo and the crop characteristics into the standard Kc coefficient. This chapter presents the tabulated Kc values of numerous annual and perennial crops as well as the main types of natural vegetation. Guidelines for the use and computation of Kc are presented. Chapter 7 - ETc and dual crop coefficient (Kc = Kcb + Ke ): This chapter presents the dual calculation method for crop evapotranspiration. Kc is split into two separate coefficients, one for crop transpiration (i.e. the basal crop coefficient Kcb ) and one for soil evaporation (Ke ). Guidelines for the daily computation are given and tabulated values for standard Kcb of annual and perennial crops are provided. 1.4.3 Crop evapotranspiration under non-standard conditions (ETc act ) The crop evapotranspiration under non-standard conditions, i.e. actual crop conditions (ETcact )istheevapotranspirationfromcropsgrownundermanagementandenvironmental conditions that differ from the standard conditions. When cultivating crops in fields, the real crop evapotranspiration may fall below ETc due to non-optimal conditions such as the presence of pests and diseases, soil salinity and/or low soil fertility that impact plant size or vigour, and by water shortage or waterlogging, as well as non-optimal planting density and other crop management issues. This may result in scanty plant growth, low canopy density and may reduce the evapotranspiration rate below ETc .10 Crop evapotranspiration The crop evapotranspiration under non-standard conditions is calculated by using a water stress coefficient Ks and/or by adjusting Kc for all kinds of other stresses and environmental constraints on crop evapotranspiration. The adjustment of ETc for water stress, management and environmental constraints is discussed in Part C of these guidelines (Box 1.3). BOX 1.3 Chapters concerning the calculation of crop evapotranspiration under non-standard conditions (ETc act ) PART C Chapter 8 - ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions: This chapter discusses the reduction in transpiration induced by soil moisture stress or soil and water salinity. The resulting evapotranspiration will decrease compared to the crop evapotranspiration under standard conditions. The evapotranspiration is computed by using a water stress coefficient, Ks , describing the effect of water stress on crop transpiration. Various parameters related to the water and salinity stress are defined and tabulated for numerous crops and particular attention is paid to the soil water conditions as affected by stresses. Chapter 9 - Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation: Procedures that can be used to make adjustments to the Kc to account for less than perfect growing conditions or stand characteristics are discussed. The procedures can also be used to determine Kc for agricultural crops not listed in the tables of Part B. The estimation of crop coefficients from ground cover and height is discussed in detail, including tabulated computational parameters. Chapter 10 - ETc under various crop management practices: This chapter discusses various types of management practices that may cause the values for Kc and ETc to deviate from the standard conditions described in Part B. Adjustment procedures for Kc to account for surface mulches, intercropping, small areas of vegetation, management induced stress and non-growing periods under various types of surface conditions are presented. In addition, strategies, methods and technologies to achieve water savings are discussed with particular attention to irrigation scheduling. 1.5 DETERMINING EVAPOTRANSPIRATION 1.5.1 ET measurement and estimation Evapotranspiration is not easy to measure. Specific devices and accurate measurements of various physical parameters include the soil water balance in lysimeters or the field, as well as the energy balance performed on the ground or through remote sensing, and are required to determine evapotranspiration. The methods are commonly expensive, demanding in terms of accuracy of measurements and can only be fully exploited by well-trained research personnel. Although the methods are inappropriate for routine measurements, they remain important for the evaluation of ET estimates obtained by more indirect methods. This section describes the main methods for measuring actual ETc or indirectly deriving ETc estimates, and emphasizes the need for accuracy in measurements, describing some essential aspects that affect measurement accuracy.11 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration When used correctly, the accuracy of the described measurement systems can be very high. However, in many instances, understanding the underlying physics of turbulence and heat, water and energy transfer that govern the measurement is crucial to avoid subtle biases and mistakes that will compromise the accuracy of the data. For the purpose of producing representative and reliable data, the deployment of equilibrium air-boundary layer systems, such as eddy covariance, the Bowen ratio, and scintillometers, must abide by fetch requirements and minimum equipment heights. Energy balance methods like the Bowen ratio and scintillometers must incorporate representative measurements of net radiation and soil heat flux density, which typically require multiple locations for sensors, especially when used in spatially non-uniform systems such as riparian and other non-agricultural systems. Measurements of eddy covariance and scintillometry require physically based “corrections”, for example, to obtain the so-called energy balance closure where the sum of λET, sensible heat flux and ground heat flux equals the net radiation as introduced in the next section. Many practitioners may lack the necessary training or experience to use ETc measurement methods, which can lead to significant measurement biases. Lysimeters and soil water balance methods are older techniques that can potentially provide reliable measurements, but only if certain fundamental criteria for the representativeness of vegetation and environmental conditions are met. Sap flow sensors rely on empirical correction factors derived from the physiology and anatomy of species under consideration, and on the accuracy of the scaling techniques used to go from a branch or tree to a group of plants and larger area estimates of ET, including evaporation from soil. In order to achieve high integrity data, the following sections provide a brief overview of ET measurement and estimation systems, as well as different problems and requirements related to each one of these. 1.5.2 Energy balance and micro-climatological methods Evaporation of water requires relatively large amounts of energy, either in the form of sensible heat or radiant energy. Therefore, the evapotranspiration process is governed by energy exchange at the vegetation surface and is limited by the amount of energy available. Because of this limitation, it is possible to estimate the evapotranspiration rate by applying the principle of energy conservation. The energy arriving at the surface must equal the energy leaving the surface for the same period. The energy leaving the surface includes the energy consumed by the λET process. The λ variable represents the latent heat of vaporization, described earlier, that is required per unit of ET. The use of the word “latent” means that this energy is “hidden” in the vapour that is produced by the ET. All primary fluxes of energy should be considered when deriving an energy balance equation. The equation for an evaporating surface can be written as: Rn - G - λET - H = 0 (1.1) where Rn is the net radiation, H is the sensible heat flux, G is the soil heat flux and λET is the latent heat flux. The various terms can be either positive or negative. Positive Rn supplies energy to the surface and positive G, λET and H remove energy from the surface (Figure 1.5).12 Crop evapotranspiration FIGURE 1.5 Schematic representation of the diurnal variation of the components of the energy balance above a well-watered transpiring surface on a cloudless day time (hour) energy arriving/leaving the surface

ET H G Rn λ Source: FAO56, 1998 In Equation 1.1. only vertical fluxes are considered and any energy that is being transferred horizontally, by local advection of dry air or heat near the edge of a field that is surrounded by dryness, is ignored. Therefore, the equation is to be applied for large,extensivesurfacesofhomogeneousvegetationonly.Inaddition,themethodology, especially the estimation of ETo , does not apply to greenhouses and other protected cultivation facilities where aerodynamic conditions and energy exchange can be much different than those experienced for open agricultural fields. The equation is restricted to the four components: Rn , λET, H and G. Other energy terms, such as heat stored or released in the plant, or the energy used in metabolic activities and photosynthesis, are not considered. These terms account for only a small fraction of the daily net radiation and can be considered negligible when compared with the other four components. The latent heat flux (λET) representing the evapotranspiration fraction can be derived from the energy balance equation if all other components are known. Net radiation (Rn ) and soil heat fluxes (G) can be measured or estimated from climatic parameters. Measurements of the sensible heat (H) are however complex and cannot be easily obtained. H requires accurate measurement of temperature gradients above the surface. Anothermethodforestimatingevapotranspirationisusingamasstransfermethod.This approach considers the vertical movement of small parcels of air (eddies) above a large homogeneous surface. The eddies transport material (water vapour) and energy (heat and momentum) from and towards the evaporating surface. By assuming steady state conditions and that the eddy transfer coefficients for water vapour are proportional to those for heat and momentum, the evapotranspiration rate can be computed from the vertical gradients of air temperature and water vapour above the surface via the Bowen Ratio Energy Balance (BREB). The Bowen ratio BR is defined as the ratio of H to λET (BR = H/λET) and λET is determined as λET = (Rn −G)/(1+BR).13 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration Other direct estimation methods use gradients of wind speed and water vapour to directly estimate the vapour flux. These methods and other methods, such as Eddy Covariance (EC), require accurate measurement of vapour pressure, and air temperature or wind speed at different levels above the surface. Therefore, their application is restricted to primarily research situations. The BREB technique performs best when ET is not constrained by soil water so that λET tends to be larger than H and relative error is reduced. The Bowen ratio rises and the relative error in ET increases as water becomes less readily available. Requirements of the BREB system include: • sufficient distance of fetch to establish an equilibrium boundary layer (EBL) deeper than the instrument height (this requirement is frequently violated); • sufficient elevation of the instrument above the canopy to avoid the roughness sublayer where gradients of temperature and vapour pressure are difficult to establish and may not be in proportion to the true BR; • representative and accurate measurement of Rn and G; and • multiple net radiometers and soil heat flux plates, required when measurements are performed over heterogeneous or sparse areas. Eddycovariance(EC)systemsareoftenusedforcropETmonitoringduetotheirrelative simplicity of setup, moderate sensor prices and, depending on the equipment settings, the abilitytosimultaneouslymeasureH,λETandCO2 fluxes.Theconceptofeddycovariance (also known as eddy correlation) refers to the statistical covariance between vertical fluxes of vapour or sensible heat within the upward and downward legs of turbulent eddies. Quick response sensors must be used to make high speed measurements of air temperature (T), vertical wind velocity (w), and mean vapour pressure (e) or mean specific humidity (q), normally at frequencies of 5-20 Hz (5-20 times per second), typically at 10 Hz. Additionally, ET can be calculated as a residual from the energy balance equation as λET = Rn – G – H, which has the advantage of eliminating the need to measure q, although it does require accurate measurements of Rn and G. A large number of corrections are required with the EC procedure, and there is a variety of software available for EC flux processing. Many studies have reported a lack of energy balance closure for EC data since the sum of the measured turbulent fluxes (H + λET) usually underestimates the measured available energy (Rn − G). This underestimation can often be as large as 30 percent. Potential causes for the lack of closure may be horizontal advection, storage of heat in the canopies, energy used by photosynthesis, change in storage of heat in the developing boundary layer below the instrumentation (causing flux divergence), errors in the measurements of turbulent fluxes, error in measurement of the vertical wind velocity by the sonic anemometer, and inadequate or too much separation between the anemometer and humidity sensor. Additionally, an error can occur in Rn or G measurements due to miscalibration and improper installation or nonrepresentative placement of sensors over nonuniform vegetation such as with rows of trees. The literature is not conclusive about the method to be followed for resolving the energy imbalance, although the most important issue is that the EC measurements are consistent with the conservation of energy. Generally, users force the energy balance closure using the residual technique by scaling H and λET in the same proportion until the sum equals Rn – G, or by assuming correct H measurement and14 Crop evapotranspiration underestimation of λET. Days associated with vertical regional advection of warm, dry air may show a poor energy balance closure as compared to the rest of the days. Therefore, energy balance closure cannot be considered as a data quality assessment, since closure error can still be present even if data acquired by the EC system are of good quality and vice versa. The EC system requirements are as follows: • a uniform fetch with sufficient distance to create an equilibrium boundary layer (EBL) deeper than the instrument height (this is frequently violated); • sufficient elevation of instruments above the canopy to reduce roughness sublayer impacts and to increase mean eddy size to match the sensor path lengths; • accomplishing the necessary data “corrections”; • accurate,highfrequencyinstrumentationandagoodknowledgeandunderstanding of the physics of turbulence; and • utilization of net radiation and soil heat flux measurements if energy balance closure error is to be identified and corrected. A scintillometer is an optical device that measures small fluctuations in the refractive index of air caused by temperature, humidity, and pressure induced variations in density. The most common scintillometers measure sensible heat flux, H, by relating the structure parameter to a temperature parameter and the Monin-Obukhov stability parameters. A scintillometer system for measuring sensible heat flux consists of an optical transmitter and a receiver at the ends of an atmospheric propagation path. The receiver detects and evaluates the intensity of fluctuations of the transmitted signal, called scintillations. λET is estimated as a residual of the surface energy balance. To obtain λET, measurements of Rn and G are needed, which can be problematic and biased in heterogeneous and sparse vegetation systems. Scintillometers are relatively simple to operate and maintain. Their cost can equal or exceed those of BREB and EC systems. A distinct advantage of scintillometry is the ability to derive sensible heat flux that is integrated over a long transect, up to several km in length. This integration is especially useful for measuring H over complex natural vegetation where spatial variation can have scales of hundreds of meters. However, insufficient representativeness of Rn and G measurement over a transect can greatly reduce the accuracy of ET determined from the scintillometer based integration of H. Rn measurements may need augmentation by satellite-based images of albedo and surface temperature. Soil heat flux densities can vary in the same manner along a mixed transect. 1.5.3 Soil water balance Evapotranspiration can also be determined by measuring the various components of the soil water (SW) balance. The method consists of assessing the incoming and outgoing water flux into the crop root zone over some time period (Figure 1.6). Irrigation (I) and rainfall (P) add water to the root zone. Part of I and P might be lost by surface runoff (RO) and by deep percolation (DP) that will eventually recharge the water table. Water might also be transported upward by capillary rise (CR) from a shallow water table towards the root zone or even be transferred horizontally by subsurface flow in (SFin ) or out (SFout ) of the root zone. In many situations, however, except under conditions with large slopes, SFin and SFout are minor and can be ignored. Soil evaporation and crop transpiration deplete water15 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration from the root zone. If all fluxes other than evapotranspiration (ET) can be assessed, the evapotranspiration can be deduced from the change in soil water content (ΔSW) over the selected time period: ET= I + P - RO - DP+CR - ΔSF - ΔSW (1.2) A positive ΔSW indicates an increase in total soil water content. Some fluxes such as subsurface flow, deep percolation and capillary rise from a water table are difficult to assess when very short time periods are considered. The soil water balance method can generally give ET estimates over long time periods on the order of week-long or ten-day periods but can only provide accurate daily ET estimates when appropriate and highly accurate soil water content sensors are adopted and appropriate modelling software is used. It may be difficult to capture the increase in evaporation following a large irrigation event when soils are such that one day or more is required for water within the root zone to drain. FIGURE 1.6 Soil water balance of the root zone root zone deep percolation subsurface flow subsurface flow runoff rainfall irrigation transpiration evaporation capillary rise in out Source: FAO56, 1998 Evapotranspirationfrommassbalanceoverlargeareas,alsoreferredtoastheinflow outflow or mass balance method, is a water balance technique that can be applied over large, integrated areas of land and water to develop watershed scale estimates of ET. These estimates may be useful for calibration of or comparison to watershed and remote-sensing based models. Examples of ET by water balance are applications to large areas such as valleys where the inflow and outflow are determined from stream flow and precipitation measurements, and where the basin is confined to eliminate other significant sources of inflow or outflow such as groundwater underflow. Such applications generally provide only gross seasonal estimates of the average ET from16 Crop evapotranspiration agricultural and non-agricultural areas within the watershed. The results represent ET from a combination of vegetation types and are generally applicable only to climatic, vegetation and water availability conditions similar to those existing in the study area. Mass balances require precipitation as the primary input and consequently, the ET estimate can only be as accurate as the precipitation estimate. Fine resolution grids of precipitation on the scale of a km or less may be required in areas having substantial precipitation from convective storms and areas having substantial relief with orographic influence on precipitation amounts. As with soil water balance applications, precipitation data from standard gauges may need to be corrected for under-catch. Accurate measurement of basin or irrigation project outflow is necessary as is locating the measurement area boundaries to minimize groundwater inflows and outflows. 1.5.4 Lysimeters By isolating the crop root zone from its environment and controlling the processes that are difficult to measure, the different terms in the soil water balance equation can be determined with greater accuracy. This is done in lysimeters where the crop grows in isolated tanks filled with either disturbed or undisturbed soil. In precision weighing lysimeters, where the water loss is directly measured by measuring the change of mass of the complete system, evapotranspiration can be obtained with an accuracy of a few hundredths of a millimetre, and small time periods such as an hour or even less can be considered. In non-weighing drainage lysimeters, the evapotranspiration for a given time period is determined by deducting the drainage water, collected at the bottom of the lysimeters, from the total water input. Non-weighing drainage lysimeters can generally only provide ET information at a weekly or longer time scale. On the contrary, static water table lysimeters can be very accurate and provide for daily time step or longer measurements since ET corresponds to the amount of water transferred during the considered time period from the groundwater. These lysimeters are of particular interest for studies referring to the contribution of the groundwater to the crop under study. They are commonly used to define the target depth of the water table, or when the groundwater is saline. Many studies have used lysimeter ET measurements for evaluation, calibration and validation of different ET approaches. However, lysimeter measurements are very sensitive to environmental aspects, which are often not well interpreted or even taken into account. Moreover, there tends to be a lack of necessary information reported for associated meteorological and environmental conditions, and processing of lysimeter data in published scientific papers, which calls into question the validity and representativeness of certain reported data sets. A requirement of lysimeters is that the vegetation both inside and immediately outside of the lysimeter be perfectly matched (same height and leaf area index) to that of the surrounding field. This requirement has historically not been closely adhered to in a majorityoflysimeterstudiesandhasresultedinseverelyerroneousandunrepresentative ETc and Kc data sets. Typical positive measurement biases include a clothesline effect where lysimeter vegetation is taller than that outside and situations where vegetation from inside the lysimeter creeps outside the lysimeter due to lower vegetation density outside the lysimeter. Lower vegetation density immediately outside a lysimeter can occur due to foot traffic or soil compaction or a thick lysimeter boundary. The outward extension of vegetation from the lysimeter can thereby increase the amount of solar radiation captured by lysimeter vegetation and converted to ET. This causes an over measurement and overstatement of true field-wide ET.17 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration In a similar manner, one serious problem in lysimeter data is produced by a so-called “bloom effect” on small lysimeters, which means that the area of exposure of plant canopy to solar radiation is higher than the considered effective area of the lysimeter. Finally, radiation emission from warmed sides of a lysimeter into the inner vegetation can increase lysimeter ET to rates higher than the surrounding field. Recent studies have established guidelines for the requirements of lysimeter-based ET measurements, including a minimum fetch of 50 m around the lysimeter, while plant population, crop height, leaf area, ground coverage, irrigation water management and soil conditions inside the lysimeter must be identical to those in the rest of the field. In conclusion,becausealysimeterrepresentsasmallsampleofETforanentirefieldcondition, it is essential that the lysimeter represents an exact reproduction of the field condition. As lysimeters are difficult and expensive to construct and as their operation and maintenance require special care, their use is limited to specific and careful research purposes. 1.5.5 Sap flow systems Sap flow methods have been used to estimate transpiration from individual plants. The systems introduce a small source of heat into the trunk or branch of a plant and estimate the flow of water in the xylem by either measuring the velocity of a heat pulse carried away from the heat source in the transpiration stream or by the dissipation of heat energy in the stem due to convection in the transpiration stream. These methods do not measure the direct evaporation component of ET, but only the plant transpiration component. This can be an advantage if the goal is to measure plant water use, but is a disadvantage if the goal is to estimate total ET over an extended heterogeneous surface. Three main methods can be employed. Literature suggests that no one set of theory and instrumentation is applicable to all sizes or species of trees. Heat pulse velocity measurements can determine transpiration in forests and orchards, where the sensors can be applied to any woody stem larger than about 40 mm in diameter. The system is inexpensive, easy to install, and suited to automated data collection. The most commonly used correction method is the compensation heat pulse method. The method may require a knowledge of the wood anatomy of the species being measured to determine sapwood area. The Granier heat dissipation method is suited to transpiration measurements in trees, and it involves inserting a heating needle into the sapwood layer of a large trunk or branch. Heat is applied at a constant rate rather than as a pulse. Different calibration coefficients may be needed for each tree species and the method assumes that sap flow is radially uniform around the trunk. The tissue heat balance method is the most direct sap flux method with the fewest assumptions. It involves wrapping a heating wire around an entire branch segment and measuring conductive heat losses upstream, downstream and radially away from the heat source by thermocouples referenced to the temperature at the heating wire. The energy balance is conducted on the entire stem segment so that no knowledge of the xylem anatomy or area of conducting sapwood is needed. Unfortunately, this is the most difficult method to implement and can only be applied to branches under 50 mm in diameter, because the heat source must be able to uniformly heat the entire segment of the branch around which it is wrapped. Whereas heat pulse and heat dissipation sensors placed in a tree trunk can measure transpiration through the entire tree, numerous tissue heat balance sensors, placed on individual branches would be needed to sample sap flux on a single tree, and a scaling method would then be needed to extrapolate from the branches to the whole tree.18 Crop evapotranspiration The heat pulse and thermal dissipation methods must be calibrated for each new application, ideally through the use of weighing lysimeters or by pulling water through stem sections of the plants of interest while measuring velocity and heat dissipation parameters with sap flux. Scaling ET from individual limbs to full plants and from full plants to large areas is uncertain due to differences among tree structure, radiation interception per tree, and water availability. Shaded limbs tend to have lower rates of transpiration than sunlit limbs, and limbs lower in a canopy tend to have less aerodynamic exchange of energy and vapor. Besides scaling of transpiration from specific species of trees, additional uncertainty in areal ET is caused by stands of multiple species, the presence of understory vegetation, and evaporation from bare soil. 1.5.6 Remote Sensing Remote sensing from vegetation indices. The estimation of ET using a vegetation index (VI) is possible due to the generally close correspondence between vegetation amount and transpiration, where, as vegetation cover increases, leaf area increases, and the relative rate of transpiration increases. Generally, the estimation of ET is made using the Kc act ETo approach where the value for Kc act is estimated from the VI. The VI is usually updated over time using satellite or drone imagery. Because the Kc act from VI represents the transpiration component, challenges with VI-based methods include estimating evaporation from bare soil associated with precipitation and irrigation events. Also challenging is the estimation of reduced ET associated with soil-water shortage, because these processes are not generally shown by the VI. Under conditions of long periods of water stress, vegetation growth is expected to be less and therefore the VI may be less. However, during conditions of short periods of water stress, the VI may appear to be at unstressed levels even though the ET may decline due to partial or full closure of stomates. Therefore, VI-based methods are often best used to estimate ET under adequate water supplies. More discussion on estimating Kc from VI is given in Chapter 5 and Chapter 9. Remote sensing from energy balance. Remote sensing surface energy balance (RSSEB) methodstakeadvantageofthephysicalfactthatconversionofliquidwatertothevapour form requires very substantial amounts of energy. This conversion of liquid to vapour during the evaporation process involves the same physical requirements whether the process is the transpiration of water from inside a plant leaf or the evaporation of water from a wet soil surface. Approximately 2.4 million Joules (2.4 megajoules) of energy are required to evaporate one kilogram of water at room temperature. The energy source can be in the form of heat energy from the surrounding air (this is termed “sensible heat” since it can be sensed with a thermometer), and the energy source also includes the radiant energy from the sun and atmosphere in the form of photons contacting the evaporating surface. Energy from photons is converted into freeing water molecules from a liquid to vapour state. The surface energy balance determination of ETc act is usually conducted using Equation 1.1. RSSEB is useful in that it produces the combined ET including both transpiration from vegetation and evaporation of water from wet soil surfaces. Thermal sensor based RSSEB models are capable of estimating actual crop ET, ETc act . RSSEB-based estimation of ETc act is useful in situations where ETc act is lower than the potential crop ET, ETc , during times of water shortage or due to poor water management. The more dependable and universal satellite-based models employ a complete surface energy balance (SEB), introduced in Equation 1.1, where ET is computed as a residual of surface energy. This determination requires a thermal imager onboard the satellite. The focus of RSSEB is often on the production of field-scale resolution maps of actual ET, meaning those produced from moderately high resolution thermal sensor-equipped19 1. Introduction to evapotranspiration satellites such as Landsat that have 30-m pixel size, a thermal sensor, and a relatively frequent revisit time (8 days for Landsat). RSSEB can help evaluate variation in ETc act across large populations of fields having the same crop type but differing cropping dates, crop varieties or water management. RSSEB provides a valuable complement to the Kc ETo approach. RSSEB can be used to confirm or calibrate crop coefficients for new crops or in new areas. In turn, the Kc ETo approach is useful in checking or confirming ETc act estimates made by RSSEB models. Some RSSEB models, for example, the METRIC model, utilize reference ET to establish upper limits on the estimated ETc act . 1.5.7 ET computed from meteorological data Owing to the difficulty of obtaining accurate field measurements, ET is commonly computed from weather data. A large number of empirical or semi-empirical equations have been developed for assessing crop or reference crop evapotranspiration from meteorological data. Some of the methods are only valid under specific climatic and agronomic conditions and cannot be applied under conditions different from those under which they were originally developed. Numerous researchers have analyzed the performance of the various calculation methods for different locations. As a result of an Expert Consultation held in May 1990, the FAO Penman-Monteith ETo method is recommended as the standard method for the definition and computation of the grass reference evapotranspiration (ETo ). The ET from crop surfaces under standard conditions is determined by crop coefficients (Kc ) that relate ETc to ETo . The ET from crop surfaces under non-standard conditions is adjusted by a water stress coefficient (Ks ) and/or by modifying the crop coefficient (ETc act = Ks Kc ETo = Kc act ETo ). 1.5.8 ET estimated from pan evaporation Evaporation from an open water surface provides an index of the integrated effects of radiation, air temperature, air humidity and wind on evapotranspiration. However, differences in the water and cropped surface produce significant differences in the water evaporation from an open water surface and the crop canopy. The pan has proved its practical value and has been used successfully to estimate reference evapotranspiration by observing the evaporation loss from a water surface and applying empirical coefficients to relate pan evaporation to ETo . The procedure is outlined in Chapter 4.21 2. FAO Penman-Monteith ETo equation FAO Penman-Monteith ETo equation This chapter introduces the user to the need to standardize one method to compute reference evapotranspiration (ETo ) from meteorological data. The FAO Penman Monteith method is recommended as the sole standard ETo method for determining reference evapotranspiration. The method, its derivation, the required meteorological data and reference weather site conditions, and the corresponding definition of the reference surface are described in this chapter. Also included is the correction of data collected over non-reference weather sites and the application of Quality Assurance and Quality Control (QAQC) to measured meteorological data. 2.1 NEED FOR A STANDARD ETO METHOD A large number of more or less empirical methods have been developed over the last 80 years by numerous scientists and specialists worldwide to estimate evapotranspiration using different climatic variables. Relationships were often subject to rigorous local calibrations and have proved to have limited global validity. Testing the accuracy of ET methods under a new set of conditions is laborious, time-consuming and costly, and yet evapotranspiration data are frequently needed at short notice for project planning or irrigation scheduling design. To meet this need, guidelines were developed and published in the FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 24 “Crop water requirements”. To accommodate users with different data availability, four methods were presented to calculate the reference crop evapotranspiration (ETo ): the Blaney Criddle, radiation, modified Penman and pan evaporation methods. The modified Penman method was considered to offer the best results with a minimum possible error in relation to a living grass reference crop. It was expected that the pan method would give acceptable estimates, depending on the location of the pan. The radiation method was suggested in FAO Paper No. 24 for areas where available climatic data include measured air temperature and sunshine, cloudiness or radiation, but not measured wind speed and air humidity. Finally, the publication proposed the use of the Blaney Criddle method for areas where available climatic data cover air temperature data only. These climatic methods to calculate ETo were all calibrated for ten-day or monthly calculations, not for daily or hourly calculations. The Blaney-Criddle method was recommended for periods of one month or longer. For the pan method, it was suggested that calculations should be done for periods of ten days or longer. Users have not always respected these conditions and calculations have often been done on daily time steps. Advances in research and the more accurate assessment of crop water use have revealed weaknesses in the methodologies. Numerous researchers have analyzed the performance of the four methods for different locations. Although the results of such analyses could have been influenced by site or measurement conditions, or by bias in weather data collection, it became evident that the proposed methods do not behave the same way in different locations around the world. Deviations from computed to observed values were often found to exceed ranges indicated by FAO. The modified Penman was frequently found to overestimate ETo , even by up to 20 percent for low evaporative conditions. The other FAO recommended equations showed variable adherence to the reference crop evapotranspiration standard of clipped, cool-season grass. Chapter 222 Crop evapotranspiration To evaluate the performance of these and other estimation procedures under different climatological conditions, a major study was undertaken under the auspices of the Committee on Irrigation Water Requirements of the American Society of Civil Engineers (ASCE). The ASCE study analyzed the performance of 20 different methods, using detailed procedures to assess the validity of the methods compared to a set of carefully screened lysimeter data from 11 international locations with a broad range of climatic conditions. The study proved very revealing and showed the widely varying performance of the methods under different climatic conditions. In a parallel study commissioned by the European Community, a consortium of European research institutes evaluated the performance of various evapotranspiration methods using data from different lysimeter studies in Europe. The studies confirmed the overestimation of the modified Penman introduced in FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 24, and the variable performance of the different methods depending on their adaptation to local conditions. The comparative studies may be summarized as follows: • The semi-empirical Penman methods may require local calibration of the wind function to achieve satisfactory results. • Theradiationmethodsshowgoodresultsinhumidclimateswheretheaerodynamic term is relatively small, but performance in arid conditions is erratic and tends to underestimate evapotranspiration. • Temperature methods remain empirical and require local calibration in order to achieve satisfactory results. A possible exception is the 1985 Hargreaves-Samani method, which has shown reasonable ETo results with a global validity. However, it requires validated parameterization. • Pan evapotranspiration methods clearly reflect the shortcomings of predicting crop evapotranspiration from open water evaporation collected from a shallow pan. The methods are susceptible to the microclimatic conditions under which the pans are operating and the rigour of station maintenance. Their performance proves erratic. • The relatively accurate and consistent performance of the FAO Penman-Monteith approach (FAO-PM ETo ) in both arid and humid climates has been indicated in both the ASCE and European studies. The analysis of the performance of the various calculation methods reveals the need for formulating a standard method for the computation of ETo . The FAO-PM method is recommended as the sole standard method. It is a method with a strong likelihood of correctly predicting ETo in a wide range of locations and climates and has provision for application in data-short situations. The use of older FAO or other reference ET methods is no longer encouraged. As a result, the FAO-PM method was utilized in the 1998 edition of FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 56 as the sole standard ETo method. Its adoption has been widely accepted by the international community as attested by citations in more than 47 000 publications and a large number of tests on overall accuracy. The FAO-PM ETo method continues to be the ETo method utilized in this 2025 edition of FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 56.23 2. FAO Penman-Monteith ETo equation 2.2 FORMULATION OF THE PENMAN-MONTEITH EQUATION 2.2.1 Penman-Monteith combination equation In 1948, Penman combined the energy balance with the mass transfer method and derived an equation to compute the evaporation from an open water surface from standard climatological records of sunshine, temperature, air humidity and wind speed. This so-called combination method was further developed by many researchers, including Monteith in 1965, and was extended to cropped surfaces by introducing resistance factors. The resistance nomenclature distinguishes between aerodynamic resistance and surface resistance factors (Figure 2.1). The surface resistance parameters are often combined into one parameter, the “bulk” surface resistance parameter, which operates in series with the aerodynamic resistance. The surface resistance, rs , describes the resistance of vapour flow through stomata openings, total leaf area and soil surface. The aerodynamic resistance, ra , describes the resistance to vapour and heat flow from the vegetation upward and involves friction from air flowing over vegetative surfaces (Figure 2.1). Although the exchange process in a vegetation layer is too complex to be fully described by the two resistance factors, good correlations can be obtained between measured and calculated evapotranspiration rates, especially for a uniform grass reference surface. FIGURE 2.1 Simplified representation of the (bulk) surface and aerodynamic resistances for water vapour flow aerodynamic resistance (bulk) surface resistance evaporating surface reference level stomatal soil cuticular a i r fl o w ra rs Source: FAO56, 1998 The derivation and formulation of the Penman and Penman-Monteith combination equations do require a degree of exactness in the representation of temperature and vapour pressure as measured above the surface to that which occurs over a fully transpiring surface. This is because vapour transport from the surface increases vapour pressure a few meters above the surface and cools the air temperature. The Penman Monteith (PM) combination equation expects these behaviours of the near-surface24 Crop evapotranspiration boundary layer to occur and incorporates these expectations into the ET estimate. Applying the combination equation with high temperature and low vapour pressure measured over a non-transpiring surface instructs the equation to estimate ET that is biased high. This occurs because the equation, through its formulation, is being instructed to estimate the ET that explains the non-representative high temperature and low vapour pressure being assumed to exist above a fully transpiring surface. Weather data collected above a non- or low-transpiring surface is not synchronized with the assumptions involved in the formulation of the combination equation. The Penman-Monteith (PM) combination equation (Figure 2.1) is: (es - ea ) (2.1) ∆(Rn -G) + ρa cp − −− − −− − ra λET =− −− −− −− −− −− −− − − −− −− −− −− − rs ∆ + γ (1+− −−) ra where λET is the latent heat flux representing the energy consumed by the ET process, Rn is the net radiation, G is the soil heat flux, (es − ea ) represents the vapour pressure deficit of the air, ρa is the mean air density at constant pressure, cp is the specific heat of the air, Δ represents the slope of the saturation vapour pressure-temperature relationship, γ is the psychrometric constant, rs and ra are the (bulk) surface and aerodynamic resistances respectively. The parameters of the equation are defined in Chapter 3. The Penman-Monteith combination equation (2.1) as formulated above includes all parameters that govern energy exchange and corresponding latent heat flux (evapotranspiration) from uniform expanses of transpiring vegetation. Most of the parameters are measured or can be readily calculated from weather data. This is due to the elimination of surface temperature and vapour pressure by combining the energy balance with the mass transfer method in the combination equation. The equation can be utilized for the direct calculation of any crop evapotranspiration as the surface and aerodynamic resistances are crop specific. 2.2.2 Aerodynamic resistance (ra ) The transfer of heat and water vapour from the evaporating surface into the air above the canopy is determined by the aerodynamic resistance: zm - d zh - d (2.2) ln − −− −− − ln − −− −− − zom zoh ra = − −− −− −− −− −− −− −− −− −− −− −− − − − k2 uz where ra aerodynamic resistance [s m-1 ], zm height of wind measurements [m], zh height of humidity measurements [m],25 2. FAO Penman-Monteith ETo equation d zero plane displacement height [m], zom roughness length governing momentum transfer [m], zoh roughness length governing the transfer of heat and vapour [m], k von Karman’s constant, 0.41 [-], uz wind speed at height z [m s-1 ]. The equation is restricted to neutral or nearly neutral stability conditions, i.e. where temperature, atmospheric pressure, and wind velocity distributions follow nearly adiabatic conditions (only small amounts of heat exchange) and where vertical transport due to buoyancy effects is small. The application of the equation for short time periods (hourly or less) may require the inclusion of corrections for stability. However, when predicting ETo from the defined well-watered reference surface, heat exchanged is small, and therefore stability correction is normally not required. Many studies have explored the nature of the wind regime in plant canopies. Zero displacement heights and roughness lengths have to be considered when the surface is covered by vegetation. The factors depend upon the crop height and architecture. Several empirical equations for the estimate of d, zom and zoh have been developed. The derivation of the aerodynamic resistance for the grass reference surface is presented in Box 2.1. The Leaf Area Index (LAI), a dimensionless quantity, is the leaf area (upper side only) per unit area of soil below it. It is expressed as m2 leaf area per m2 ground area. The active LAI is the index of the leaf area that actively contributes to surface heat and vapour transfer. It is generally the upper, sunlit portion of a dense canopy. The LAI values for various crops differ widely but values of 3 to 5 are common for many mature crops. For a given crop, green LAI changes throughout the season and normally reaches its maximum before or at flowering (Figure 2.2). LAI further depends on the plant density and the crop variety. There is, however, a lack of consolidated information on changes in rl over time for the different crops. The information available in the literature on stomatal conductance or resistance is often oriented toward physiological or ecophysiological studies. However, influences vary from one crop to another, and different varieties can be affected differently. The resistance increases when the crop is water stressed and the soil water availability limits crop evapotranspiration. Some studies indicate that stomatal resistance is influenced to some extent by radiation intensity, temperature, and vapour pressure deficit. 2.3 REFERENCE SURFACE To obviate the need to define unique evaporation parameters for each crop and stage of growth, the concept of a reference surface was introduced. Evapotranspiration rates of the various crops are related to the evapotranspiration rate from the reference surface (ETo ) by means of crop coefficients. In the past, an open water surface has been proposed as a reference surface. However, the differences in aerodynamic, vegetation control and radiation characteristics present a strong challenge in relating ET to measurements of free water evaporation. Relating ETo to a specific crop has the advantage of incorporating the biological and physical processes involved in ET from cropped surfaces. Grass, together with alfalfa, is a well-studied crop regarding its aerodynamic and surface characteristics and is accepted worldwide as a reference surface. Because the resistance to diffusion of vapour strongly depends on crop height, ground cover, LAI and soil moisture conditions, the characteristics of the reference crop should be well28 Crop evapotranspiration defined and fixed. Changes in crop height result in variations in the roughness and LAI. Consequently, the associated canopy and aerodynamic resistances will vary appreciably with time. Moreover, water stress and the degree of ground cover have an effect on the resistances and also on the albedo. To avoid problems of local calibration which would require demanding and expensive studies, a hypothetical grass reference has been selected with well-defined properties. Difficulties with a living grass reference result from the fact that the grass variety and morphology can significantly affect the evapotranspiration rate, especially during peak water use. Large differences may exist between warm-season and cool-season grass types. Cool-season grasses have a lower degree of stomatal control and hence higher rates of evapotranspiration. However, it may be difficult to grow cool-season grasses in some arid, tropical climates. The 1990 FAO Expert Consultation on Revision of FAO Methodologies for Crop Water Requirements accepted the following unambiguous definition for the reference surface when applied using 24-hour calculation timesteps (Figure 2.3): A hypothetical reference crop with an assumed crop height of 0.12 m, a fixed surface resistance of 70 s m-1 and an albedo of 0.23. The reference surface closely resembles an extensive surface of cool-season green grass of uniform height, actively growing, completely shading the ground and with adequate water. The requirements that the grass surface should be extensive and uniform result from the assumption that all fluxes are one-dimensional upwards and that the values for air temperature, vapour pressure and wind speed two to three meters above the surface are in equilibrium with the ETo rate of the surface. An ideal example of an extensive surface is an irrigated field of more than 4 ha in size. FAO Penman-Monteith ETo equation The FAO Penman-Monteith method is selected as the method by which the evapotranspiration of this reference surface (ETo ) can be unambiguously determined, and as the method which provides consistent ETo values in all regions and climates. 2.4 FAO PENMAN-MONTEITH EQUATION 2.4.1 Equation The consultation of experts and researchers was organized by FAO in May 1990, in collaboration with the International Commission for Irrigation and Drainage and with the World Meteorological Organization, to review the FAO methodologies on crop water requirements and to advise on the revision and update of procedures. The panel of experts recommended the adoption of the Penman-Monteith combination equation as a new standard for reference evapotranspiration and advised on procedures for the calculation of the various parameters. By defining the reference crop as a hypothetical crop with an assumed height of 0.12 m having a surface resistance of 70 s m-1 for 24-hour time steps and an albedo of 0.23, closely resembling the evaporation of an extensive surface of cool-season green grass of uniform height, actively growing and adequately watered, the FAO Penman-Monteith ETo equation was developed (Figure 2.3). Research and experience demonstrated that the method provides ETo values consistent with actual crop water use data worldwide. From the original Penman-Monteith combination equation (Equation 2.1) and the equations of the aerodynamic (Equation 2.2) and surface resistance (Equation 2.3), the FAO Penman-Monteith method to estimate ETo can be derived (Box 2.3) for 24-hour calculation time steps: 900 0.408 ∆ (Rn - G)+ γ ----------------- u2 (es - ea ) T + 273 ETo
