The average value for the atmospheric pressure is 81.8 kPa. The psychrometric constant, γ, is 0.054 kPa °C-1 . 3.2.4 Air temperature Agrometeorology is concerned with the air temperature near the level of the crop canopy. In traditional and modern automatic weather stations the air temperature is measured inside shelters (Stevenson screens, or ventilated, or naturally aspirated radiation shields) placed in accordance with World Meteorological Organization (WMO) standards at 2 m above the ground. The shelters are designed to protect the instruments from direct exposure to solar heating. The louvered construction promotes free air movement around the instruments. Air temperature is measured with thermometers, thermistors or thermocouples mounted in the shelter. Minimum and maximum thermometers record the minimum and maximum air temperature over a 24-hour period and use mercury and alcohol. Older measurement systems have used bimetallic thermographs as mechanical temperature recorders. These were easy to read and maintain. However, mechanical thermographs require verification and adjustment of the position of the pen recorder to plot the instantaneous temperature on paper over a day or week. These instruments were usually installed in shelters that were naturally ventilated. Modern temperature sensors are generally the thermistor or the thermocouple. These sensors provide accurate analogue measurements and are normally utilized in automated weather stations. Thermistors provide direct measurements of air (or soil) temperature, whereas thermocouples require an additional base temperature reading at67 3. Meteorological variables and parameters the datalogger terminal that is normally provided by a thermistor. Electronic weather stations often sample air temperature each second or minute and report hourly averages in addition to 24-hour maximum and minimum values. Due to the non-linearity of humidity data required in the FAO-PM ETo equation, the vapour pressure for a certain period should be computed as the average of the vapour pressure at the daily maximum and minimum air temperatures during that period. The daily maximum air temperature (Tmax ) and daily minimum air temperature (Tmin ) are, respectively, the maximum and minimum air temperature observed during the 24-hour period, beginning at midnight. Tmax and Tmin for longer periods such as weeks, 10 days or months are obtained by dividing the sum of the respective daily values by the number of days in the period. The mean daily air temperature (Tmean ) is only employed in the 24-hour FAO-PM ETo equation to calculate the slope of the saturation vapour pressure curve (Δ) and the impact of mean air density (ρa ) as the effect of temperature variations on the value of the climatic parameter is small in these cases. For standardization, the Tmean for 24-hour periods is defined as the average of the daily maximum (Tmax ) and minimum temperatures (Tmin ) rather than as the average of hourly temperature measurements. Tmax + Tmin (3.4) Tmean = − −− −−− −− −− − 2 The temperature is given in degrees Celsius (°C) or Fahrenheit (°F). The conversion table is given in Annex 1. In some calculation procedures, the temperature is required in Kelvin (K), which can be obtained by adding 273.15 to the temperature expressed in degrees Celsius (in practice K = °C + 273.15). The Kelvin and Celsius scales have the same scale interval. 3.2.5 Air humidity Concepts The water content of the air can be expressed in several ways. In agrometeorology, vapour pressure, dewpoint temperature and relative humidity are common expressions to indicate air humidity. Vapour pressure Water vapour is a gas, and its pressure contributes to the total atmospheric pressure. The amount of water in the air is related directly to the partial pressure exerted by the water vapour in the air and is, therefore, a direct measure of the air water content. When air is enclosed above an evaporating water surface, an equilibrium is reached between the water molecules escaping and returning to the water reservoir. At that moment, the air is said to be saturated since it cannot store any extra water molecules. The corresponding pressure is called the saturation vapour pressure (eo (T)). The number of water molecules that can be stored in the air depends on the temperature (T). The higher the air temperature, the higher the storage capacity, and the higher its saturation vapour pressure (Figure 3.2).68 Crop evapotranspiration FIGURE 3.2 Saturation vapour pressure shown as a function of temperature: eo (T) curve temperature (°C) 0 0 4 2 6 8 10

e °(T) saturation vapour pressure (kPa) Source: FAO56, 1998 As can be seen from Figure 3.2, the slope of the curve changes exponentially with temperature. At low temperatures, the slope is small and varies only slightly as the temperature rises. At high temperatures, the slope is large and small changes in T result in large changes in slope. The slope of the saturation vapour pressure curve, Δ, is an important parameter in describing vaporization and is required in the equations for calculating ETo from climatic data. The actual vapour pressure (ea ) is the vapour pressure exerted by the actual quantity of water in the air. When the air is not saturated, the actual vapour pressure will be lower than the saturation vapour pressure. The difference between the saturation and actual vapour pressure is called the vapour pressure deficit or saturation deficit and is an accurate indicator of the actual evaporative capacity of the air. In standard S.I. units, pressure is no longer expressed in centimetre of water, millimetre of mercury, bars, atmosphere, etc., but in pascals (Pa). Conversion factors between various units and Pa are given in Annex 1. As a pascal refers to a relatively small force (1 newton) applied on a relatively large surface (1 m2 ), multiples of the basic unit are often used. Vapour pressure is expressed in kilopascals (1 kPa = 1 000 Pa). Dewpoint temperature The dewpoint temperature is the temperature to which the air needs to be cooled to make the air saturated. The actual vapour pressure of the air is the saturation vapour pressure at the dewpoint temperature. The drier the air, the larger the difference between the air temperature and dewpoint temperature. Relative humidity The relative humidity (RH) expresses the degree of saturation of the air as a ratio of the actual (ea ) to the saturation (eo (T)) vapour pressure at the same temperature (T): ea

eo (T)69 3. Meteorological variables and parameters Relative humidity is the ratio between the amount of water the ambient air actually holds and the amount it could hold at the same temperature. It is dimensionless and is commonly given as a percentage. Although the actual vapour pressure might be relatively constant throughout the day, the relative humidity fluctuates between a maximum near sunrise and a minimum around early afternoon (Figure 3.3). The variation of the relative humidity is the result of the fact that the saturation vapour pressure is determined by the air temperature. As the temperature changes during the day, the relative humidity also changes substantially. FIGURE 3.3 Variation of the relative humidity over 24 hours for a constant actual vapour pressure of 2.4 kPa 20 25 30 35 40 60 80 100

eª =2.4 kPa 18 21 24 temperature (°C) t e m p e r a t u r e RH RH relative humidity (%) Source: FAO56, 1998 3.2.6 Measurement It is not possible to directly measure the actual vapour pressure. The vapour pressure is commonly derived from relative humidity or dewpoint temperature. Relative humidity is measured directly with hygrometers. Older mechanical systems in mechanically based field stations used hair hygrometers where the hair changes its length in response to changes in air humidity. These systems were normally operated as mechanical hygrographs. Measurements with hair hygrometers tend to lose accuracy with dust and aging of the hair. Modern electronic hygrometers use films from a dielectric polymer that changes its dielectric constant with changes in surface moisture, thus inducing a variation in the capacity of a condensator using that dielectric. These instruments are normally called dielectric polymer capacitive hygrometers. Accuracy can be higher than for hair hygrometers, the systems can be fully automated, and modern electronic sensors require little maintenance. Vapour pressure can be measured indirectly with psychrometers that measure the temperature difference between two thermometers, the so-called dry and wet bulb thermometers. The dry bulb thermometer measures the temperature of the air. The bulb of the wet bulb thermometer is covered with a wick that is kept constantly saturated with distilled water. Evaporation of water from the wick, requiring energy, lowers the temperature of the thermometer. The drier the air, the larger the evaporative cooling and the larger the temperature drop. The difference between the dry and wet70 Crop evapotranspiration bulb temperatures is called the wet bulb depression and is a measure of the air humidity. Usually, the dry and wet bulb thermometers are swung in the air by hand to produce sufficient air flow past the bulbs. The Assmann psychrometer has a forced ventilation of the wet bulb and dry bulb thermometers. When wet and dry bulb instruments are placed inside a naturally ventilated inside a shelter, problems can arise if air flow is not sufficient to maintain an appropriate evaporation rate and associated cooling. In more modern systems, the dry and wet bulb temperatures are measured by thermocouples or by thermistors, the so-called thermocouple psychrometers and thermo-sound psychrometers. These psychrometers are used in automatic weather stations and, when properly maintained and operated, tend to provide accurate measurements. The dewpoint temperature is measured with dewpoint meters. The underlying principle of some types of apparatus is the cooling of the ambient air until dew formation occurs. The corresponding temperature is the dewpoint temperature. Measurement systems can useamirrorlikemetallicsurfacethatisartificiallycooleduntilvapourcondensesonitand blocks the reading of reflected light. Other dew sensor systems use chemical or electric properties of certain materials that are altered when absorbing water vapour. Instruments for measuring dew point temperature require careful operation and maintenance and are seldom available in weather stations. The accuracy of estimation of the actual vapour pressure from Tdew is generally good under good maintenance protocol. Relative humidity and dewpoint temperature data can be notoriously plagued by measurement errors. Measurement error is common for both older hygro-thermograph types of instruments and older electronic instruments prior to about the year 2000. The more modern electronic instruments since about the year 2000 tend to have improved dependability, consistency, and accuracy. However, the data collected still must be reviewed and verified. Great care should be taken to assess the accuracy and integrity of RH and dewpoint data. The user is encouraged to always compare computed dewpoint temperatures to daily minimum air temperatures, as described near the end of Chapter 2. Frequently, it is better to utilize a dewpoint temperature that is estimated from daily minimum air temperature, rather than to use unreliable relative humidity measurements. The user is encouraged to utilize good judgement in this area. 3.2.7 Calculation procedures Mean saturation vapour pressure (es ) As saturation vapour pressure is related to air temperature, it can be calculated from the air temperature. When reported RH data are known to have been derived from measured or calculated vapour pressure or dewpoint temperature, then the same calculations that were originally used to derive the RH data should be followed to calculate ea . In other words, if RHmean had been derived from a daily average vapour pressure and daily average air temperature, then Equation 3.15 should be utilized. If RHmean data had been derived as an average of daily RHmax and RHmin , then Equation 3.16 should be utilized. Studies reported by the American Society of Civil Engineers in 2016 indicated that Equation 3.15 may produce more representative estimates of daily average ea than Equation 3.16. However, Equations 3.15 and 3.16 are usually less desirable than Equations 3.13 or 3.14. Estimating ea from RHmin and Tmax alone is discouraged because of tendencies for large uncertainty in measured RHmin that can be magnified by large values for eo (Tmax ). Estimating ea from daily average dewpoint temperature or hourly average dewpoint temperature is the most desirable approach. For hourly or shorter time periods, Equation 3.15 is valid, where T is the hourly average air temperature.75 3. The local intensity of radiation is, however, determined by the angle between the direction of the sun’s rays and the normal to the surface of the atmosphere. This angle will change during the day and will be different at different latitudes and during different seasons. The solar radiation received at the top of the earth’s atmosphere on a horizontal surface is called the extraterrestrial (solar) or exo-atmospheric radiation, Ra . If the sun is directly overhead, the angle of incidence is zero and the extraterrestrial radiation is 0.0820 MJ m-2 min-1 . As seasons change, the position of the sun, the length of the day and, hence, Ra change as well. Extraterrestrial radiation is thus a function of latitude, date and time of day. Daily values of Ra throughout the year for different latitudes are plotted in Figure 3.4. FIGURE 3.4 Annual variation in extraterrestrial radiation (Ra ) at the Equator, 20°and 40° north and south 0 Jan Feb Mar Apr May Jun Equator Jul Aug Sep Oct Nov Dec 10 40°N 20°N 20°S 40°S 20 30 40 50 R ª (MJ m -2 day -1 ) Source: FAO56, 199877 3. Meteorological variables and parameters Solar or shortwave radiation (Rs ) As the radiation penetrates the atmosphere, some of the radiation is scattered, reflected or absorbed by the atmospheric gases, clouds and dust. The amount of radiation reaching a horizontal plane is known as the solar radiation, Rs . Because the sun emits energy by means of electromagnetic waves characterized by short wavelengths, solar radiation is also referred to as shortwave radiation. For a cloudless day, Rs is roughly 75 percent of extra-terrestrial radiation due to about 25 percent of Ra absorbed or scattered in the atmosphere. On a cloudy day, additional radiation is scattered or absorbed by clouds, but even with extremely dense cloud cover, about 25 percent of the extraterrestrial radiation may still reach the earth’s surface mainly as diffuse sky radiation. Solar radiation is also known as global radiation, meaning that it is the sum of direct shortwave radiation from the sun and diffuse sky radiation from all upward angles. Relative shortwave radiation (Rs /Rso ) The relative shortwave radiation is the ratio of the solar radiation (Rs ) to the clear-sky solar radiation (Rso ). Rs is the solar radiation that actually reaches the earth’s surface in a given period, while Rso is the solar radiation that would reach the same surface during the same period but under cloudless conditions. The relative shortwave radiation is a way to express the cloudiness of the atmosphere; the cloudier the sky the smaller the ratio. The ratio varies between about 0.33 (dense cloud cover) and 1 (clear sky). In the absence of a direct measurement of Rn , the relative shortwave radiation is used in the computation of the net longwave radiation. Relative sunshine duration (n/N) The relative sunshine duration is another ratio that expresses the cloudiness of the atmosphere. It is the ratio of the actual duration of sunshine, n, to the maximum possible duration of sunshine or daylight hours N. In the absence of any clouds, the actual duration of sunshine is equal to the daylight hours (n = N) and the ratio is one, while on completely cloudy days n and, consequently, the ratio may be zero. In the absence of a direct measurement of Rs , the relative sunshine duration, n/N, can be used to derive solar radiation from extraterrestrial radiation. As with extraterrestrial radiation, the daylength N depends on the position of the sun and is hence a function of latitude and date. Daily values of N throughout the year for different latitudes are plotted in Figure 3.5. FIGURE 3.5 Annual variation of the daylight hours (N) at the Equator, 20° and 40° north and south 8 Jan Feb Mar Apr May Jun Equator Jul Aug Sep Oct Nov Dec 9 10 40°N 20°N 20°S 40°S 11 12 13 14 15 16 N daylight hours (hours/day) Source: FAO56, 199878 Crop evapotranspiration Albedo (α) and net solar radiation (Rns ) A considerable amount of solar radiation reaching the earth’s surface is reflected. The fraction, α, of the solar radiation reflected by the surface is known as the albedo. The albedo is highly variable for different surfaces and for the angle of incidence or slope of the ground surface. It may be as large as 0.95 for freshly fallen snow and as small as 0.05 for wet bare soil or open water. A green vegetation cover has an albedo of about 0.20-0.25. For the green grass reference crop, α is assumed to have a value of 0.23. The net solar radiation, Rns , is the fraction of the solar radiation Rs that is not reflected from the surface. Its value is (1 − α)Rs . Net longwave radiation (Rnl ) Thesolarradiationabsorbedbytheearthisconvertedtoheatenergy.Byseveralprocesses, including the emission of radiation, the earth loses this energy. The earth, which is at a much lower temperature than the sun, emits radiative energy with wavelengths longer than those from the sun. Therefore, the terrestrial radiation is referred to as longwave radiation. The emitted longwave radiation (Rl, up ) is absorbed by the atmosphere or is lost into space. As a consequence of the atmosphere being above absolute zero K, the atmosphere radiates energy of its own, as illustrated in Figure 3.6. Part of the radiation finds its way back to the earth’s surface. The longwave radiation received at the surface from the atmosphere (Rl, down ) helps to sustain surface temperature. Consequently, the earth’s surface both emits and receives longwave radiation. The difference between outgoing and incoming longwave radiation is called the net longwave radiation, Rnl . As the outgoing longwave radiation is almost always greater than the incoming longwave radiation, a positive Rnl represents an energy loss from the surface. Net radiation (Rn ) The net radiation, Rn , is the difference between incoming and outgoing radiation of both short and long wavelengths. It is the balance between the energy absorbed, reflected, and emitted by the earth’s surface or the difference between the incoming net shortwave (Rns ) and the net outgoing longwave (Rnl ) radiation (Figure 3.6). Rn is normally positive during the daytime and negative during the nighttime. The total daily value for Rn is almost always positive over a period of 24 hours, except in extreme conditions at high latitudes. FIGURE 3.6 Various components of radiation Earth R ns R s Ra R l, down R l, up Rs α R nl atmosphere clouds Source: FAO56, 199879 3. Meteorological variables and parameters Soil heat flux (G) In making estimates of evapotranspiration, all terms of the energy balance (Equation 1.1) should be considered. The soil heat flux, G, is the energy that is utilized in heating the soil. G is positive when the soil is warming and negative when the soil is cooling. Although the soil heat flux is small compared to Rn and may often be ignored, the amount of energy gained or lost by the soil in this process should theoretically be subtracted or added to Rn when estimating evapotranspiration. 3.3.2 Units The standard unit used in this guideline to express energy received on a unit surface per unit time is megajoules per square meter per day (MJ m-2 day-1 ) or megajoules per square meter per hour (MJ m-2 hr-1 ). For hourly or shorter time periods, radiation is expressed in (MJ m-2 hr-1 ) for use in the FAO-PM ETo equation. It may also be expressed as watts per square meter (W m-2 ). In some meteorological bulletins, other units might be used, or radiation might even be expressed in units no longer accepted as standard S.I. units, such as calories cm-2 day-1 . In the 24-hour FAO-PM ETo equation (Equation 2.4), radiation expressed in MJ m-2 day-1 is converted (Box 3.2) to equivalent evaporation in mm day-1 by using a conversion factor equal to the inverse of the latent heat of vaporization (1/λ = 0.408): equivalent evaporation [mm day-1 ] = 0.408 x Radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 ] (3.17a) or, for hourly or shorter time periods, equivalent evaporation [mm hr-1 ] = 0.408 x Radiation [MJ m-2 hr-1 ]. 1 mm day-1 2.45 245 58.5 28.4 1 3.3.3 Measurement Solar radiation can be measured with pyranometers, radiometers or solarimeters. The instruments contain a sensor installed on a horizontal surface that measures the intensity of the total solar radiation, i.e. both direct and diffuse radiation from clear sky and/or cloudy conditions. Solar radiation is most commonly measured with pyranometers. Pyranometers measure the shortwave incoming radiation over a solid angle in the shape of a hemisphere oriented upwards. In the most common “glassed dome” pyranometers, a thermopile is used within the instrument as the sensor, where thermal gradients are measured across hot and cold areas (black and white) or as conducted into the instrument base (solid black). The radiation intensity is proportional to the temperature differences between the sensing areas. Accuracy depends upon the sensitivity and age of the material used in the sensors, the response time and the distortion characteristics of the material constituting the dome covering the sensors. A second type of pyranometer that is less expensive and that is gaining acceptance is the silicon diode instrument where electric current is generated by a photo sensitive diode in proportion to solar intensity. Ordinarily, silicon diode pyranometers are not fully sensitive to the full spectrum of visible light, and the calibration of the instrument is only valid for upward-facing solar measurements. Whenapyranometerisorienteddownwardsitmeasuresthereflectedshortwaveradiation, and is thus called an albedometer. When two pyranometers are associated they must have full sensitivity to all spectra of visible light. This is important since together, with one orientedupwardsandtheotherdownwards,thenetshort-waveradiationcanbemeasured bysubtractingonereadingfromtheother.Theinstrumentisthencalledanetpyranometer. A point of caution is that any instrument used as an albedometer or net pyranometer composition of reflected light from vegetation is biased toward green. Therefore, most albedometers must be of the glass domed thermopile type and not the photo diode type. Where pyranometers are not available, solar radiation can be estimated from the duration ofbrightsunshine.Sunshineduration,n,ismostcommonlyrecordedwiththeCampbell Stokes heliograph. This instrument records periods of bright sunshine by using a glass globe that acts as a lens. The sun rays are concentrated at a focal point that burns a hole in a specially treated card mounted concentrically with the sphere. The movement of the sun changes the focal point throughout the day and a trace is burned into the card. If the sunisobscured,thetraceisinterrupted.Thehoursofbrightsunshineareindicatedbythe81 3. Meteorological variables and parameters lengths of the line segments. Care is required to avoid the accumulation of rainwater on the paper. The heliograph must be oriented South in the northern hemisphere and North in the southern hemisphere. In China, another type of heliograph is used, where the solar beam penetrates through an orifice and traces a recording onto paper treated with a sensible chemical substance. Electronic records of sunshine duration can be obtained using photo-electric or rotating optical fibre sunshine recorders. Sunshine recorders are much less used today as compared to less expensive and more readily automated pyranometers. For purposes of establishing Rs , pyranometers are much preferred. Net longwave and net shortwave radiation can be measured by recording the difference in output between sensors facing upward and downward, with both covering a solid angle in the shape of a hemisphere. In a net radiometer where a single output of Rn is produced, the glass domes are replaced by polyethylene domes that have a transmission range for both shortwave and longwave radiation. The sensors for net radiation are made from several thermocouples sensing heat generated by radiation from all wavelengths. All radiometers transform the radiation energy into thermal energy, a portion of which is transformed into an electric voltage gradient that provides appropriate conditions for continuous recording using dataloggers. Four component radiometers measure incoming shortwave, reflected shortwave, incoming longwave and emitted longwave radiation all separately. Four component radiometers are generally considered to be more accurate than integrated net radiometers.Theyhavetheadvantagethattheirfourcomponentmeasurementscaneach be quality assured separately. In addition, upward and downward facing components can be swapped periodically, for a few moments, by inverting the instrument. This provides a verification that both shortwave sensors have similar readings and that both longwave sensors have similar readings. The black bodies of radiometers can lose their sensing capabilities with time, so that these instruments require regular and frequent calibration. Other net radiometers have been comprised of ventilated differential thermopiles, but they are seldom utilized. The quantity of heat conducted into the soil, G, can be measured with systems of soil heat flux plates and thermocouples or thermistors. 3.3.4 Calculation procedures Extraterrestrial radiation for daily periods (Ra ) The extraterrestrial radiation, Ra , for each day of the year and for different latitudes can be estimated from the solar constant, the solar declination, and the time of the year by: 24 (60) Ra = -----------------Gsc dr [ωs sin (ϕ) sin (δ) + cos (ϕ) cos (δ) sin (ωs )] (3.18) π where Ra extraterrestrial radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 ], Gsc solar constant = 0.0820 MJ m-2 min-1 , dr inverse of the square of the relative distance Earth-Sun (Equation 3.20), ωs sunset hour angle (Equation 3.22 or 3.23) [rad], ϕ latitude (Equation 3.19) [rad], δ solar declination (Equation 3.21) [rad].82 Crop evapotranspiration Ra is expressed in the above equation in MJ m-2 day-1 . Meteorological variables and parameters Clear-sky solar radiation (Rso ) The calculation of the clear-sky radiation, Rso , when n = N, is required for computing net longwave radiation. • For near sea level or when calibrated values for as and bs are available: Rso = (as + bs )Ra (3.33) where Rso clear-sky solar radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 ], as +bs fraction of extraterrestrial radiation reaching the earth on clear-sky days (n = N). • When calibrated values for as and bs are not available an approximate estimate that is based only upon elevation representing average air mass is: Rso = (0.75+ 2 × 10-5 z)Ra (3.34) where z is station elevation above sea level [m]. A more accurate estimate for Rso is obtained by considering the sun’s angle through the atmosphere and the amount of water vapor, which is variable from day to day and is an effective absorber of solar radiation. The angle of the sun, which changes during the day, defines the relative length of the atmosphere that radiation must pass through and undergo absorption and scattering. In this procedure, the estimate is separated into the components of beam and diffuse radiation: Rso = (KB + KD ) Ra (3.35) where KB the clearness index for direct beam radiation [-], KD the corresponding index for diffuse radiation [-]. Equation 3.36 for KB uses improved coefficients and considers the effects of air pressure, sun angle and precipitable water: -0.00146 P (W ) 0.4 KB

Kt sin � sin � where Kt a clearness coefficient that is affected by turbidity and haze (0 < Kt ≤ 1.0), P atmospheric pressure [kPa], β angle of the sun above the horizon [rad], W precipitable water in the atmosphere [mm]. The ratio P / Kt sinβ represents the effective amount of air mass that the solar beam passes through. Kt = 1.0 for clean air and Kt ≈ 0.5 for turbid, dusty, or polluted air. The value for Kt may vary with the time of year and with the cleansing of the atmosphere by precipitation. General values for Kt for a region can be determined using a pristine pyranometer that has a calibration traceable to the national or international solar standard. In general, for routine estimation of Rn and Rso envelopes using Kt = 1.0 is recommended, especially for agricultural areas where the air is likely to be free of dust. An exception might be during the pre-monsoon season on the Indian subcontinent when substantial haze can exist. Aerosols and dust can substantially increase the haziness of the atmosphere, especially when coupled with high humidity levels.88 Crop evapotranspiration Haze reduces the beam component of solar radiation but may increase the diffuse component through increased scattering of radiation. Under hazy conditions, a value Kt = 0.5 essentially doubles the effective thickness of the atmosphere, and a value Kt = 0.25 quadruples the effective thickness. Precipitable water is estimated as: W= 0.14 ea P + 2.1 (3.37) where W precipitable water in the atmosphere [mm], ea actualvaporpressureoftheair(ataheightofapproximately2m[kPa], P mean atmospheric pressure [kPa]. The diffuse radiation index KD is estimated from KB : KD = 0.35 - 0.36 KB for KB ≥0.15 (3.38a) KD = 0.18 + 0.82 KB for KB <0.15 (3.38b) The diffusive component increases as the direct beam component decreases when KB > 0.15 due to the increased amount of scatter of the direct beam component. For clear sky conditions, KB is always > 0.15 for daily data and, nearly always, is > 0.15 for hourly periods. For daily (24-hour) time periods, the mean daily sun angle, weighted according to Ra , can be estimated as: (2π ) sin �24

(3.39) 365 where �24 theaverageβduringthedaylightperiod,weightedaccordingtoRa [rad], φ latitude [rad], J day of the year [1-366]. The sin (�24 ) variable is used in place of sin(β) in Equation 3.36 for 24-hour time steps and represents the average sun angle during daylight hours. For hourly or shorter periods, the sun angle β is calculated as: sin � = sin φsin δ + cos φcos δ cos ω (3.40) where φ latitude [rad], δ solar declination [rad], ω solar time angle at the midpoint of the hourly or shorter period [rad]. Equations from 3.35 to 3.40 produce quite accurate estimates for Rso . However, reasons may exist for measured Rs on cloud-free days to deviate from the theoretical Rso curve. These reasons include air turbidity and haziness caused by dust and aerosols that are not reflected by the equations, effects of nearly invisible clouds high overhead, and late afternoon clouding. The inclusion of precipitable water in Equation 3.36 instructs89 3. Meteorological variables and parameters the Rso estimate to increase on dry days due to less water vapor to absorb shortwave radiation. Reflection of solar radiation by adjacent clouds can occasionally increase measured hourly Rs to levels that are above Rso . The relationship Rso = (KB + KD ) Ra is useful for verifying correct operation of pyranometers as described in Chapter 2. Net solar or net shortwave radiation (Rns ) The net shortwave radiation resulting from the balance between incoming and reflected solar radiation is given by: Rns = (1 - α) Rs (3.41) where Rns net solar or shortwave radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 ], α albedoorcanopyreflectioncoefficient,whichis0.23forthehypothetical grass reference crop [dimensionless], Rs the incoming solar radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 ]. Rns is expressed in the above equation in MJ m-2 day-1 for 24-h time steps. Net longwave radiation (Rnl ) The net longwave radiation is defined as the difference between longwave radiation emitted and reflected from the surface and the longwave radiation emitted to the surface from the atmosphere. The rate of longwave energy emission is proportional to the absolute temperature of the surface raised to the fourth power. This relation is expressed quantitatively by the Stefan-Boltzmann law. The net energy flux is less than that emitted from the earth’s surface due to the absorption and downward radiation from the sky. Water vapour, clouds, carbon dioxide and dust are absorbers and emitters of longwave radiation. Their concentrations should be known when assessing the net outgoing flux. In general, the longwave radiation emitted from the surface is greater than the longwave radiation emitted to the surface from the sky. This is because, under sunlit conditions, the surface tends to be warmer than the effective atmospheric temperature. As humidity and cloudiness play an important role, the Stefan-Boltzmann law is corrected by these two factors when estimating the net outgoing flux of longwave radiation. It is assumed that the concentrations of the other absorbers are constant resulting: Tmax, K 4 + Tmin, K 4 (0.34 - 0.14 √ ea ) Rs Rnl

2 Rso where Rnl net outgoing longwave radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 (or hour-1 )], σ Stefan-Boltzmann constant [ 4.903×10-9 MJ K-4 m-2 day-1 for 24-hour timesteps, or 2.041×10-10 MJ K-4 m-2 hour-1 for hourly timesteps], Tmax, K maximum absolute temperature [K = °C + 273.15], Tmin, K minimum absolute temperature [K = °C + 273.15], ea actual vapour pressure [kPa], Rs /Rso relative shortwave radiation (limited to ≤ 1.0), Rs measured or calculated solar radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 (or hour-1 )], Rso calculated(Equation3.35)clear-skyradiation[MJm-2 day-1 (orhour-1 )].90 Crop evapotranspiration An average of the maximum air temperature to the fourth power and the minimum air temperature to the fourth power is commonly used in the Stefan-Boltzmann equation for 24-hour time steps (Example 3.10). It should be noted that: • The term (0.34−0.14√ ea ) expresses the adjustment for air humidity impacts on atmospheric emission of longwave radiation, and Rnl will be smaller if the humidity increases due to more downwelling longwave radiation. • The effect of cloudiness is expressed by (1.35 Rs /Rso −0.35). The term becomes smaller if the cloudiness increases and hence Rs decreases. Because cloud temperatures tend to be warmer than the clear atmosphere, as cloudiness increases, the magnitude of downwelling longwave radiation increases and Rnl becomes smaller. • The smaller the adjustment terms, the larger the flux of longwave radiation emitted to the surface from the atmosphere and accordingly, the smaller the net outgoing flux of longwave radiation. It should be noted that the Rs /Rso term in Equation 3.42 must be limited so that Rs /Rso ≤ 1.0. Where measurements of incoming and outgoing short and longwave radiation during bright sunny and overcast hours are available, calibration of the coefficients in Equation 3.42 can be carried out. Annex 2 (Table A2.8) lists values for σTK 4 for different air temperatures. Because soil heat flux over 24-hour time periods is small compared to Rn , particularly when the surface is covered by vegetation such as with the grass reference, a simple calculation procedure is presented here for calculation time steps 24 hours or longer, based on the idea that the soil temperature follows air temperature: Ti - Ti -1 G = cs

Δt where G soil heat flux [MJ m-2 day-1 ], cs soil heat capacity [MJ m-3 °C-1 ], Ti air temperature at time i [°C], Ti-1 air temperature at time i-1 [°C], Δt length of time interval [day], Δz effective soil depth that is warmed or cooled [m]. As the soil temperature lags air temperature, the average temperature for a period should be considered when assessing the daily soil heat flux, i.e. Δt should exceed one day. The depth of penetration of the temperature wave is determined by the length of the time interval. The effective soil depth, Δz, is only 0.10-0.20 m for a time interval of one or a few days but might be 2 m or more for monthly periods. The soil heat capacity is related to its mineral composition and water content. • For day and ten-day periods: As the magnitude of the day or ten-day soil heat flux beneath the grass reference surface is relatively small, it may be ignored and thus: Gday ≈ 0 (3.45) • For monthly periods: When assuming a constant soil heat capacity of 2.1 MJ m-3 °C-1 and an appropriate soil depth, Equation 3.44 can be used to derive G for monthly periods (Example 3.12): Gmonth,i = 0.7 (Tmonth, i+1 - Tmonth, i-1 ) (3.46)93 3. Meteorological variables and parameters or, if Tmonth, i+1 is unknown: Gmonth, i = 0.14 (Tmonth, i - Tmonth, i-1 ) (3.47) where Tmonth, i mean air temperature of month i [°C], Tmonth, i-1 mean air temperature of previous month [°C], Tmonth, i+1 mean air temperature of next month [°C]. • For hourly or shorter periods: For hourly (or shorter) calculations, G beneath a dense cover of grass does not correlate well with air temperature. Hourly G can be approximated during daylight periods as: Ghr = 0.1 Rn (3.48) and during nighttime periods as: Ghr = 0.5 Rn (3.49) Where the soil is warming, the soil heat flux G is positive. The amount of energy required for this process is subtracted from Rn when estimating evapotranspiration. Wind direction refers to the direction from which the wind is blowing. For the computation of evapotranspiration, wind speed is the relevant variable. As wind speed at a given location varies with time, it is necessary to express it as an average over a given time interval. Wind speed is given in meters per second (m s-1 ) or kilometres per day (km day-1 ). For the standardized calculation of evapotranspiration with the PM equation, wind speed measured at 2 m or transformed to an equivalent wind speed at 2 m above the surface is required. Wind speed is measured with anemometers. The anemometers commonly used in weather stations are composed of cups or propellers that are turned by the force of the wind. By counting the number of revolutions over a given time period, the average94 Crop evapotranspiration wind speed over the measuring period is computed. In some modern weather stations, sonic anemometers are used. These have the advantage of having no moving parts or bearings that require maintenance. Measurementsbycupandpropelleranemometersarereliableprovidedthatmaintenance ensures appropriate functioning of the mechanical parts. Older designs of anemometers utilize mechanical counters as the output device. Modern anemometers may be equipped with generators giving a voltage signal that is proportional to the wind speed. Other anemometers may be equipped with small magnetic reed switches or with opto electronic couplers that generate electric impulses in proportion to the wind speed. The electronic devices are utilized in automatic weather stations. Anemometers that collect wind speed data to be used to calculate ETo are always placed at a height not less than 2 m above the ground, following recommendations by WMO. The accuracy of wind speed measurements depends on the upwind fetch as much as on instrumentation. A large upwind fetch that is free of buildings and trees is definitely required for representative measurements. In many agrometeorological weather stations, it may be preferred to place the anemometer at 3 to 4 m above the ground surface so that the anemometer has less opportunity to be obstructed or influenced by tall crops, such as maize, growing in the vicinity. The wind speed measurement is then adjusted, numerically, to an equivalent wind speed at 2 m (Equation 3.50). 3.4.2 Wind profile relationship Surface friction tends to slow down wind passing over it. Therefore, wind speed is slowest at the surface and increases with height. For this reason, anemometers are placed at a chosen standard height, i.e. 10 m in meteorology and 2, 3 or 4 m in agrometeorology. To adjust wind speed data obtained from instruments placed at elevations other than the standard 2 m height to the 2 m height, a logarithmic wind speed profile may be used for measurements above a short grassed surface (Example 3.13): 4.87 u2 = uZ

ln (67.8 z - 5.42) where u2 wind speed at 2 m above ground surface [m s-1 ], uz measured wind speed at z m above ground surface [m s-1 ], z height of measurement above ground surface [m]. The corresponding multipliers or conversion factors are given in Annex 2 (Table A2.9) and are plotted in Figure 3.7. Equation 3.50 assumes a neutral or nearly neutral boundary layer of air for some distance above the surface. This condition is common for well-watered grassed surfaces where ET consumes most of the energy available so that sensible heat flux, H, and thus the temperature gradient between surface and air, and thus buoyancy effects are small.95 3. 3.5 CLIMATIC DATA ACQUISITION Meteorologicaldataarerecordedatvarioustypesofweatherstations.Agrometeorological stations are intended to be sited in cropped areas where instruments are exposed to atmospheric conditions similar to those for the surrounding crops. In these stations, air temperature and humidity, wind speed and solar radiation or sunshine duration are typically measured at 2 m above an extensive surface of grass or short crop. Where neededandfeasible,thecoverofthestationisirrigated.Guidelinesfortheestablishment and maintenance of agrometeorological stations are given in the FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 27. The World Meteorological Organization (WMO) has published, since 1950, the Guide to Instruments and Methods of Observation (WMO No. 8). The 2021 edition includes several volumes: I: Measurement of Meteorological Variables, 2021 edition; II: Measurement of Cryospheric Variables, 2018 edition; III: Observing Systems, 2021 edition; IV: Space-based Observations, 2018 edition; V: Quality Assurance and Management of Observing Systems, 2018 edition.96 Crop evapotranspiration Data collected at stations other than agrometeorological stations require a careful analysis of their validity before their use. For example, in aeronautic stations, data relevant to aviation are measured. As airports are often situated near urban conditions and away from irrigated areas, temperatures are often higher than those found in rural agricultural areas as explained in Chapter 2. Wind speed is commonly measured at 10 m height above the ground surface and needs to be adjusted to the standard 2 m height for the ETo estimate (Figure 3.7). The country’s national meteorological services should be contacted for information on the climatic data collected at various types of weather stations in the country. National services commonly publish meteorological bulletins listing processed climatic data from the various stations. As described in Chapter 2, gridded weather data sets are becoming commonly available. It is essential that those data be assessed for quality and representativeness for use in estimating ETo . 3.6 ESTIMATING MISSING CLIMATIC DATA The assessment of the reference evapotranspiration with the FAO-PM ETo method is developed in Chapter 4. The calculation of ETo with the FAO-PM ETo equation requires hourly, daily, ten-day, or monthly maximum and minimum air temperature (Tmax and Tmin ), actual vapour pressure (ea ), net radiation (Rn calculated from Rs ) and wind speed standardized to 2 m (u2 ). If some of the required weather data are missing or cannot be calculated, it is strongly recommended that the user estimate the missing climatic data with one of the following procedures and continue to use the FAO-PM ETo equation. The use of an alternative ETo calculation procedure, requiring only limited meteorological parameters, is less recommended. Procedures to estimate missing humidity, solar radiation and wind speed data are given in this section. 3.6.1 Estimating missing humidity data Where humidity data are lacking or are of questionable quality, an estimate of actual vapour pressure, ea, can be obtained by assuming that the dewpoint temperature (Tdew ) is near the daily minimum temperature (Tmin ). This statement implicitly assumes that at sunrise, when the air temperature is close to Tmin , that the near-surface air over a well-watered, transpiring surface is nearly saturated with water vapour and the relative humidity is nearly 100 percent. Reasons for this are described in Chapter 2 and procedures to estimate Tdew are presented in Chapter 2. 3.6.2 Estimating missing radiation data Net radiation measuring devices, requiring professional control, have rarely been installedinagrometeorologicalstations.Intheabsenceofdirectmeasurement,longwave and net radiation can be derived from more commonly observed weather parameters, i.e., solar radiation or sunshine hours, air temperature and vapour pressure. Where solar radiation is not measured, it can be estimated from measured hours of bright sunshine. However, where daily sunshine hours (n) are not available, solar radiation data cannot be computed with the calculation procedures previously presented. This section presents additional alternative methods to estimate solar radiation data. Solar radiation data from a nearby weather station This method relies on the fact that for the same month and often for the same day, the variables affecting incoming solar radiation, Rs , and sunshine duration, n, are similar throughout a given region. This implies or requires that: (i) the size of the region is small; (ii) the air masses governing rainfall and cloudiness are nearly identical within97 3. Meteorological variables and parameters parts of the region; and (iii) the physiography of the region is almost homogenous. Differences in relief should be negligible as they strongly influence the movement of air masses. Under such conditions, radiation data observed at nearby stations can be used. Caution should be used when applying this method to mountainous and coastal areas where differences in exposure and altitude could be important or where cloudiness and rainfall are variable due to convective conditions. Moreover, data from a station located nearby but situated on the other side of a mountain may not be transferable as conditions governing cloud formation and radiation may be different. The user should observe climatic conditions in both locations and obtain information from local persons concerning general differences in cloud cover and type. Where the north-south distance to a weather station within the same homogeneous region exceeds 50 km so that the value for Ra changes, the Rs measurement should be adjusted using the ratio of the solar to extraterrestrial radiation, Rs /Ra : Rs,reg RS = − − − −− −Ra (3.51) Ra,reg where Rs,reg solar radiation at the regional location [MJ m-2 day-1 ], Ra,reg extraterrestrial radiation at the regional location [MJ m-2 day-1 ]. Once the solar radiation has been derived from the radiation data of a nearby station, the net longwave radiation (Equation 3.42) and the net radiation (Equation 3.43) can be calculated. The estimation method of Equation 3.51 is recommended for monthly calculations of ETo . If using the method for daily estimates of ETo , a more careful analysis of weather data in the importing and exporting meteorological stations has to be performed to verify whether both stations are in the same homogeneous climatic region and are close enough to experience similar conditions within the same day. The analysis should include the comparison of daily weather data from both stations, particularly the maximum and minimum air temperature and humidity. In fact, similar cloudiness and sunshine durations are related to similarities in temperature and humidity trends. Related statistical procedures are described in Annex 3. Generally, daily calculations of ETo with estimated radiation data are justified when utilized as a sum or an average over a several-day period. This is the case for the computation of the mean evapotranspiration demand between successive irrigations or when planning irrigation schedules. Under these conditions, the relative error for one day often counterbalances the error for another day of the averaging period. Daily estimates should not be utilized as true daily estimates but only in averages over the period under consideration. Solar radiation data derived from air temperature difference Thedifferencebetweenthemaximumandminimumairtemperatureisrelatedtothedegree of cloud cover in a location. Clear-sky conditions result in high temperatures during the day (Tmax ), because the atmosphere is transparent to the incoming solar radiation, and in low temperatures during the night (Tmin ), because less downward longwave radiation is emitted by the atmosphere. On the other hand, in overcast conditions, Tmax is relatively smaller because a significant part of the incoming solar radiation never reaches the earth’s surface and is absorbed and reflected by the clouds. Similarly, Tmin will be relatively higher because the cloud cover acts as a blanket and decreases the net outgoing longwave radiation. Therefore, the difference between the maximum and minimum air temperature (Tmax − Tmin ) can be used as an indicator of the fraction of extraterrestrial radiation that reaches the earth’s surface. This principle has been utilized by Hargreaves and Samani to develop estimates of ETo using only air temperature data.98 Crop evapotranspiration The Hargreaves-Samani radiation formula, adjusted and validated at several weather stations in a variety of climate conditions, becomes: Rs = kRs√(Tmax - Tmin ) Ra (3.52) where Ra extraterrestrial radiation [MJ m-2 d-1 ], Tmax maximum air temperature [°C], Tmin minimum air temperature [°C], kRs empirical adjustment coefficient [°C-0.5 ]. The fraction of extraterrestrial radiation that reaches the earth’s surface, Rs /Ra , ranges from about 0.25 on a day with dense cloud cover to about 0.75 to 0.80 on a cloudless day with a clear sky, depending on elevation. Rs estimated by Equation 3.52 should be limited to ≤ Rso from Equation 3.34 or 3.35. The square root of the temperature difference is closely related to the existing daily solar radiation in a given location. The adjustment coefficient kRs was originally recommended by Hargreaves and Samani as 0.16 for land locations, not influenced by large water bodies, and 0.19 for “coastal” locations. However, kRs is empirical and relates to the atmospheric conditions that influence the availability of incoming solar radiation at crop and natural surfaces, and so with the availability of energy for evaporation. Thus, kRs has been shown to vary spatially with site elevation and the distance to large water bodies. In addition, its value may increase with the aridity of the site and with wind speed. A useful approach is to estimate kRs values using various long-term (ideally > 30-years) average weather variables that allow for characterizing the site location: the difference between the maximum and minimum air temperature (TD = Tmax −Tmin ), RH and u2 . In practice, the site averages are computed from available local observations or from a nearby weather station, where climates should be similar, when such data are not available locally (Example 3.14). For all types of climates, kRs may be estimated from a global multiple linear regression as follows: kRs

+ 0.0194 u2 avg - 0.0017 RHavg (3.53) where kRs empirical radiation adjustment coefficient [°C-0.5 ], TDavg long-term average of the seasonal difference between Tmax and Tmin (TD = Tmax - Tmin ) [ºC], u2 avg long-term seasonal average (or seasonal estimate) of daily wind speed at 2 m height [m s-2 ], RHavg long-term seasonal average (or seasonal estimate) of daily relative humidity [%]. Alternatively, the kRs estimation can be refined according to the local climate group as defined by the UNEP aridity index (AI) characteristic of the region, defined in Box 2.4 in Chapter 2. This is done using a range of multiple linear regression equations. In this approach, kRs becomes: • For Hyper-arid and Arid locations (AI < 0.20) kRs

Nevertheless, the regression coefficients are close to 1.0, and errors are much smaller than the daily means of the observed values. The quality of predictions depends upon the data ingested, which may lead to future improvements when more data is available for the reanalysis. When averaged over longer periods, error in estimates and occurrence of outlying values should decrease. 3.6.3 Missing wind speed data Wind speed data from a nearby weather station Importing wind speed data from a nearby station, as for radiation data, relies on the fact that the air flow above a “homogeneous” region may have relatively large variations through the course of a day but small variations when referring to longer periods or the total for the day. Data from a nearby station may be imported where air masses are of the same origin or where the same fronts govern air flows in the region and where the relief is similar. When importing wind speed data from another station, the regional climate, trends in the variation of other meteorological parameters and relief should be comparable. Strong winds are often associated with low relative humidity and light winds are common with high relative humidity. Thus, trends in variation of daily maximum and minimum relative humidities should be similar in both locations. In mountainous areas, data should not necessarily be imported from the nearest station but from nearby stations with similar elevation and exposure to the dominant winds. The paired stations may even vary from one season to another, depending on the dominant winds. Imported wind speed data can be used when making monthly estimates of evapotranspiration. Daily calculations are justified when utilized as a sum or average over a several-day period, such as a week or decade. Empirical estimates of monthly wind speed As the variation in wind speed averaged over monthly periods is relatively small and fluctuates around average values, monthly values of wind speed may be estimated. The “average” wind speed estimates may be selected from information available for the regional climate but should take seasonal changes into account. General values are suggested in Table 3.2. TABLE 3.2 General classes of monthly wind speed data Description Mean monthly wind speed at 2 m light wind light to moderate wind moderate to strong wind strong wind ... ≤ 1.0 m s-1 1 - 3 m s-1 3 - 5 m s-1 ... ≥ 5.0 m s-1 Where no wind data are available within the region, a value of 2 m s-1 can be used as a temporary estimate. This value is the average over 2 000 weather stations around the globe. Figure 3.11 shows examples of using the regional average u2 and the world standard average (i.e. default value of u2 = 2 m s-1 ) to estimate ETo , one for a strong wind location and the other for a light-moderate wind location. The large underestimation105 3. Meteorological variables and parameters of ETo when using the default average value is due to overall higher u2 values observed at the location (u2 avg = 5.3 m s-1 ). The results are different at locations characterized by light-moderate wind speeds with an overestimation tendency when the default u2 value is used in ETo estimation. In general, wind speed at 2 m, u2 , should be limited to about u2 ≥ 0.5 m s-1 when used in the FAO-PM-ETo equation. This is necessary to account for the effects of boundary layer instability and buoyancy of air in promoting exchange of vapour at the surface when air is calm. This effect occurs when the wind speed is small, and buoyancy of warm air induces air exchange at the surface; u2 ≥ 0.5 m s-1 in the ETo equation improves the estimation accuracy. Many of the suggested procedures rely upon maximum and minimum air temperature measurements. Unfortunately, there is no dependable way to estimate air temperature when it is missing. Therefore, it is suggested that maximum and minimum daily air temperature data are the minimum data requirements necessary to apply the FAO-PM equation. Figure 3.12 presents an example of the successful application of PMT, when using Equation 3.52 for estimating Rs and Equations 3.53 and 3.54a for estimating kRs and using the default u2 value of 2 m/s. As expected, using the climate focused multiple linear regression equation for kRs , the regression coefficient for ETo is closer to 1.0 relative to the global regression Equation 3.53, and RMSE is also smaller. This condition has been observed in most of the analyzed applications. Meteorological variables and parameters As recommended in the section above, the FAO Penman-Monteith Temperature method, PMT, is recommended over other empirical ETo methods, even when daily maximum and minimum air temperature data are the only data available. This is because the PM equation contains appropriate and physically based relationships among weather variables and because the missing weather variables can be relatively accurately estimated using informed procedures described in the previous section and in Chapter 2. Therefore, the PM equation is likely to produce ETo estimates that are sensitive to the expected behaviours of the missing weather variables. This is in contrast to a stand-alone temperature-based method such as the Hargreaves-Samani method that has no means to change its behaviour under different general weather conditions such as high wind locations vs. low wind locations, or humid vs. arid locations. With this in mind, however, the Hargreaves-Samani ETo equation is provided in the following section as a backup procedure. 3.6.5 An alternative equation for computing ETo when key weather data are missing: the Hargreaves-Samani equation When solar radiation data, relative humidity data and/or wind speed data are missing, they should be estimated using the procedures presented in the previous section. As an alternative, ETo can be estimated using the Hargreaves-Samani (HS) ETo equation where: ETo HS = 0.0135 kRs HS Ra (Tmax -Tmin )0.5 (Tmean + 17.8) (3.55) λ where Tmax maximumofdailyairtemperaturerelativetothetimestepcalculation[o C], Tmin minimumofdailyairtemperaturerelativetothetimestepcalculation[o C], Tmean mean air of daily temperature relative to the time step calculation [o C], λ latent heat of vaporization [2.45 MJ kg−1 ], kRs HS the radiation adjustment coefficient for the Hargreaves-Samani equation [°C-0.5 ], Ra the extraterrestrial radiation [MJ m-2 day-1 ], 0.0135 factorforconversionofunitsfromtheAmericantotheInternationalsystem, 17.8 an empirical parameter. The default kRs HS value is 0.17 in the original formulation. As per Equations 3.53 and 3.54, the empirical kRs HS adjustment coefficient may be estimated using a multiple linear regression equation using the long-term average weather variables (TD=Tmax −Tmin , RH and u2 ) that allow characterization of the site location. Thus, the general equation is: kRs HS

+ 0.0151 u2 avg - 0.0017 RHavg (3.56) where parameters are defined as for Equation 3.53. If necessary, Equation 3.56 can be calibrated on a monthly or annual basis by determining the empirical coefficients of the regression equation: ETo = a + b ETo HS Eq.3.55 (3.58) where the “Eq. 3.55” subscript refers to ETo estimated using Equation 3.55 Figure 3.13 presents an example of the application of Equation 3.55 for estimating ETo and using Equation 3.56 for the kRs HS estimation (Example 3.16). In the same example the ETo estimation with the PMT approach is also presented for the same locations. Results for the applications to a humid and a semi-arid location are good since the regression coefficients are quite close to 1.0 indicating that predictions have a small bias of estimation, and estimation errors RMSE are much smaller than the average values of ETo . These goodness-of-fit indicators, also observed for many other applications, suggest that the approach is feasible. However, results for the PMT method are slightly better than those for the HS equation. In general, estimating solar radiation, vapor pressure and wind speed as described in Equations 3.52 and 2.6 or 2.7, and Table 3.2 and then utilizing these estimates in Equation 2.4 (the FAO-PM ETo equation) should provide somewhat more accurate estimates as compared to estimating ETo directly using Equation 3.55 This is due to the ability of the estimation equations to incorporate general climatic characteristics such as high or low wind speed or high or low relative humidity into the ETo estimate made using Equation 2.4.109 3. In July, the mean monthly maximum and minimum air temperatures are 26.6 and 14.8 °C respectively. Using the long term FAO-CLIMWAT weather database it was possible to estimate for that location the (Tmax -Tmin )avg = 9.34 ºC, u2 avg = 2.14 m s-1 and RHavg = 79%. The Aridity index = 1.17 thus, because AI≥1.0, that location behaves as a humid climate type. Equation 3.55 using the default kRs HS value tends to underpredict ETo under high wind conditions (u2 > 3 m s-1 ) and to overpredict under conditions of high relative humidity.113 4. Determination of ETo Determination of ETo This chapter demonstrates how crop reference evapotranspiration (ETo ) is determined either from meteorological data or from pan evaporation. The FAO Penman-Monteith method is maintained as the sole standard method for the computation of ETo from meteorological data. The method itself is introduced in Chapter 2, and the computation of all data required for the calculation of ETo is described and discussed in Chapter 3. This chapter presents guidelines to calculate ETo with different time steps, ranging from hours to months, and with missing climatic data. The ETo calculation can be done by hand with the help of a calculation sheet, or by means of a computer. The use of computers is highly recommended. ETo can also be approximated from the evaporation loss from a water surface. The procedure to obtain ETo from pan evaporation and the coefficients for different types of pans are presented in this chapter. 4.1 FAO PENMAN-MONTEITH ETO EQUATION From the original Penman-Monteith equation and the equations of the aerodynamic and canopy resistance, the FAO Penman-Monteith equation has been derived in Chapter 2. For 24-hour calculation time steps: 900 0.408 Δ (Rn - G)+ γ − −−− − −−u2 (es - ea ) T + 273 (2.4 bis) ETo =− −− −− −− −−− −−−− −− −− − − − − −− −− −−−− −− −− −− − Δ + γ (1 + 0.34 u2 ) where ETo reference evapotranspiration [mm day-1 ], Rn net radiation at the crop surface [MJ m-2 day-1 ], G soil heat flux density [MJ m-2 day-1 ], T air temperature at 2 m height [°C], u2 wind speed at 2 m height [m s-1 ], es saturation vapour pressure [kPa], ea actual vapour pressure [kPa], es −ea saturation vapour pressure deficit [kPa], Δ slope vapour pressure curve [kPa °C-1 ], γ psychrometric constant [kPa °C-1 ]. The FAO Penman-Monteith ETo (FAO-PM ETo ) equation determines the evapotranspiration from the hypothetical grass reference surface and provides a standard to which evapotranspiration in different periods of the year or in other regions can be compared and to which the evapotranspiration from other crops can be related. Chapter 4114 Crop evapotranspiration 4.1.1 Calculation procedure Calculation sheet ETo can be estimated for daily (24-hour) calculation time steps by means of the calculation sheet presented in Box 4.1. The calculation sheet refers to tables in Annex 2 for the determination of some of the climatic parameters. The calculation procedure consists of the following steps: 1. Derivation of some climatic parameters from the daily maximum (Tmax ) and minimum (Tmin ) air temperature, altitude (z) and mean wind speed (u2 ). 2. Calculation of the vapour pressure deficit (es − ea ). The saturation vapour pressure (es ) is derived from Tmax and Tmin , while the actual vapour pressure (ea ) can be derived from the dewpoint temperature (Tdew ), from maximum (RHmax ) and minimum (RHmin ) relative humidity, or from the maximum (RHmax ), or from mean relative humidity (RHmean ). 3. Determination of the net radiation (Rn ) as the difference between the net shortwave radiation (Rns ) and the net longwave radiation (Rnl ). In the calculation sheet, the effect of soil heat flux (G) is ignored for daily calculations as the magnitude of the flux in this case is relatively small. The net radiation, expressed in MJ m-2 day-1 , is converted to mm day-1 (equivalent evaporation) in the FAO-PM ETo equation by using 0.408 as the conversion factor within the equation. 4. ETo is obtained by combining the results of the previous steps. Examples 4.1, 4.2, and 4.3 present typical cases using the calculation sheet for monthly, daily, and hourly calculation timesteps. It is highly recommended that computerized software be verified by comparing, step-by-step, with component calculations in these examples. Computerized calculations Calculations of the reference crop evapotranspiration (ETo ) are nearly always computerized. The calculation procedures of all data required for the calculation of ETo by means of the FAO-PM ETo equation are presented in Chapter 3. Typical sequences in which the calculations can be executed are given in the calculation sheets. The procedures presented in Boxes 3.1 (vapour pressure deficit), 3.3 (extraterrestrial radiation and daylight hours), 3.4 (net radiation) and 4.1 (ETo ) can be used when developing a spreadsheet or computer program to calculate ETo . Examples 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3 can be used to confirm computerized calculations for monthly, daily, and hourly timesteps, respectively. Many software packages already use the FAO-PM ETo equation to assess the reference evapotranspiration. As an example, the output of CROPWAT, the FAO software for irrigation scheduling, is presented in Figure 4.1 4.1.2 ETo calculated with different time steps The selection of the time step with which ETo is calculated depends on the purpose of the calculation, the accuracy required and the time step of the climatic data available.115 4. Determination of ETo Ten-day or monthly time step Notwithstanding the non-linearity in the FAO-PM ETo equation and some weather parameter methods, mean ten-day or monthly weather data can be used to compute the mean ten-day or monthly values for the reference evapotranspiration. The value of the reference evapotranspiration calculated with mean monthly weather data is indeed very similar to the average of the daily ETo values calculated with daily average weather data for that month. The meteorological data consist of: • Air temperature: ten-day or monthly average daily maximum (Tmax ) and average daily minimum temperature (Tmin ). • Air humidity: ten-day or monthly average of the daily actual vapour pressure (ea ) derived from psychrometric, dewpoint or relative humidity data. • Wind speed: ten-day or monthly average of daily wind speed data transformed to a 2 m height (u2 ). • Radiation: ten-day or monthly average of daily net radiation (Rn ) computed from the mean ten-day or monthly measured shortwave radiation or from actual durationofdailysunshinehours(n).Theextraterrestrialradiation(Ra )anddaylight hours (N) for a specific day of the month can be computed using Equations 3.18 and 3.31 in Chapter 3, or can be selected from Tables A2.5 and A2.6 in Annex 2. When the soil is warming (spring) or cooling (autumn), the soil heat flux (G) for monthly periods may become significant relative to the mean monthly Rn . In these cases G cannot be ignored and its value should be determined from the mean monthly air temperatures of the previous and next month. Chapter 3 outlines the calculation procedure (Equations 3.46 and 3.47). Determination of ETo Daily time step Calculation of ETo with the FAO-PM ETo equation on 24-hour time scales will generally provide accurate results. The required meteorological data consists of: • Air temperature: maximum (Tmax ) and minimum (Tmin ) daily air temperatures. • Air humidity: mean daily actual vapour pressure (ea ) derived from psychrometric, dewpoint temperature or relative humidity data. • Wind speed: daily average for 24 hours of wind speed transformed to the 2 m height (u2 ). • Radiation: net radiation (Rn ) measured or computed from solar and longwave radiation or from the actual duration of sunshine (n). The extraterrestrial radiation (Ra ) and daylight hours (N) for a specific day of the month should be computed using Equations 3.18 and 3.31. As the magnitude of daily soil heat flux (G) beneath the reference grass surface is relatively small, it may be ignored for 24-hour time steps. Determination of ETo Hourly time step In areas where substantial changes in wind speed, dewpoint or cloudiness occur during the day, calculation of the ETo equation using hourly time steps is generally better than using 24-hour calculation time steps. Such weather changes can cause 24-hour means to misrepresent the evaporative power of the environment during parts of the day and may introduce errors in the calculations. However, under most conditions, the application of the FAO-PM ETo equation with 24-hour data produces accurate results. Withtheadventofelectronic,automatedweatherstations,weatherdataareincreasingly reported for hourly or shorter periods. Therefore, in situations where calculations are computerized, the FAO-PM ETo equation can be applied on an hourly basis with good results. When applying the FAO-PM ETo equation on an hourly or shorter time scale, the equation and some of the procedures for calculating meteorological data should be adjusted for the smaller time step. The FAO-PM ETo equation for: • hourly time steps during daytime (when Rn ≥ 0): 37 0.408 Δ (Rn - G) + γ − −− −− −− − u2 (es - ea ) T + 273 (2.5a bis) ETo = − −− −− −− −−−−−− −− −− −−− −−− −− −−− −− −− −− −−− −− Δ + γ (1 + 0.24 u2 ) where ETo reference evapotranspiration [mm hour-1 ], Rn net radiation at the grass surface [MJ m-2 hour-1 ], G soil heat flux density [MJ m-2 hour-1 ], T mean hourly air temperature at 2 m height [°C], u2 average hourly wind speed at 2 m height [m s-1 ], es average hourly saturation vapour pressure [kPa], ea average hourly actual vapour pressure [kPa], es −ea saturation vapour pressure deficit [kPa], Δ slope vapour pressure curve [kPa °C-1 ], γ psychrometric constant [kPa °C-1 ]. • hourly time steps during night time (when Rn < 0): 37 −− −−− −− u2 (es - ea ) 0.408 Δ (Rn - G) + γ T + 273 (2.5b bis) ETo = − −− −− −−−− −− −− −− −−− −− −− −− −−− −− −− −− − − −− Δ + γ (1+0.96 u2 ) In Equation 2.5a for hourly timesteps, the coefficient in the numerator is 37 rather than 900 that is used for 24-hour timesteps and the coefficient in the denominator is 0.24 rather than 0.34 that is used for 24-hour. During nighttime (when Rn < 0) the 0.24 coefficient in the denominator is changed to 0.96 in Equation 2.5b (hourly). Given relativehumiditymeasurements,the hourlyactual vapour pressure is determinedas: (RHhr ) ea = e° (Thr ) − −−− −− (4.1) 100122 Crop evapotranspiration where ea average hourly actual vapour pressure [kPa], e°(Thr ) saturation vapour pressure at air temperature Thr [kPa] (Equation 3.6), RHhr average hourly relative humidity [%]. The net radiation is the difference between the net shortwave radiation (Rns ) and the net longwave radiation (Rnl ) at the hourly time steps. Consequently: • If Rns and Rnl need to be calculated, the extraterrestrial radiation value (Ra ) for the hourly period (Equation 3.25) should be used. • In the computation of Rnl by means of Equation 3.42, (σTmax, K 4 + σTmin, K 4 )/2 is replaced by σThr, K 4 and the Stefan-Boltzman constant becomes σ = (4.903/24) 10-9 = 2.043 10-10 MJ m-2 h-1 Since the ratio Rs /Rso is used to represent cloud cover, when calculating Rnl for hourly periods during the nighttime, Rso becomes zero, so that the ratio Rs /Rso does not apply. That ratio in Equation 3.42 can be set equal to the Rs /Rso calculated for a time period occurring2-3hoursbeforesunset,beforethesunanglebecomessmall.Thiswillgenerally serve as a good approximation of cloudiness occurring during the subsequent nighttime. The hourly period that is 2 to 3 hours before sunset can be identified during the computation of Ra as the period where ω, calculated from Equation 3.28, is within the range (ωs − 0.79) ≤ ω ≤ (ωs − 0.52), where ωs is calculated using Equation 3.22. As a more approximate alternative, one can assume Rs /Rso = 0.4 to 0.6 during nighttime periods in humid and subhumid climates and Rs /Rso = 0.7 to 0.8 in arid and semiarid climates. A value of Rs /Rso = 0.3 presumes total cloud cover. Soil heat flux is important for hourly calculations. Equations 3.48 and 3.49 can be used to derive G for the hourly periods. The required meteorological data consists of: • Air temperature: mean hourly temperature (Thr ). • Air humidity: average hourly relative humidity (RHhr ) or hourly dewpoint temperature. • Wind speed: average hourly wind speed data measured at or transformed to 2 m height (u2 ). • Radiation: total hourly solar (Rs ) or net radiation (Rn ). Because of the need for standardization, the constants in Equations 2.5 are based on a constant surface resistance (rs ) of 50 s m-1 during daytime periods, i.e., when Rn > 0. The rs = 50 s m-1 is represented by the 0.24 coefficient in the denominator of Equation 2.5a. This constant resistance best represents the behaviour of the clipped grass reference during daytime hours. The constant resistance is increased to 200 s m-1 for night-time periods, when Rn < 0, and is represented by using a constant 0.96 in the denominator of Equation 2.5b instead of the constant 0.24. When the calculations of hourly ETo from Equation 2.5a and 2.5b are summed over 24-hour periods to produce 24-hour ETo , the hourly sums are generally equivalent to calculations of ETo made on a 24-hour time step. Precise estimates of ETo for specific hourly periods may require the use of aerodynamic stability functions and functions for modifying the value for rs based on levels of radiation, humidity and temperature. Application of these complex functions is not123 4. Determination of ETo normally required when hourly calculations are to be summed to 24-hour totals. Therefore, those functions are not described here. Equations 2.5a and 2.5b can be applied to time steps shorter or longer than hourly timesteps, for example, to 0.5-hour timesteps or 3-hour timesteps as long as all units per time are expressed as units per hour. Due to the added complexity of hourly calculation timesteps, it is highly recommended that a computerized software be verified by comparing, step-by-step, with component calculations in Example 4.3. The example shows calculations for both nighttime and daytime hourly periods. Where some of these data are missing or cannot be calculated, it is strongly recommended that the user estimate the missing climatic data with one of the procedures presented in Chapters 2 and 3 and that the FAO-PM method be used for the calculation of ETo . The use of an alternative ETo calculation procedure, requiring only limited meteorological parameters, is less recommended. Example 4.4 illustrates the estimation of monthly ETo with the FAO-PM for a data set containing only maximum and minimum air temperature. The procedures given in Chapters 2 and 3 to estimate missing humidity, radiation and wind speed data should be validated by comparing ETo calculated with full and with limited data sets for weather stations in the region having complete data sets.125 4. Determination of ETo EXAMPLE 4.4 Determination of monthly ETo with missing data Given the monthly average daily maximum and average daily minimum air temperature of July from a station near Foggia, Italy (41°28' N, altitude 74 m). No other climatic data were recorded. Using the long term CLIMWAT weather database, the Aridity Index (AI) was computed from Chapter 2, Box 2.4, as the ratio between the mean annual precipitation(mm)andthemeanannualclimaticevaporationindex(mm),resultinginAI = 0.51, thus, indicating that the location has a dry sub-humid climate type. In addition, using the same data set it was possible to estimate the average (Tmax −Tmin ) = 13.0 ºC, u2 = 2.42 m s-1 and RH = 71%.
