If the original approach is used Astage 1 and DREW are not required.251 7. ETc and dual crop coefficient (Kc = Kcb + Ke ) The sequential procedure for calculating crop evapotranspiration is presented in Box 7.1. Dual crop coefficient case studies for a dry bean crop at Kimberly, USA, for a pea crop for industry at Alpiarça, Portugal, and a super-high density (hedgerow) olive orchard at Viana do Alentejo, Portugal, are presented with summaries of calculation procedures in Boxes 7.2, 7.3 and 7.4. BOX 7.1 Calculating ETc The calculation procedure lends itself to application by computer, either in the form of electronic spreadsheets (as calculated for Example 7.9) or in the form of structured programming languages. The calculation procedure consists of determining: a. Reference evapotranspiration, ETo : Estimate ETo using the procedure given in Chapter 4. b. Growth stages: Determine the locally adjusted lengths of the four growth stages by performing field observations (for general information on cumulative growing degree days used to estimate growth stage lengths, consult Table 6.11): • initial growth stage: Lini [days]; • crop development stage: Ldev [days]; • mid-season stage: Lmid [days]; and • late season stage: Llate [days]. c. Basal crop coefficient, Kcb : Calculate basal crop coefficients for each day of the growing period: • select Kcb ini , Kcb mid and Kcb end from Tables 7.1 through 7.3; • adjust Kcb mid and Kcb end to the local climatic conditions (Equation 7.2); and • determine the daily Kcb values ◊ initial growth stage: Kcb = Kcb ini , ◊ crop development stage: from Kcb ini to Kcb mid (Equation 6.20), ◊ mid-season stage: Kcb = Kcb mid , ◊ late season stage: from Kcb mid to Kcb end (Equation 6.20). d. Evaporation coefficient, Ke : Calculate the maximum value for Kc , i.e. the upper limit Kc max (Equation 7.4), and determine for each day of the growing period: • the fraction of soil covered by vegetation, fc (Tables 7.1-7.3, Table 7.7 or Equation 7.8); • the fraction of soil surface wetted by irrigation or precipitation, fw (Table 7.6); • the fraction of soil covered by vegetation, fc (Tables 7.1-7.3, Table 7.7 or Equation 7.8); • the fraction of soil surface wetted by irrigation or precipitation, fw (Table 7.6); • the fraction of soil surface from which most evaporation occurs, few (Equation 7.7); • the cumulative depletion from the REW layer, DREW , and from the evaporating soil layer, De , determined by means of a daily (or shorter) soil water balance of the topsoil (Equation 7.14 and 7.15); • the corresponding evaporation reduction coefficient, Kr (Equation 7.12); and • the soil evaporation coefficient, Ke (Equation 7.3). e. Crop evapotranspiration, ETc : Calculate ETc = (Kcb + Ke ) ETo (Equation 7.1).252 Crop evapotranspiration BOX 7.2 Case study for the dual crop coefficient applied to a dry bean crop at Kimberly, Idaho, the United States Results from applying the Kcb + Ke procedure for a snap bean crop harvested as dry seed are shown in Figure 7.6. This example uses the same data set that were used in the case study of Box 6.2. The observed ETc data were measured using a precision lysimeter system at Kimberly, Idaho. Values for Kcb ini , Kcb mid , and Kcb end were calculated in Example 7.1 and Table 7.2 as 0.15, 1.04, and 0.30. However, the Kcb mid is set here to 1.14 to agree best with the observed ETc data for the particular bean crop, as demonstrated in the figure below. The time lengths of growth stages were 25, 25, 30, and 20 days. The Kcb values are plotted in Figure 7.1. The value for Kc max from Eq. 7.4 for the mid-season period averaged 1.24, based on u2 = 2.2 m s-1 and RHmin = 30% for Kimberly. The soil at Kimberly was a silt loam texture. Assuming that the depth of the evaporation soil layer, Ze , was 0.1 m, values for TEW = 22 mm and REW = 9 mm, based on Eq. 7.5 and using soil data from Table 7.5. The occurrence and magnitudes of individual wetting events are shown in the figure below. Nearly all wetting events were from irrigation. Because the irrigation was by furrow irrigation of alternate rows, the value for fw was set equal to 0.5. Irrigation events occurred at about midday or during early afternoon. The agreement between the estimated values for daily Kcb + Ke (thin continuous line) and actual 24-hour measurements (symbols) is relatively good. Measured and predicted Kcb +Ke became higher following wetting by rainfall or irrigation, as expected. The two wet soil evaporation “spikes” occurring during the late initial period and early development period (between days 160 and 180) were less than Kc max , because this evaporation was from a soil surface only partially wetted by furrow irrigation where fw = 0.5. The value for few was constrained to fw by Eq. 7.7 during these two events, because during this period, fw < 1 - fc . Therefore, less than all of the “potential energy” was converted into evaporation due to the limitation on maximum evaporation per unit surface area that was imposed by Eq. 7.3. ETc and dual crop coefficient (Kc = Kcb + Ke ) BOX 7.3 Case study for dual crop coefficient applied to an industrial green pea crop, at Alpiarça, Portugal The results of applying the (Kcb + Ke ) method for a vining pea crop harvested early in the season as green seed for the industry are shown in Figure 7.7. The ETc and its partitioning were estimated using the soil water balance model SIMDualKc, that employs the dual crop coefficient method, after its calibration with observed soil water content data. The values for Kcb ini , Kcb mid , and Kcb end in Table 7.2 were used to initialize the model. After calibration and adjustment to climate conditions (Equation 7.2) the Kcb values were 0.15, 1.13, and 1.09 respectively for the initial, mid-season and end-season stages. The lengths of growth stages were observed in the field as 36 (105 GDD), 32 (255 GDD), 50 (505 GDD), and 2 days (35 GDD). GDD values were computed assuming a Tbase = 5 ºC and a Tupper = 27 ºC. The late season lasted only a few days because the harvest took place immediately after the pea grain had reached maturity. The fraction of ground cover by the crop reached 0.98 at mid-season, and the maximum height was 0.45 m. The value for Kc max from Eq. 7.4 for the mid-season period averaged 1.20 considering u2 = 1.8 m s-1 and RHmin = 53% for Alpiarça. The soil was a silty-loam texture. Assuming that the depth of the evaporation soil layer, Ze , was 0.1 m, values for TEW = 24 mm and REW = 10 mm, based on Eq. 7.5 and using soil data from Table 7.5. The occurrence and magnitudes of individual wetting events are shown in Figure 7.7. The number of wetting events from precipitation and irrigation was similar with a total number of 34 events. Gross irrigation depths ranged from 10 to 19 mm. Because the irrigation was bysprinkler,thevalueforfw wassetequalto1.0.Irrigationeventsoccurredatearlymorning or late afternoon to avoid wind drift evaporation losses. Ke peaks occur in response to high soil water content at the beginning of the cropping season, as well as in response to rainfall and irrigation events. Several Ke peaks occur mainly in the initial and crop development stages when the fraction of ground cover by the crop was small and therefore solar energy was largely available at the soil surface for evaporation. From the middle of the development stage, the Ke peaks became smaller. From the mid-season to harvest, the peaks were very low because the crop almost completely covered the soil (fc = 0.98) and evaporation became nearly null. Therefore, due to the limit on maximum evaporation per unit area imposed by Equation 7.3, less than all the potential energy was converted to soil evaporation. ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions Forces acting on the soil water decrease its potential energy and make it less available for plant root extraction. When the soil is wet, the water has a high potential energy, is relatively free to move and is easily taken up by the plant roots. In dry soils, the water has a low potential energy and is strongly bound by capillary and absorptive forces to the soil matrix and is less easily extracted by the crop. When the potential energy of the soil water drops below a threshold value, the crop is said to be water stressed. The effects of soil water stress are described by multiplying the basal crop coefficient by the water stress coefficient, Ks : ETc act = (Ks Kcb +Ke ) ETo (8.1) For soil water limiting conditions, Ks < 1. Where there is no soil water deficit, Ks = 1. Ks describes the effect of water stress on crop transpiration. Where the single crop coefficient is used, the effect of water stress is incorporated into Kc as: ETc act = Ks Kc ETo = Kc act ETo (8.2) Salinity also decreases the potential energy of the soil water and makes water more difficult to be extracted by the plant roots. Thus Equations 8.1 and 8.2 also apply when salinity of the soil and the irrigation water affect extraction by the roots, with Ks becoming a salinity and water stress coefficient, i.e. salinity and water stress combine in Ks < 1 as developed later in this chapter. Because the water stress coefficient impacts only crop transpiration, rather than evaporation from soil, the application of Ks using Equation 8.1 is generally more accurate than its application using Equation 8.2. However, in situations where evaporation from soil is not a large component of ETc , the use of Equation 8.2 will provide quite reasonable results. 8.1 SOIL WATER AVAILABILITY 8.1.1 Total available water (TAW) Soil water availability refers to the capacity of a soil to retain water available to plants. After heavy rainfall or irrigation, the soil will drain until field capacity is reached. Field capacity is the amount of water that a well-drained soil should hold against gravitational forces, or the amount of water remaining when downward drainage has markedly decreased. In the absence of water supply, the water content in the root zone decreases as a result of water uptake by the crop. As water uptake progresses, the remaining water is held to the soil particles with greater force, lowering its potential energy and making it more difficult for the plant to extract it. Eventually, a point is reached where the crop can no longer extract the remaining water. The water uptake becomes zero when wilting point is reached. Wilting point is the water content at which plants will permanently wilt. As the water content above field capacity cannot be held Chapter 8256 Crop evapotranspiration against the forces of gravity and will drain, and as the water content below wilting point cannot be extracted by plant roots, the total available water in the root zone is the difference between the water content at field capacity and wilting point: TAW = 1000 (θ FC - θ WP ) Zr (8.3) where TAW the total available soil water in the root zone [mm], θFC the water content at field capacity [m3 m-3 ], θWP the water content at wilting point [m3 m-3 ], Zr the rooting depth [m]. TAWistheamountofwaterthatacropcanextractfromitsrootzone,anditsmagnitude depends on the type of soil and the rooting depth. Typical ranges for field capacity and wilting point are listed in Table 7.5 for various soil texture classes. Ranges of the maximum effective rooting depth for various crops are given in Tables 6.1 through 6.3. 8.1.2 Readily available water (RAW) Although water is theoretically available in the root zone until the wilting point is attained, the crop water uptake is reduced well before the wilting point is reached. Where the soil is sufficiently wet, the soil supplies water fast enough to meet the atmospheric demand of the crop, and water uptake equals ETc . As the soil water content decreases, water becomes more strongly bound to the soil matrix and is more difficult to extract. When the soil water content drops below a threshold value, soil water can no longer be transported quickly enough toward the roots to respond to the transpiration demand and the crop begins to experience stress. The fraction of TAW that a crop can extract from the root zone without suffering water stress is the readily available soil water: RAW = p TAW (8.4) where RAW the readily available soil water in the root zone [mm], p average fraction of total available soil water (TAW) that can be depleted from the root zone before moisture stress (reduction in ET) occurs [0-1]. Values for p are listed in Table 8.1 through 8.4 respectively for vegetable, field, woody fruit and grass crops. The factor p differs from one crop to another. The factor p normally varies from 0.20 for very shallow rooted plants at high rates of ETc (> 8 mm d-1 ) to 0.70 for deep rooted plants at low rates of ETc (< 3 mm d-1 ). A value of 0.50 for p is commonly used for many field crops, a lower value is often used for vegetable crops, and a higher value is used for woody crops with large roots and to grasses, generally less susceptible to water stress. For many woody crops there is little information and, thus, indicative values are given for each group. The p value is small for frequent and very frequent irrigation, and larger for surface irrigation where time intervals between irrigation events are large. Therefore, the values of p listed in Table 8.1 through 8.4 are for large time intervals between irrigation events, thus for surface irrigation. Users may reduce the tabulated p values for the irrigation method that they are using. The fraction p is a function of the evaporation power of the atmosphere. At low rates of ETc , the p values listed in Table 8.1 through 8.4 are higher than at high rates of ETc . For hot dry weather conditions, where ETc is high, p maybe 10-25 percent less than the tabulated values in Table 8.1 through 8.4, and the soil is still relatively wet when257 8. ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions the stress starts to occur. When the crop evapotranspiration is low, p may be up to 20 percent more than the listed values. Often, a constant value is used for p for a specific growing period, rather than varying the value each day. A numerical approximation for adjusting p for ETc rate is: p = pTable + 0.04 (5 − ETc ) (8.5) where the adjusted p is limited to 0.1 ≤ p ≤ 0.8 and ETc is in mm day-1 . The influence of the ETc numerical adjustment on p is shown in Figure 8.1. Examples of estimation of RAW for various soils and crops are given in Example 8.1. FIGURE 8.1 Depletion factor for different levels of crop evapotranspiration 1.0 0.8 0.6 0.4 0.2 0.0

crop evapotranspiration (mm/day) depletion factor minimum maximum p(Table 8.1) = 0.6 0.5 0.4 0.3 0.2 Source: FAO, 1998 Expressing the tolerance of crops to water stress as a function of the fraction (p) of TAW is only an approximation. The rate of root water uptake is, in fact, influenced more directly by the potential energy level of the soil water (soil matric potential and the associated hydraulic conductivity) than by water content alone. As a certain soil matric potential corresponds in different soil types with different soil water contents, the value for p is also a function of the soil type. Generally, it can be stated that for fine textured soils (clay) the p values listed in Table 8.1 can be reduced by 5-10 percent, while for more coarse textured soils (sand), they can be increased by 5-10 percent. RAW is similar to the term Management Allowed Depletion (MAD) often used in irrigation management practice. However, values for MAD are influenced by management and economic factors in addition to the physical factors influencing p. Generally, MAD < RAW where there is risk aversion or uncertainty, and MAD > RAW where plant moisture deficit is an intentional part of soil water management.258 Crop evapotranspiration TABLE 8.1 Soil water depletion fraction for no stress (p) for vegetable crops Vegetable Crops Depletion fraction p (for ETc ≈ 5 mm day-1 ) Root, tuber, bulb and stem vegetable crops Asparagus (Asparagus officinalis) 0.45 Carrots (Daucus carota) 0.30 Cassava (Manihot esculenta) 0.50 Celeriac, celery root (Apium graveolens group rapaceum) 0.30 Chufa, tigernut (Cyperus esculentus) 0.35 Garlic (Allium sativum) 0.30 Leek (Allium porrum) 0.30 Mashua (Tropaeolum tuberosum) 0.35 Onions (Allium cepa) 0.30 Parsnip (Pastinaca sativa) 0.40 Potato (Solanum tuberosum) 0.40 Radish (Raphanus sativus) 0.30 Rutabaga (Brassica napus) 0.40 Sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas) 0.45 Table beets (Beta vulgaris) 0.45 Turnip (Brassica rapa) 0.40 Yam, Taro (Colocasia esculenta) 0.20 Leafy and flower vegetable crops African cabbage, spiderwisp (Cleome gynandra) 0.40 African spinach, (Amaranthus cruentus) 0.30 Artichoke (Cynara scolymus) 0.45 Arugula, rucula (Eruca sativa or E. vesicaria) 0.35 Broccoli (Brassica oleracea cv. italica) 0.40 Brussel sprouts (Brassica oleracea cv. gemmifera) 0.40 Cabbage Common cabbage (Brassica oleracea) 0.35 Kale, leaf cabbage (B. oleracea var. sabellica), and Collard green (B. oleracea var. This is accomplished by multiplying the crop coefficient by the water stress coefficient, Ks (Equations 8.1 and 8.2). Water content in the root zone can also be expressed by the root zone depletion, Dr , i.e. the water consumed relative to field capacity. At field capacity, the root zone depletion is zero (Dr = 0). When soil water is consumed by evapotranspiration the depletion is increasing, and stress will be induced when Dr reaches RAW. When the root zone depletion exceeds RAW (the water content drops below the threshold θt ), the root zone depletion is high enough to limit evapotranspiration to less than potential values and the crop evapotranspiration begins to decrease in proportion to the amount of water remaining in the root zone (Figure 8.2 and Example 8.2).264 Crop evapotranspiration FIGURE 8.2 Water stress coefficient, Ks 0 θ: soil water content TAW TAW RAW RAW Ks 1.00 0.80 0.60 0.40 0.20 0.00 Dr : depletion from root zone (mm) θFC θt θWP Source: FAO, 1998 For Dr > RAW, Ks is given by: TAW-Dr TAW - Dr Ks

TAW-RAW (1-p)TAW where Ks transpirationreductionfactordependingontheavailablesoilwater[0-1], Dr root zone depletion [mm], TAW total available soil water in the root zone [mm], p fractionofTAWthatacropcanextractwithoutsufferingwaterstress[-]. After the calculation of Ks , the actual evapotranspiration ETc act is computed by means of Equation 8.1 or 8.2. When the root zone depletion (Dr ) is smaller than Readily Available Water (RAW), Ks = 1.265 8. day Source: FAO, 1998 8.3 SOIL WATER BALANCE The estimation of Ks requires a daily water balance computation for the root zone. Schematically (Figure 8.4), the root zone can be presented by means of a container in which the water content may fluctuate. To express the water content as root zone depletion is useful. It makes the adding and subtracting of losses and gains straightforward as the various parameters of the soil water budget are usually expressed in terms of water depth. Rainfall, irrigation, and capillary rise of groundwater towards the root zone add water to the root zone and decrease the root zone depletion. Soil evaporation, crop transpiration and percolation losses remove water from the root zone and increase the depletion. The daily water balance, expressed in terms of depletion at the end of the day is: Dr , i = Dr , i-1 -(P - RO)i - Ii - CRi + ETc act , i + DPi (8.7) where Dr,i root zone depletion at the end of the day i [mm], Dr,i-1 water content in the root zone at the end of the previous day, i-1 [mm], P precipitation [mm], RO runoff [mm], I applied irrigation depth [mm], CR capillary rise [mm], DP deep percolation or drainage depth [mm], ETc act,i (actual) crop evapotranspiration on day i [mm].267 8. ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions FIGURE 8.4 Water balance of the soil root zone Irrigation Evapotranspiration Rain Runoff saturation fiel capacity threshold wilting point Capillary Rise Deep Percolation depletion RAW TAW Source: FAO, 1998 8.3.1 Limits on Dr,i In Figure 8.4 it is assumed that water can be stored in the root zone until field capacity is reached.Althoughfollowingheavyrainorirrigationthewatercontentmighttemporally exceed field capacity, the total amount of water above field capacity is assumed to be lost the same day by deep percolation, following any ET for that day. By assuming that the root zone is at field capacity following heavy rain or irrigation, the minimum value for the depletion Dr,i is zero. As a result of percolation and evapotranspiration, the water content in the root zone will gradually decrease and the root zone depletion will increase. In the absence of any wetting event, the water content will steadily reach its minimum value θWP . At that moment no water is left for evapotranspiration in the root zone, Ks becomes zero, and the root zone depletion has reached its maximum value TAW. The limits imposed on Dr,i are consequently 0 ≤ Dr,i ≤ TAW. 8.3.2 Initial depletion To initiate the water balance for the root zone, the initial depletion Dr,i-1 should be estimated. The initial depletion can be derived from measured soil water content by: Dr , i-1 = 1000 (θFC - θi-1 ) Zr (8.8) where θi-1 is the average soil water content for the effective root zone. Following heavy rain orirrigation, theuser can assumethatthe rootzone isnearfieldcapacity,i.e.Dr,i-1 ≈ 0. 8.3.3 Precipitation (P), runoff (RO) and irrigation (I) Pi is equivalent to daily precipitation. Daily precipitation in amounts less than about 0.2 ETo is normally entirely evaporated and can usually be ignored in the water balance calculations especially when the single crop coefficient approach is being used. Ii is equivalent to the mean infiltrated irrigation depth expressed for the entire field surface. Runoff from the surface during precipitation can be predicted using standard procedures from hydrological texts.268 Crop evapotranspiration 8.3.4 Capillary rise (CR) The amount of water transported upwards by capillary rise from the water table to the root zone depends on the soil type, the water table depth below the ground surface (WTD), the crop evapotranspiration and the wetness of the root zone. However, CR is primarily influenced by WTD as explained in Box 8.1. The wetness of the root zone is represented by the actual soil water storage in the root zone (Wa ). When WTD is close to the root zone, especially if ETc act is high, upward fluxes are expected to occur even when the soil water storage is close to field capacity. Under such WTD and ETc act conditions, CR fluxes are important, especially in silty soils. These fluxes depend mainly on WTD and may start at different water table depths and various values of soil water storage, depending on the soil type. This storage is termed the critical soil water storage (Wc ) [mm] and varies between the soil water storage at field capacity and the storage when the soil water content corresponds to the depletion fraction p for no stress (Table 8.1). The critical soil water storage may be calculated as: Wc = a1 WTDb1 (8.9) where WTD water table depth [m], a1 soil water storage for the maximum root depth at field capacity [mm], b1 adjustment parameter depending on soil physical characteristics, namely soil texture [-] (Table 8.5). ETc act progressively depletes the soil water storage, and the upward fluxes attain a maximum value, called the potential capillary flux (CRmax ), which is mainly dependent on WTD and less dependent on ETc act . For shallow water table depths (WTD ≤ 3 m), a steady soil moisture profile is formed when CRmax is reached and the soil water storage assumes an almost constant value, termed the steady soil water storage (Ws ), that is almost independent of ETc . In contrast, when the water table is deeper (WTD > 3 m), the upward flow may no longer be sufficient to sustain ETc act , which continuously decreases due to increased soil water deficit. Under such conditions steady soil water storage (Ws ) varies with ETc act and CRmax is only reached when the soil water storage is low. The Ws , which is the total water stored in the root zone [mm], primarily depends upon the groundwaterdepthandthereforemaybeapproximatedsimilarlytoWc (Equation8.9)as: Ws = a2 WTDb2 for WTD ≤ 3 m (8.10a) Ws = 240 mm for WTD > 3 m (8.10b) a2 = 1.1 (WFC + WWP )/2 (8.10c) where, a2 empirical unsaturated soil water storage parameter [mm], b2 adjustment parameter depending on the soil texture [-], WFC and WWP root zone water storage at field capacity and at the wilting point respectively [mm]. Indicative values for parameters a2 and b2 are given in Table 8.5. TABLE 8.5 Parameters of the capillary rise parametric equations for three different soil types Parameters Silt loam soil Sandy loam soil Clay loam soil a1 [mm] 385 321 407 b1 − 0.17 − 0.16 − 0.32 a2 [mm] 320 303 289 b2 − 0.27 − 0.54 − 0.16 a3 − 1.3 − 0.15 − 1.4 b3 6.6 2.1 6.8 a4 4.6 7.55 1.11 b4

under soil water and salinity stress conditions The potential capillary flux, CRmax , depends upon the groundwater depth and the crop water demand, ETc act , and varies daily with the fluctuations of WTD and ETc act . A threshold value for WTD exists, so that when WTD is greater than the threshold, CRmax no longer increases. This critical WTD (WTDc ), corresponds to the water table depth when CR reaches the virtually constant value CRmax , i.e., the maximum potential groundwater contribution. It has been observed that the critical water table depth mainly depends on the ETc act : WTDc =a3 ETc act + b3 When ETc act ≤ 4 mm d-1 (8.11a) WTDc = 1.4 m When ETc act > 4 mm d-1 (8.11b) where indicative values for a3 and b3 are given in Table 8.5. When WTD > WTDc , the flux CRmax decreases with WTD according to a power law. In contrast, at high water table depths (WTD ≤ WTDc ), CRmax no longer depends on WTDc but on ET rates. Thus, CRmax [mm d-1 ] can be estimated daily, approximately, as: CRmax = k ETc act for WTD ≤ WTDc (8.12a) CRmax = a4 WTDb4 for WTD > WTDc (8.12b) where k factor relating evapotranspiration with crop transpiration [-] (Eq. 8.12), a4 and b4 empirical parameters related with the soil characteristics [-] (Table 8.5). For ETc act ≤ 4 mm d-1 , the maximum value for CRmax approaches the potential crop transpiration (Tc ) while for larger ET rates (ETc > 4 mm d-1 ) it is smaller than Tc because CR fluxes are insufficient to sustain the potential root extraction rates in the absence of precipitation or irrigation. Then the parameter k in Equation 8.12 is calculated as either a function of ET estimated from the leaf area index (LAI), or as a fixed dependency of ETc act : k = 1 – e-0.6 LAI for ETc act ≤ 4 mm d-1 (8.13a) k = 3.8/ETc act for ETc act > 4 mm d-1 (8.13b) CR is higher and closer to its potential maximum value when WTD is close to WTDc and the soil water storage in the root zone is smaller, near the steady soil water storage, Ws (Equations 8.10). The fluxes CR may be approximated as a function of CRmax (Equations 8.14) as influenced by the soil water storage in the root zone: CR = CRmax for Wa < Ws (8.14a) (Wc -Wa ) CR = CRmax − −− −− −− for Ws ≤ Wa ≤ Wc (8.14b) Wc -Ws CR = 0 for Wa > Wc (8.14c) WTD values in equations 8.9 to 8.12 should be limited to a depth within the root zone. The parameters a1 through a4 and b1 through b4 , for accuracy of computations, may need to be optimized. If CR observations are available, parameters in Table 8.5 can be used to begin computations, with changes to those parameters made until differences between observed and estimated CR are small. If CR observations are not available for calibration of those parameters, the search for the best parameter values is done by comparing the results of the ETc act using the parameters of Table 8.5 with270 Crop evapotranspiration those obtained in successive steps of the calibration process until differences between observed and estimated ET are small. An example application is presented for a case study in Inner Mongolia, China, using the soil water balance model SIMDualKc. As soon as Dr,i exceeds RAW, the crop evapotranspiration is reduced and ETc can be computed from Equation 8.1 or 8.2. 8.3.6 Deep percolation (DP) Following heavy rain or irrigation, the soil water content in the root zone might exceed field capacity. In this simple procedure it is assumed that the soil water content is at θFC within the same day of the wetting event, so that the depletion Dr, i in Equation 8.7 becomes zero. Therefore, following heavy rain or irrigation: DPi = (Pi - ROi ) + Ii - ETc act,i - Dr , i-1 when DPi ≥ 0 (8.15) As long as the soil water content in the root zone is below field capacity (i.e. Dr, i > 0), it is assumed that the soil will not drain and DPi = 0. The decrease of the soil water storage in the root zone (W [mm]) due to internal drainage when actual soil water storage is initially above the water storage at field capacity (W > WFC ), may be represented by a decay function of time as: W = aD tbD (8.16) where t time after an irrigation or rain that increases storage to above field capacity [days], aD potentialdepthofwaterstoragebetweenfieldcapacityandsaturation over the effective depth of the root zone [mm], bD adjustment parameter referring to the potential rate of soil drainage. DP (mm d-1 ) is computed daily for successive days without rain or irrigation until the soil drains to field capacity. This is done using the daily storages Wi and Wi+1 for days i and i+1 and the ET for the preceding day: DPi+1 = (Wi – Wi+1 ) – ETc act, i (8.17) Table 8.6 presents indicative values for the parameters of Equation 8.16. TABLE 8.6 Indicative values for the drainage parameters of Equation 8.16 for three primary soil types and associated water holding characteristics Soil type θsat [m3 m-3 ] θ FC [m3 m-3 ] aD [mm] bD [-]

The soil water content at saturation was 0.33 m3 m-3 , at field capacity was 0.22 m3 m-3 and at the wilting point was 0.05 m3 m-3 . Using the described decay function, the model was able to adequately simulate soil water and deep percolation. The estimated seasonal DP was 170 mm. The primary objective of irrigation is to apply water at the right time and in the right amount. The timing and depth of future irrigations can be planned by calculating the soil water balance of the root zone on a daily basis (Equation 8.7). To avoid crop water stress, irrigations should be applied before or at the moment when the readily availablesoilwaterisdepletedsothatDr,i isalways≤RAW.Toavoiddeeppercolationlosses that may leach valuable nutrients out of the root zone, the net irrigation depth should be smaller than or equal to the root zone depletion (Ii ≤ Dr,i ) unless leaching of salts is needed. Example 8.3 illustrates the application of a water balance of the root zone to predict irrigation dates to avoid water stress. The example utilizes various calculations for Ke from Example 7.8. BOX 8.3 Case study on the application of the dual Kc approach to assess the irrigation scheduling used for a water-stressed soybean crop The results of applying the (Kcb + Ke ) approach to a water-stressed soybean crop are shown in the Figure 8.9. The ETc act and its partitioning were estimated using the soil water balance model SIMDualKc after calibration with observed soil water content data. After calibration and adjustment to climate conditions (Equation 7.2) the potential Kcb values were 0.15, 1.10, and 0.35 respectively for the initial, mid-season and end-season stages while the p value was 0.50 for all the crop growth stages. The maximum fraction of ground covered by soybeans was 0.99. The occurrence and magnitudes of individual wetting events from precipitation are shown in the Figure 8.9. The number of wetting events was 27 and the seasonal precipitation was 786 mm. However, almost 45% of this water was not available to the crop due to the occurrence of DP and runoff that commonly occur following large precipitation events. The Kcb act curve deviates from the potential Kcb curve due to water stress. A moderate water stress occurred during the first half of the mid-season, while a negligible water deficit occurred during the late season. High Ke peaks occurred in response to precipitation events when the soil water content of the evaporation layer was high at the beginning of the crop season. The Ke peaks during the mid-season stage are quite small because the fraction of ground covered by the crop was very high during that period. The observed ETc act data were measured using an eddy covariance system and actual Tc was measured using a sap flow method. This example uses the same data that were used in the case study of Box 7.4 but herein using the farmers’ irrigation schedule. The Figure 8.10 shows the daily dynamics of potential and actual Kcb and Ke curves estimated using the soil water balance model SIMDualKc after calibration of Kcb against the previously mentioned observations. The potential values for Kcb ini , Kcb mid , and Kcb end were estimated to be 0.30, 0.49, and 0.43, respectively. A Kcb for the non-growing period, when the crop is dormant, was set at 0.30. The soil of the farmer’s field had a sandy loam texture. The depth of the evaporation soil layer, Ze , was 0.1 m, with TEW = 18 mm and REW = 9 mm based on Eq. 7.5 and the observed soil data. The fraction of ground covered by the trees reached a maximum of 0.38 by mid-season and the maximum tree height was 4 m. The occurrence of precipitation and irrigation events is shown in the Figure 8.10, which includes the out of season and dormant periods. All irrigation wetting events occurred during the mid-season stage when precipitation and soil water storage were not sufficient to maintain crop transpiration. This is observed by the open circles (observed total Kcb + Ke ) and the dashed line (actual Kcb curve) below the solid line representing the potential Kcb curve. The soil evaporation Ke curve (dotted line) peaked after wetting events by rain or high-frequency irrigation. The deviation of the actual Kcb curve (and observations) from the potential Kcb curve whenever a water deficit occurred in the mid-season stage indicates that the drip irrigation schedule adopted by the farmer was inadequate to ensure potential crop transpiration and represented a water deficit irrigation management. The selection of values to be used must be done carefully because the crop response to water stress depends on the crop cultivars, which may have a wide range of variation as tabulated for various crops. Recognizing the degree of tolerance to moisture stress for the cultivars being used is recommended. Also to be considered is the effect of cumulative stress and its timing during the season because stress impacts may be different when crops recover from stress during the first part of the cycle, or are subjected to stress at more critical stages such as flowering or fruiting. For the crops not included in Table 8.7, users are advised to consider Ky for crops of the same family or that have similar behaviour. Nevertheless, care must be used for this purpose. 8.6 STRESS EFFECTS OF SOIL SALINITY Salts in the soil water solution can reduce evapotranspiration by making soil water less "available" for plant root extraction. Salts have an affinity for water and hence additional force is required for the crop to extract water from a saline soil. The presence of salts in the soil water solution reduces the total potential energy of the soil water solution. In addition, some salts may cause toxic effects in plants and can reduce plant metabolism, growth and yield. A function is presented hereafter that predicts the reduction in evapotranspiration caused by the salinity of soil water. The function is derived by combining yield-salinity equations from the FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No29 with yield-ET equations from FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No33. The resulting equation provides a first approximation to the reduction in evapotranspiration expected under various salinity conditions. 8.6.1 Salinity impacts and crops tolerance There is evidence that crop yield and transpiration are not as sensitive to low osmotic potential as they are to low matric potential. Under saline conditions, many plants are able to partially compensate for the low osmotic potential of the soil water by280 Crop evapotranspiration building up higher internal solute contents. This is done by absorbing ions from the soil solution and by synthesizing organic osmolytes. Both of these reactions reduce the impact of osmotic potential on water availability. However, the synthesis of organic osmolytes does require the expenditure of metabolic energy. Therefore, plant growth is often reduced under saline conditions. The reduced plant growth impacts transpiration by reducing ground cover, and a further reduction in transpiration is sometimes additionally caused by partial stomatal closure. Other impacts of salts in the soil include direct sodium and chloride toxicities and induced nutrient deficiencies. These deficiencies reduce plant growth by reducing the rate of leaf elongation, the enlargement, and the division of cells in leaves. The modality depends on the method of irrigation. With sprinkler irrigation, adsorption of sodium and chloride through the leaf can result in toxic conditions for all crop species. With surface or trickle irrigation, direct toxic conditions generally occur only in vine and tree crops; however, high levels of sodium can induce calcium deficiencies for all crop species. Sincesaltconcentrationchangesasthesoilwatercontentchanges,soilsalinityisnormally measured and expressed on the basis of the electrical conductivity of the saturation extract of the soil (ECe ). The ECe is defined as the electrical conductivity of the soil water solution after the addition of a sufficient quantity of distilled water to bring the soil water content to saturation. ECe is typically expressed in deciSiemens per meter (dS m-1 ). Under optimum management conditions, crop yields remain at potential levels until a specific, threshold electrical conductivity of the saturation soil water extract (ECe threshold ) is reached. If the average ECe of the root zone increases above this critical threshold value, the yield is presumed to begin to decrease linearly in proportion to the increase in salinity. The rate of decrease in yield with increase in salinity is usually expressed as a slope, b, having units of % reduction in yield per dS m-1 increase in ECe . Allplantsdonotrespondtosalinityinasimilarmanner;somecropscanproduceacceptable yields at much higher soil salinity levels than others. This is because some crops are better able to make the needed osmotic adjustments that enable them to extract more water from a saline soil, or they may be more tolerant of some of the toxic effects of salinity. Salt tolerance for many agricultural crops is provided in the FAO Irrigation and Drainage Papers No. 33 and 48. The ECe threshold and slope b from these and other sources are listed in Tables 8.8 through 8.11, respectively for vegetable, field, fruit tree and vine, and grass and forage crops. The data serve only as a guideline - tolerance vary depending on climate, soil conditions and cultural practices. Crops are often less tolerant during germination and seedling stage. As can be observed from the data in these Tables, there is an 8 to 11-fold range in salt tolerance of agricultural crops. The effect of soil salinity on yield and crop evapotranspiration is hence crop specific. The ECe threshold and b parameters in Tables 8.8 through 8.11 were determined primarily in research experiments using nearly steady-state irrigation where soil water contents were maintained at levels close to field capacity. However, under most types of irrigation scheduling for sprinkler and surface irrigation, the soil water content is typically depleted to well below field capacity, so that the EC of the soil water solution, ECsw , increases prior to irrigation, even though the EC of the saturation extract does not change. The increased salt concentration in the soil water solution reduces the osmotic potential of the soil water solution (it becomes more negative), so that the plant must expend more metabolic energy and may exert more mechanical force to absorb water. In addition, the metabolic and toxic effects of salts on plants may become more pronounced as the soil dries and concentrations increase. However, the variation in soil water content during an irrigation interval has not been found to strongly influence crop evapotranspiration. This is because of the rise of soil water content to levels that are above that experienced under steady state irrigation early in a long irrigation interval.281 8. There is a similar, counteractive decrease in soil water content later in a long irrigation interval. In addition, the distribution of salts in the root zone under low frequency irrigation can reduce salinity impacts during the first portion of the irrigation interval. Also, under high frequency irrigation of the soil surface, soil evaporation losses are higher. Consequently, given the same application depth, the leaching fraction is reduced. For these reasons, the length of irrigation interval and the change in EC of soil water during the interval have usually not been found to be factors in the reduction of ET, given that the same depths of water are infiltrated into the root zone over time.285 8. ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions Increased evaporation (Ke ) under high frequency irrigation of the soil surface may counteract reductions in Kc caused by stress due to high ECe of the root zone. Under these conditions, the total Kc and ETc are not very different from the non-saline, standard conditions under less frequent irrigation, even though crop yields and crop transpiration are reduced. Because of this, under saline conditions, the Ks reducing factor should only be applied with the dual Kc approach. The effects of soil salinity and water stress are generally additive in their impacts on crop evapotranspiration. Therefore, the same yield-ET functions may hold for both water shortage induced stress and for salinity induced stress. 8.6.2 Yield-Salinity relationship A widely practiced approach for predicting the reduction in crop yield due to salinity has been described in the FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No29. The approach presumes that, under optimum management conditions, crop yields remain at potential levels until a specific, threshold electrical conductivity of the soil water solution is reached. When salinity increases beyond this threshold, crop yields are presumed to decrease linearly in proportion to the increase in salinity. The soil water salinity is expressed as the electrical conductivity of the saturation extract, ECe . In equation form, the salinity response function is a linear equation. The procedure of FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No29 is followed: Yact b − − −− = 1-(ECe - ECe threshold )− −− when ECe > ECe threshold (8.19) Ymax 100 where Yact actual crop yield, Ymax maximum expected crop yield when ECe < ECe threshold , ECe mean electrical conductivity of the root zone saturation extract [dS m-1 ], ECe threshold electrical conductivity of the saturation extract at the threshold of ECe when crop yield first reduces below Ym [dS m-1 ], b rateofyieldreductionperunitofECe aboveECe threshold [%/(dSm-1 )]. Values for ECe threshold and b have been provided in the FAO Irrigation and Drainage Papers No29 and 48 as well as in the original version of FAO 56 and are updated and newly listed in Tables 8.7 through 8.10 for several agricultural crops. Relative to vegetable crops there is no published information on herbs, spices, and special vegetable crops, but it is commonly known that these crops are sensitive, or moderately sensitive, to soil and water salinity. There is also insufficient information about fruit tree and shrub crops, where tolerance varies much among crops. Generally, they should be considered as moderately sensitive (MS) at best. Salinity-yield data from the FAO Irrigation and Drainage papers No 29 and 48 were mostly from studies where soil water content was held at about –30 kPa potential or higher at the 0.3 to 0.6 m soil depth, depending on the crop. However, these papers indicate that for most crops, the data are transferable to typical field situations where the readily available soil water (RAW) is depleted between irrigations. The accumulation of salts in terms of their osmotic effects is further modified depending on soil texture, agro-climatic conditions, ionic components of salinity, and soil-irrigation crop management conditions.286 Crop evapotranspiration 8.6.3 Salinity-ET reduction relationship Without water stress (Dr < RAW) When salinity stress occurs without water stress, Equations 8.18 and 8.19 can be combined and solved for an equivalent Ks , where Ks = ETc act /ETc : b Ks = 1 - − −− −− − (ECe - ECe threshold ) when ECe > ECe threshold (8.20) Ky 100 for conditions when ECe > ECe threshold and soil water depletion is less than the readily available soil water depth (Dr < RAW). Dr and RAW are defined in a previous section. Combined salinity-water stress (Dr > RAW) When soil water stress occurs in addition to salinity stress, Equations 8.5, 8.19 and 8.20 need to be modified to appropriately perform the soil water balance as explained in Figure 8.12. Moreover, under salinity conditions, crop roots must overcome increased water retention energies due to both matric potential and decreased osmotic potential. ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions This condition takes into account that the soil water content at the wilting point is higher in saline soils and therefore the available soil water is lower than under no salinity conditions. ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions Limitations Because the impact of salinity on plant growth and yield and on crop evapotranspiration is a time-integrated process, generally only the seasonal value for Ky is used in predicting the reduction in evapotranspiration. For many crops, the seasonal Ky is nearly 1.0 (Table 8.7). For crops where Ky is unknown, the user may assume Ky = 1 in Equations 8.19 and 8.25, or may select a Ky relative to a crop type that has similar behaviour. Equations 8.19 and 8.25 are suggested as approximate estimates of salinity impacts on ET, and represent the general effects of salinity on evapotranspiration as occurring over an extended period of time (as measured in weeks or months). These equations are not expected to be accurate for predicting ETc act for specific days. Nor do they include other complicating effects such as specific ion toxicity. Application of Equations 8.19 and 8.25 presumes that the ECe represents the average ECe for the root zone. The equations presented may not be valid at high salinity, where the linear relationships between ECe , crop yield and Ks may not hold. The use of Equations 8.19 and 8.25 should usually be restricted to ECe < ECthreshold + 50/b. In addition, care is required when the equations predict Ya = 0 before Ks = 0 when Ky > 1 and vice versa. As indicated earlier, a reduction in ETc in the presence of soil salinity is often partially caused by reduced plant size and fraction of ground cover. These effects are largely included in the coefficient values. Therefore, where plant growth is affected by salinity, Equations 8.19 and 8.25 are applied and no other reductions in Kc are required. 8.6.4 Application Under steady state conditions, the value for ECe can be predicted as a function of EC of the irrigation water (ECiw ) and the leaching fraction, using a standard leaching formula. For example, the FAO-29 leaching formula LR = ECiw / (5 ECe – ECiw ) predicts the leaching requirement when approximately a 40-30-20-10 percent water extraction pattern occurs from the upper to lower quarters of the root zone prior to irrigation. ECiw is the electrical conductivity of the irrigation water. From this equation, ECe is estimated as: 1 + LF ECiw ECe

LF 5 where LF, the actual leaching fraction, is used in place of LR, the leaching requirement. Equation 8.26 predicts ECe = 1.5 ECiw under conditions where a 15-20 percent leaching fraction is used. Other leaching fraction equations can be used in place of the FAO-29 equation to fit local characteristics. Equation 8.26 is only true if the irrigation water quality and the leaching fraction are constant over the growing season. Time is required to attain a salt equilibrium in the soil. If there are important winter rains of high-quality water and often excellent leaching, the salt balance in the soil will be quite different at the beginning of the season and with a lower average ECe of the root zone than would be predicted from Equation 8.26. An appropriate local calibration of Equation 8.26 is desirable under these particular conditions.290 Crop evapotranspiration EXAMPLE 8.4 Effect of soil salinity on crop evapotranspiration A field of beans is cultivated on a silt loam soil and is irrigated during the midseason period using water having salinity ECiw = 1.0 dS m-1 . ETc under soil water and salinity stress conditions BOX 8.5 Example of the combined effects of water and salinity stresses The results of applying the (Kcb + Ke ) method for a maize crop under water and salinity stress are shown in the Figure 8.14. This example uses the same data set that were used in the case study of Box 8.1; however, only two examples were selected. The observed ETc act , soil water content and CR data were measured using five static water-table lysimeters. The ETc act partitioning was estimated using the soil water balance model SIMDualKc after proper calibration with observed soil water content data. After calibration and adjustment to climate conditions (Equation 7.2) the Kcb values were 0.15, 1.15, and 0.30 respectively for the initial, mid-season and end-season stages while the depletion factor p was 0.55 for the initial, development and mid season stages, and 0.80 for the end season stage. All lysimeters were partially covered (75 percent of the soil surface) with plastic mulch, which led to a reduction of few and constrained the Kc max (Equation 7.4), thus limited the soil evaporation component (Equation 7.3). Precipitation was prevented due to the use of a rain shelter. The occurrence of irrigation wetting events was the same in all the lysimeters. Seasonal gross irrigation was 561–591 mm. The Kcb act curve deviates from the potential Kcb curve due to the combined effect of water and salinity stresses (Figure 8.14). Salinity stress occurred since the beginning of the season because ECe was higher than the threshold limit of 1.7 dS m-1 (Table 8.2). Water stress occurred mainly during mid-season, resulting in a greater deviation of the Kcb act curve from the standard Kcb curve. Ke peaks occurred in the Ke in response to high soil watercontentatthebeginningofthecropseason,andthelargeirrigationeventspracticed. Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation ActualcropET(ETcact )referstobothagriculturalandnaturalvegetationincludingnon pristine agricultural vegetation. The latter is defined, in the usage here, as vegetation having less than perfect growing conditions or stand characteristics (i.e. relatively poor conditions of density, height, leaf area, fertility, vitality and biomass yield) as compared to “pristine” maximal yield conditions. Non-typical cropping conditions are also included and refer to types or arrangements of agricultural crops that are not listed or described in Tables 6.1 to 6.4 and 7.1 to 7.3. However, non-typical cropping conditions are mainly dealt with in Chapter 10. The approach whereby a crop is characterized by a crop coefficient, Kc , and the crop evapotranspiration is given by the product of Kc and the reference evapotranspiration ETo ,providesasimpleandconvenientwayofalsocharacterizingtheevapotranspiration from natural vegetation and for diverse non-typical cultivation practices. This chapter presents procedures for estimating actual Kc and Kcb values for natural vegetation and for agricultural vegetation independent of having Kc and Kcb values available. The section on estimating crop coefficients from fraction of ground cover and crop height is particularly important because it focuses on the estimation of ETc act aimed at its use in irrigation scheduling and water management in real time. 9.1 CALCULATION APPROACH, GENERAL ASPECTS The first step in the Kc ETo approach is the estimation of lengths of crop growth stages, which also applies to natural vegetation (Tables 6.4 and 6.5). The next step is the development of crop coefficient curves that represent the ratios of ETc to ETo during the various growth stages of the vegetation as described in Chapters 5 and 6. 9.1.1 Initial growth stage The procedure to estimate crop coefficients for the initial growth stage for agricultural non-pristine and natural vegetation is identical to that described for agricultural crops in Chapter 6 (single crop coefficient Kc ini ) or Chapter 7 (dual crop coefficient, Kcb ini + Ke ). The crop coefficient during this stage is primarily determined by the frequency with which the soil is wetted. 9.1.2 Mid and late season stages The Kc act during the mid-season period (Kc mid act and Kcb mid act ), and to a lesser extent the Kc act during the late season period, differ from those described in previous chapters 6 and 7. As the ground cover for non-pristine vegetation is often reduced, the Kc act is affected to a large extent by the frequency of precipitation and/or irrigation and by the amount of leaf area and ground cover. Natural vegetation, mainly when sparse, is affected by the frequency of precipitation and the Kc act is dependent on the amount of leaf area and ground cover. Dual crop coefficient approach The determination of Kc for non-pristine and natural vegetation should ordinarily follow the approach described in Chapter 7 whereby separate transpiration (Kcb ) and Chapter 9294 Crop evapotranspiration evaporation (Ke ) coefficients are used. The effects of evaporation from the soil surface can be directly estimated as such. Two procedures that can be used to adjust the basal crop coefficient (Kcb mid act ) for sparse vegetation are presented in this section. In these approaches, Kcb mid act is estimated either from LAI or from the effective ground cover and height. After the determination of Kcb mid act , the soil evaporation coefficient, Ke , should be determined as described in Chapter 7 to obtain the crop coefficient for the mid-season stage: Kc mid act = Kcb mid act + Ke . Even where the estimated Kcb mid act is small, the total Kc mid act (= Kcb mid act + Ke ) following precipitation may sometimes be as high or higher than the Kc for pristine vegetation due to soil evaporation from among sparse vegetation. Single crop coefficient approach When the single crop coefficient Kc of Chapter 6 is used, the average effects of soil wetting are incorporated into a general mean Kc . Some guidelines for the estimation of Kc act are given in the following sections. The single crop coefficient can also be derived from the adjusted Kcb by considering the frequency of soil wetting, i.e., Kc mid act = Kcb mid act + 0.05 for infrequent wetting and Kc mid act = Kcb mid act + 0.10 for wettings of up to once a week. For more frequent wettings, the dual crop coefficient approach should be used. Alternatively,theprocedureforadjustmentforsparsevegetationdescribedbelowinthe next section can be used. Then, Kc min can be set equal to Kc ini , where Kc ini is estimated as described in Chapter 6 because Kc ini incorporates the effects of soil evaporation and, therefore, may serve as a lower limit on the estimate for Kc mid . 9.1.3 Water stress conditions Where rainfall or irrigation is low, water stress might be induced and the evapotranspiration will drop below the standard crop evapotranspiration, ETc . The reduction in the value for Kc under conditions of low soil water availability is then determined using the stress coefficient Ks as described in Chapter 8. Similarly, when water stress is induced by salinity, then Kc act is also determined using the salinity stress coefficient Ks described in Chapter 8 (Equations 8.20 and 8.25). 9.2 MID­­­-SEASONSTAGE–ADJUSTMENTFORSPARSEVEGETATION 9.2.1 Adjustment from simple field observations As a rough approximation for Kc during the mid-season stage for crops that usually nearly completely shade the soil under pristine conditions, but where cover is reduced due to disease, stress, pests, or planting density, the values for Kc mid and Kcb mid can be reduced by a factor depending on the actual vegetation development: Kc mid act = Kc mid - Acm (9.1) where Kc mid the Kc from Table 6.1 to 6.5 (Kc mid ) or 7.1 to 7.3 (Kcb mid ) after adjusting it for climate (Equation 6.16 or 7.2), Kc mid act the actual Kc or Kcb (Kc mid act or Kcb mid act ), Acm empirical adjustment factor for mid-season unusual sparse crop cover.295 9. Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation The Kc adjustment using Equation 9.1 does not apply when crops are frequently wetted and soil evaporation increases due to the reduced ground cover. Under these conditions, increased evaporation can offset reduced transpiration from the reduced ground cover and Equation 9.1 should be applied only to Kcb . The adjustment coefficient, Acm , may be estimated from the LAI ratio: LAIact 0.5 Acm

LAIdense where LAIact is the field observed actual leaf area index (Box 9.1) and LAIdense is the leaf area index expected for the same crop under normal dense conditions under standard crop management practices. BOX 9.1 Measuring and estimating LAI LAI is defined as the area of leaves per area of underlying ground surface averaged over a large area. Only one side of the leaves is counted. LAI can be measured directly by harvesting all green healthy leaves from vegetation over a measured or prescribed area, for example, 1 m2 or 10 m2 , and then measuring and summing the areas of individual leaves using photometric methods or by measuring areas of several representative leaves, averaging, and then multiplying by the total number of leaves counted. In the absence of measurements for LAIact , it can be estimated for sparse, annual vegetation as: Population a LAIact = LAIdense −−−−−−−−−−− Populationdense where LAIdense LAI for the particular plant species under normal, “dense” or pristine growing conditions. LAIdense can be obtained from various physiological sources and textbooks on crops and vegetation Population number of plants per unit area of soil surface under the actual growing conditions [No. m-2 ] Populationdense number of plants per unit area of soil surface under the “dense” or pristine growing conditions [No. m-2 ] a a = 0.5 when population is formed from vigorous growing plants; a = 1 when plants are less vigorous The 0.5 exponent in the equation simulates the tendency for vegetation to compensate for reduced stand density by increasing the size and total leaf areas for individual plants. Therefore, LAI does not fall in direct proportion to plant population. Under conditions where the plant size does not increase with reduced stand density, the “a” exponent in the equation should be set to 1 (a = 1). These latter conditions may occur wheresoilfertilityispoororwheresoilsalinity,soilwaterstress,orwaterlogginginhibit both growth and stand density, so that the growth of individual plants is retarded. The value for Kcb mid act for non-pristine or natural vegetation should be reduced when plant density and/or leaf area are lower than for full cover conditions following the procedures described with Equations 9.1 to 9.3. Where LAI can be measured, or approximated (Box 9.1), a peak Kcb mid act for natural or non-pristine agricultural vegetation can be approximated as: Kcb mid act = Kc min + (Kcb full mid - Kc min ) (1- exp [-0.7 LAI]) (9.4) where Kcb mid act estimated basal Kcb during the mid-season when plant density and/or leafareaarelowerthanforfull cover conditions,297 9. This function, introduced in Chapter 8, is: ( Yact ) ( ETc act ) 1- − −− − = Ky 1 -− −−− − (8.18bis) Ymax ETc where Yact actual yield of the crop [kg ha-1 ] when ET is ETc act , Ymax maximum (expected) yield in the absence of environmental or water stresses [kg ha-1 ] when ET is ETc , Ky yield response factor [ ], ETc potential (expected) seasonal crop evapotranspiration in the absence of water or environmental stresses (Kc ETo ) [mm],298 Crop evapotranspiration ETc act actualseasonalcropevapotranspirationasimpactedbyenvironmental or water stresses [mm] Values for Ky are reported in Table 8.7 for a wide range of crops for estimating the effect of water stress and associated reduction in ETc act on crop yield. Factors are presented there for predicting yield reductions when stress is evenly distributed throughout the growing period. Many environmental stresses, such as water shortage, salinity, low fertility and diseases, impact yield by reducing the amount of ETc act relative to the potential amount ETc . The same can be true when yields are reduced due to the use of low densities for plant populations as referred to before. Ky Ymax Ks is multiplied by Kcb or by Kc in Equation 8.1 or 8.2 to, respectively, predict Tc act or ETc act in the presence of water stress, or other environmental stresses, or for low plant populations, or poor growth and yield conditions. The ETc act predicted using Ks from Equation 9.7 provides only a very general and approximate estimate of monthly (or even seasonal) evapotranspiration, ETc act = Ks Kc ETo . Equation 9.7 works best for forage or other indeterminate crops where the value for Ky is relatively constant during the season. Equation 9.7 is generally only valid for estimating actual crop evapotranspiration for useinregionalwaterbalancestudies,forstudiesofgroundwaterdepletionandrecharge, or for estimating historical water use. The procedure is not valid for estimating ETc for daily or weekly time periods due to the very general nature of the Ky coefficient and the seasonal time scale of the crop yield. The procedures presented previously for adjusting ETc using a daily soil water balance, salinity functions, or reductions in Kc based on leaf area or fraction of ground cover are recommended over the use of Equation 9.7. Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation 9.4 ESTIMATION OF CROP COEFFICIENTS FROM GROUND COVER AND HEIGHT A popular and generally successful method for estimating Kc is to base the estimate on the fraction of ground covered by vegetation and the height of the vegetation. These two parameters are the primary characteristics of vegetation that control the rate of ETc act in terms of its ratio to the grass reference ETo . This method, referred to as the Allen and Pereira (A&P) approach, is explained hereafter in Equations 9.8-9.14. 9.4.1 Concepts For expanses of vegetation large enough that an equilibrium boundary layer is established, so that general one-dimensional equations such as the Penman-Monteith apply, a maximum upper limit on ET is established according to the law of conservation of energy. Thus, for expanses of vegetation larger than about 500 to 2 000 m2 , the Kc will have upper limits of 1.2-1.3 for the grass reference. However, under conditions of "clotheslineeffects"(wherevegetationheightexceedsthatofthesurroundings)or"oasis effects" (where vegetation has higher soil water availability than the surroundings), peak Kc may attain larger values. Caution is required when extrapolating ET measurements from small plots to large stands because the resulting ET may be overestimated. An upper energy-constrained limit on Kc is the maximum value for Kc (Kc max ) following rain or irrigation, which is governed by the amount of energy available for evaporation. The Kc max varies with climate, ranging from about 1.05 to 1.30: ( (h ) 0.3 ) Kc max

-2) - 0.004 (RHmin - 45)] −− }, {Kcb (Table) + 0.05} (9.8) 3 where u2 mean daily wind speed at 2 m height during the growth stage [m s-1 ], RHmin mean daily minimum relative humidity during the growth stage [%], Kcb (Table) standard value for Kcb for the standardized climate (Tables 7.1 to 7.3), h mean plant height (m) during the considered growth stage (initial, development, midseason, or late-season). The basal Kcb , because it represents mostly transpiration, depends upon the amount of vegetation and can be expressed as a function of a density coefficient, Kd , as: Kcb = Kc min + Kd (Kcb full - Kc min ) (9.9) where Kcb approximate value of Kcb for the considered vegetation, Kd density coefficient for the considered vegetation, Kcb full estimated basal Kcb at peak plant growth close to full ground cover, Kc min minimum basal Kcb for bare soil (0.15 under typical agricultural conditions where some subsurface soil moisture exists). This accounts for impacts of shading by vegetation when Kcb < Kcb cover due to larger stomatal conductance of the ground cover. Equation 9.9 applies to estimating Kcb during any period, namely for any crop growth stage. Kcb full can be computed as a function of mean plant height and adjusted for climate: ( (h ) 0.3 ) Kcb full = Fr min (1.0 + kh h, 1.20) + [0.04 (u2 - 2) - 0.004 (RHmin - 45)] −− (9.11) 3 where h, u2 and RHmin are defined in Equation 9.8 but herein refer to the mid-season, Fr downward adjustment to Kcb full (Fr ≤ 1.0) for impacts of reduced stomatal conductance on transpiration, kh adjustment to the crop height, Kcb full general upper limit on Kcb mid . Effects of crop height are considered through the sum (1+kh h), with kh = 0.1 for tree and vine crops as well as tall field crops, and kh = 0.2 for short crops and vegetables as described in the following sections. The value for Kcb full represents a general upper limit on Kcb mid for vegetation having full ground cover and LAI > 3 under full water supply.301 9. Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation Equation 9.11 suggests that an upper bound for Kcb full is 1.20 plus adjustment for a nonstandard climate. Equation 9.11 produces increases in Kcb full with plant height and with more arid climates. For small, isolated stand sizes, Kcb full may need to be increased beyond the value given by Equation 9.11 due to the clothesline effect. The parameter Fr applies a downward adjustment (Fr ≤ 1.0) to Kcb full and, consequently, to Kcb , if the vegetation exhibits more stomatal control on transpiration than is typical of agricultural crops. Examples of Fr are presented in the following sections. For the period when Kcb full is computed, Fr may be estimated as: ∆+γ (1+0.34u2 ) (9.12) Fr = − −− − −− − −− − − −− − −− − − −− − r1 ∆+γ (1+0.34 u2 − − −−) rtyp where rl mean leaf resistance for the vegetation under consideration [s m-1 ], rtyp typical leaf resistance representing pristine agricultural vegetation under well-watered conditions [s m-1 ], Δ slopeofthesaturationvaporpressurevs.airtemperaturecurve[kPa°C-1 ], γ psychrometric constant [kPa °C-1 ]. This factor, Fr , is multiplied against the estimate for Kcb full in Equation 9.11 to adjust its value downward when the vegetation type adopts more stomatal control on transpiration than is typical. The adjustment using Fr is particularly important in the case of perennials and the late season. The standard value for Fr is 1.0 for most annual agricultural crops under pristine conditions, whose rl value is often approximately 100 s m-1 . The value for rl is limited to ≥ 100 s m-1 . The density coefficient Kd used in Equations 9.9 and 9.10 describes the increase in Kcb with an increase in the amount of vegetation. Where estimates of the fraction of ground cover, fc , are available, the Kd is estimated as: ( ) (9.13) 1, ML fc eff ,fc eff Kd =min where fc eff effective fraction of ground covered or shaded by vegetation [0.01–1], ML multiplier on fc eff relative to the canopy density or shading [1.0 – 2.0], h mean height of the vegetation (m). fc eff isoftenobservedastheshadedareanearnoon,between11.00and15.00.Estimationof fc eff for row crops is described in Box 9.2. fc eff is often determined from visual inspection, mainly using photographic equipment. Vegetation indices, such as the normalized difference vegetation index (NDVI), can also be used to estimate fc eff by remote sensing. The ML multiplier on fc eff in Equation 9.13 imposes an upper limit on the relative magnitude of transpiration per unit of ground area as represented by fc eff and is expected to mostly range from 1.0 to 2.0, depending on the canopy density and thickness. ML may have a low value when the canopy porosity is high. The value for ML can be modified to fit the specific vegetation and experimental data. ML values for numerous crops are tabulated hereafter in the current chapter. Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation BOX 9.2 Measuring and estimating fc eff for row crops fceff shouldnormallyrepresentthefractionofthesoilsurfacethatisshadedbyvegetation. This value is generally larger than fc , the actual fraction of the soil surface that is covered by vegetation as observed from directly overhead. The amount of shading represents the amount of solar radiation intercepted by plants for potential conversion into evapotranspiration. The total fraction of shading is a function of the sun angle and the horizontal size and shape of individual plants (or rows) relative to their height. fc eff for “rectangular” shaped canopies such as most agricultural plant rows can be approximated as: HWR fc eff = fc 1+−−−−−−− ≤ 1 tan (ŋ) where fc observed fraction of soil surface that is covered by vegetation as observed from nadir (overhead) [0.01–1], HWR height to width ratio of individual plants or groups of plants when viewed from the east or from the west [ ], tan(ŋ) tangent of the mean angle of the sun, ŋ, above the horizon during the period of maximum evapotranspiration (generally between 11.00 and 15.00 hours) [ ]. For most applications, ŋ can be computed at solar noon (12.00 hours). HWR is computed as: hcanopy (cos(Γ)) HWR = −−−−−−−−− Width where hcanopy mean vertical height of the canopy area of the plant [m], Width mean width of the canopy of a plant or group of plants (e.g., row) [m], Γ angle of plant row from east-west direction [rad] (for east-west rows, Γ = 0; for north-south rows, Γ = π/2) For north-south rows, the HWR would be zero, as cos(π/2) = 0. This implies that rows of plants that run from north to south would have fc eff ≈ fc as all soil is exposed to the sun at various times of the day and as the shaded area is the same as the fraction of vegetation cover at midday. For trees or vegetation that do not have canopies that extend to the ground, hcanopy does not include the lower trunk length, but only the active canopy. Therefore, in these situations, hcanopy < h where h is mean plant height. For round or spherical shaped canopies such as trees, fc eff can be estimated as: fc fc eff = −−−−−− ≤ 1 sin (ŋ) where sin(ŋ) is the sine of the mean angle of the sun, ŋ, above the horizon during the period of maximum evapotranspiration (generally between 11.00 and 15.00).304 Crop evapotranspiration BOX 9.2 (CONT.) S S S N N N Width Width hcanopy W i d t h Mean angle of the sun above the horizon during the period of maximum evapotranspiration (η) The sine of η can be calculated for any specific time of day as: sin(η) = sin (φ) sin (δ) + cos (φ) cos (δ) cos (ω) where φ latitude [rad] (negative for southern latitudes) δ solar declination [rad] (Eq. 3.21) ω solar time angle [rad] (Eq. 3.28) Generally, fc eff can be calculated at solar noon (12.00), so that ω = 0 and the above equation reduces to: sin (η) = sin (φ) sin (δ) + cos (φ) cos (δ) The value for η can be obtained by taking the arcsine of the above equation. 9.4.2 Application Examples of application of the computations presented above are given in the following: (a)theExample9.3referstotheestimationofthemid-seasoncropcoefficientfora2mtall crop having low vegetation cover, fc = 0.30; (b) the Example 9.4 relative to an application of the A&P approach for estimation of the mid-season actual crop coefficient of tomato in conditions of reduced ground cover; and (c) the Example 9.5 relative to an application of the A&P approach to compute the mid-season actual Kcb mid for a traditional olive orchard from ground cover and height and considering effects of stomatal control.305 9. Actual crop ET (ETc act ) by agricultural and natural vegetation 9.4.3 Parameterizationofthegroundcoverfractionandheightapproach The application of the A&P approach requires that parameters fc , fraction of ground cover, and h, crop height, are observed or estimated from remote sensing, and that appropriate values for ML , representing the intensity of canopy shading and limitations on root and stem fluxes, and Fr , the effect of increased stomatal control, are adequately selected. The parameter Fr is used in the calculation of Kcb full (Eqs. 9.11 and 9.12) and ML is used in the computation of the density coefficient Kd (Equation 9.13). Both Kd and Kcb full are used when computing Kcb for any crop or natural vegetation, with or without active ground cover (Equations 9.9 and 9.10). It has been shown that Fr plays a primary role in the estimation of Kcb and Kc , particularly for trees and vines that exercise substantial control over stomatal conductance, even under well-watered conditions. To support the application of the A&P approach for most applications to compute Kcb A&P , a selection of Fr and ML values has been performed through a numerical search of parameter values that tend to cause Kcb A&P to match the tabulated Kcb values in Chapter 7 (Kcb TAB ). The procedure consists of: 1. Selecting initial values for Fr [0.25 to 1.0] and ML [1.00 to 2.00]. Fr is likely to be close to 1.0 when the crop has young and very green leaves, particularly annual crops, and close to 0.50 during the late season of deciduous woody fruit crops and annual crops that are harvested dry. ML is close to 1.00 under dry crop conditions and close to 2.0 when the crop is not stressed; in addition, ML is close to 2.0 when the canopy is less transparent to radiation. Alternatively, the parameters in Tables 9.1 to 9.4 are considered. 2. Computing crop Kcb A&P with observed/known values of fc eff and h and using the selected initial values for Fr and ML , and then comparing the resulting Kcb A&P with the target value, which is commonly the standard tabulated value (Kcb target = Kcb TAB ) for the same crop (Tables 7.1 to 7.3), or with a Kcb target < Kcb TAB when the crop is known to be cultivated somewhat far from pristine conditions. 3. Repeating the computations using an iterative numerical search where the initial Fr values are progressively changed, but constrained by the limits of Fr , while comparing the successive values of Kcb A&P with Kcb target values for the same crop, progressively decreasing the differences between both values until differences are negligible. The value for Fr needs to be progressively modified until the computed Kcb A&P effectively represents the crop under study. 4. When a Fr value is selected, the calculations are performed with the Fr value obtained in the previous calculation set, but now changing ML within its selected interval aiming at minimizing the difference between Kcb A&P and Kcb target . 5. If increasing or decreasing ML leads to increases in the difference between Kcb A&P and Kcb TAB , then the last value for ML is retained, as well as the Fr used in the calculation; however, if the difference decreases, a new run must be performed until an ML value is found that minimizes that difference. The value selected for Fr in Step 3 remains fixed throughout Steps 4 and 5, but it may be required to reapply the numerical search, focusing on Fr . 6. The procedure ends when the difference Kcb A&P - Kcb target is minimized and values for both ML and Fr selected are within the respective intervals. These values can then be usedwithEq.9.9,9.11,and9.13tocalculatevaluesforKcbmidact foranyfractionofcover. Tables 9.1 to 9.4 propose values for the parameters of the A&P approach. Tables 9.1 and 9.2 present ML and Fr that are able to produce Kcb A&P = Kcb TAB for vegetable and field crops for mid-season and end-season, i.e., for most of the crop seasons. Table 9.3 presents ML and Fr for Kcb A&P for fruit trees, shrubs and vines targeting Kcb TAB ± 5%. Table 9.4 refers to grasses. Since Kcb was not tabulated for grasses, the Kcb TAB was estimated as Kcb = Kc – 0.05 as defined in Table 7.4 and with Kc values tabulated in Table 6.4.310 Crop evapotranspiration TABLE 9.1 Parameters for the A&P approach aimed at estimating the standard Kcb for vegetable crops with presentation of the respective Kcb values Crop Degree of ground cover or field system, training, and plant density Crop stages Observation parameters Calibrated parameters Standard Kcb fc eff h (m) ML Fr a.
