ETc act under various crop management practices ETc act under various crop management practices This chapter discusses various types of factors that may cause the values for Kc act and ETc act to deviate from the standard Kc and Kcb values described in Chapters 6 and 7. These factors refer to the effects of surface mulches, intercropping, small areas of vegetation and estimation of Kc or Kcb for periods under conditions of frozen soil as well under non-active cover. This chapter is intended to serve as a resource for situations where cultivation practices are known to deviate from those assumed in Tables 6.1 to 6.4 and 7.1 to 7.3 and where estimates of Kc act and ETc act are still necessary. This chapter is by no means exhaustive; its intent is to show some of the procedures that can be used to make adjustments to Kc to account for deviations from standard conditions. 10.1 EFFECTS OF SURFACE MULCHES Mulches are frequently used in vegetable production to reduce evaporation losses from the soil surface, to accelerate crop development in cool climates by increasing soil temperature, to reduce erosion, or to assist in weed control. Mulches may be composed of organic plant materials, or they may consist of plastic sheets or films. 10.1.1 Plastic mulches Plastic mulches generally consist of thin sheets of polyethylene, or a similar material placed over the ground surface, especially along the plant rows. Holes are cut into the plastic film at plant spacings to allow the plant vegetation to emerge. Plastic mulches can be transparent, white, or black. Colour influences albedo, mainly during the early stages of the crop. However, as the intention is to use a simple procedure for adjusting Kc for mulched crops, only generalized recommendations are given here. The published literature offers contradictory information on impacts of plastic mulches on Kc and ET. However, it appears likely that black plastic increases the soil temperature more than transparent plastic, so that transparent plastic may cause slightly smaller differences in Kc relative to not adopting any type of mulching. Plastic mulches can substantially reduce the evaporation of water from the soil surface. The impact of plastic mulch is likely more evident under trickle irrigation systems. Associated with the reduction in evaporation, however, there may occur a general increase in transpiration from the vegetation caused by the transfer of both sensible and radiative heat from the surface of the plastic cover to adjacent vegetation. Even though the transpiration rates under mulch may increase by an average of 10-30 percent over the season as compared to no mulch, the Kc values may decrease by an average of 10-30 percent due to a 30-80 percent reduction in soil evaporation. Ranges in observed reductions in Kc are given in Table 10.1 for three groups of crops: field crops referring to cereals, cotton and sugar cane; vegetable crops comprised of various species, and fruit tree and vine crops, namely apricot and pear orchards, and grape vineyards. Table 10.2 presents the percentage of reduction Kc in relation to the type of mulch which complements the information provided in Table 10.1. Chapter 10330 Crop evapotranspiration TABLE 10.1 Approximate reductions (%) in Kc for various field and horticultural crops under nearly full plasticmulchandorganicmulchrelativetousingnomulchwhenirrigationisbytrickleirrigation Crop Early vegetative stages (%) Mid-season (%) Late and end season (%) Notes Field crops (maize, sorghum, wheat, okra, peanut, etc.) Cotton Sugar cane Vegetables (banana, cucumber, curcuma, onion, tomato, etc.) Fruit tree and vine crops (apricot and pear orchards, and grapes vineyards) 5-25 15-35 15-35 10-35 15-35 5-10 5-10 10-15 5-10 10-25 5-10 5-10 10-15 5-10 10-25 Plastic films Plastic and straw Organic mulch Plastic and straw Both plastic and organic mulche TABLE 10.2 Approximate reductions (%) of soil evaporation (Es ) and crop coefficients (Kc ) of straw and plastic film mulch relative to no mulch conditions Parameter reduction Mulch material Early stages (%) Mid-season (%) Late & end season (%) Es Straw Plastic film 30-42 38-58 26-36 31-51 19-36 25-34 Kc Straw Plastic film Black Transparent White 14-34 15-35 23-43 15-24 27-33 8-17 9-20 12-19 9-22 -* 8-17 5-19 12-20 3-16 22-25 * Not given due to limited data available. Literature has reported differences between mulch and no-mulch Kc that vary widely for the same types of crops, even for the same crops. This variability mainly relates to density of planting and cultivar and types with those producing more shading causing smaller differences. Variability is also created by measurement errors and biases. Soil texture and management impact reductions in Kc since soil surface conditions may or not favour evaporation losses. The latter vary according to the stages of development and amount of shading of the soil surface. It has been possible to reduce the wide ranges in impacts by grouping crops by stage since it is always observed that reductions in Kc are greater during the initial crop stage and during the first part of the vegetative growth stage, or the pre-anthesis stages, i.e., when ground cover fc is smaller. Less mulching effect on decreasing Kc occurs by the start of mid-season since it is during that time that ground cover and fc are higher, thus allowing less solar energy to reach the soil surface. By the end of season and during the second half of the late season, differences in the Kc value slightly increase again, especially when the soil is less shaded during this period. Users are therefore advised to divide the crop season into three time-periods when estimating reductions in Kc due to mulching as indicated in Table 10.1 and 10.2. The smaller values of Kc reduction often refer to black plastic mulch because it has a low albedo, thus providing more energy for soil evaporation under mulch, thus contributing to a convergence of the Kc for mulched and non-mulched surfaces. Contrarily, mulches with transparent films likely tend to create large differences in Kc . However, users will be informed with better detail when searching in bibliographies.331 10. ETc act under various crop management practices When estimating basal Kcb for mulched crops, less adjustment is normally needed to the Kcb curve, likely being half the order of reduction in overall Kc , as it is generally understood that the “basal” evaporation of water from the soil surface that is associated with the standard Kcb value is less with a plastic mulch, though the transpiration is likely increased. The effect on Kcb can be greater with low density crops, e.g. with tree orchards and vine crops. Local calibration of Kcb (and Kc ) for use with mulch is encouraged, namely using soil water balance models and computing soil evaporation Ke . Where calculating the soil evaporation coefficient Ke with plastic mulch (Example 10.1), the fw should represent the relative equivalent fraction of the ground surface that can contribute to evaporation through the vent holes in the plastic cover and to the fraction of surface that is wetted and is not covered by the mulch. The effective area of vent holes is normally two to four times the physical area of the vents (or even higher) to account for vapour transfer from under the sheet. EXAMPLE 10.1 Effects of surface mulch A plastic mulch is placed over cucumbers under drip irrigation. The mulch is clear plastic covering the entire field surface, with small openings at each plant. Adjust both the single and basal Kc values for this crop to reflect the presence of the mulch. From Table 6.1, Kc ini , Kc mid and Kc end for fresh market cucumbers have values equal

As the plastic mulch is continuous with only small vents at each plant, the Kc ini is assumed to be only 0.10 (this value should be adjusted upward if precipitation occurs). The Kc mid and Kc end values are estimated as: Kc mid = 0.85 (1.0) = 0.85 Kc end = 0.85 (0.75) = 0.64 where the 0.85 multipliers are derived from Table 10.1 and reflect an assumed 15 percent reduction in ETc due to the mulch, assuming an approximately weekly irrigation frequency. From Table 7.1, the values for Kcb ini , Kcb mid , and Kcb end are 0.15, 0.95 and 0.70 for this same cucumber crop. The Kcb ini is assumed to be similar to the Kc ini for mulched cover and is therefore set equal to 0.10. The Kcb mid and Kcb end values are estimated to be reduced by near 10 percent so that: Kcb mid = 0.90 (0.95) = 0.85 Kcb end = 0.90 (0.7) = 0.63 These basal values are similar to the adjusted values for Kc . This is expected as evaporation from the mulch covered surface can be ignored. Additional adjustment to these Kc values to account for climate is necessary using Eq. 6.16 and 7.1. The values for mean Kc and Kcb are similar with values of 0.10 for the initial stage, 0.85 for the mid-season stage and 0.64 at the end of the late season stage.332 Crop evapotranspiration 10.1.2 Organic mulches Effects of organic mulches are considered through the differences between mulched and non-mulched Kc as in Table 10.1 and 10.2. Organic mulches are often used with orchard production and with row crops under reduced tillage operations. Organic mulches may consist of unincorporated plant residues or foreign material imported to the field such as straw. The depth of the organic mulch and the fraction of the soil surface covered can vary widely. These two parameters will affect the amount of reduction in evaporation from the soil surface, with Kc differences increasing when larger amounts of mulch are used, namely straw mulch, as well as when the percentage of ground coverage is larger. A general rule when applying Kc from Tables 6.1, 6.2 and 6.3, and when applying the dual Kcb + Ke method, is to reduce the amount of soil water evaporation by about 5 percent for each 10 percent of soil surface that is effectively covered by an organic mulch. For example, if 50 percent of the soil surface were covered by an organic crop mulch of high density, then the soil evaporation would be reduced by about 25 percent. In the case of Kc ini , which represents mostly evaporation from soil, one would reduce Kc ini by about 25 percent. For less dense mulches, this percentage should be reduced by 5 or 10 percent. In the cases of Kc mid and Kc end , one may reduce these values by 25 percent of the difference between (Kc mid − Kcb mid ) and (Kc end − Kcb end ) from Tables 6.1, 6.2 and 6.3 and Tables 7.1, 7.2 and 7.3. Generally, the differences between values in these Tables are only 5 - 10 percent for mid- and late-season, so that the adjustment to Kc mid and Kc end to account for an organic mulch may not be very large. However, one may instead consider utilizing information in Table 10.1 and 10.2. When applying the dual crop coefficient approach (Kc = Kcb + Ke ) with a separate water balance of the surface soil layer, the magnitude of the evaporation component (Ke ETo ) should be reduced by about 5 percent for each 10 percent of soil surface covered by the organic mulch. Kcb is not changed. These recommendations are only approximate and attempt to account for the effects of partial reflection of solar radiation from residue, micro advection of heat from residue into the soil, lateral movement of soil water from below residue to exposed soil, and the insulating effect of the organic cover. As these parameters can vary widely, local observations and measurements are required if more precise estimates are required. 10.2 INTERCROPPING Intercropping refers to the situation where two different crops are grown together within one field. For the estimation of the crop coefficient, a distinction is made between (see Figure 10.1): • contiguous vegetation, where the canopies of the two crops intermingle at some height (e.g. corn and beans intercropping); • overlapping crops, where the canopy of one crop is well above that of the other so that the canopies cannot be considered to be contiguous (e.g. date trees overlapping pomegranate trees at an oasis); and • border crops, where tall crops such as windbreaks border fields of shorter crops, or high trees border a field crop.333 10. ETc act under various crop management practices There is an upper limit to the energy available to evaporate water. This is represented by Kc max (Equation 7.4 bis) for all crops in cultivated fields larger than 3 ha: ({ (h ) 0.3 }{Kcb +0.05}) (7.4 bis) Kc max =max 1.2 + [0.04 (u2 -2) - 0.004 (RHmin -45)] −−− 3 where h is the height for the taller crop. Under all conditions when combining crop coefficients for multiple crops, Kc should be constrained by this upper bound (Kc ≤ Kc max ). FIGURE 10.1 Different situations of intercropping: contiguous referring to two field crops, e.g. corn and beans; overlapping when tall trees and short trees are intercropped, e.g. date palm trees overlapping pomegranate trees at an oasis; and border crop tall tree crops are planted on the border of a field, e.g. date palm bordering alfalfa in a oasis, or when windbreaks protect horticultural crops overlapping border crop contiguous Source: Authors’ own elaboration 10.2.1 Contiguous vegetation, single Kc Where the taller crop has canopy foliage that extends down to the same elevation as that of the top of the shorter crop, the vegetation canopy can be considered to be contiguous. For example, in Africa and South America, maize and beans are frequently intercropped as contiguous vegetation, with one row of maize planted per one or more rows of beans. Another example is the cultivation of five to seven rows of wheat intercropped with three rows of maize in many areas of China. Case of single Kc for similar fractions of ground covered by both crops Where the leaf area or fraction of ground covered by the vegetation (fc ) is similar for each crop, the Kc in Tables 6.1 to 6.3 and Tables 7.1 to 7.3 for the taller crop (if this Kc is higher) can be taken to represent the entire field. The taller crop will act in some sense as a clothesline so that Kc (and ETc ) for the taller crop per unit of ground area is increased over that given in Tables 6.1 to 6.3 and Tables 7.1 to 7.3. However, the Kc (and ETc ) for the shorter crop will be reduced due to the windbreak effect by the taller crop. As a result, the Kc for the field as a whole may be similar to the weighted average of the Kc values for the two crops from Tables 6.1 to 6.3 and Tables 7.1 to 7.3, or, the total Kc may more closely follow the Kc predicted for a field sown entirely to the taller crop (Kc field ≈ Kc taller crop ). Yields for the shorter crop may be reduced relative to those for single cultivar production due to the effects of shading by the taller crop and the competition for soil water.334 Crop evapotranspiration Case of single Kc for different fractions of ground covered by both crops Where the fractions of ground covered by each crop are different, the Kc for an intercropped field can be estimated by weighting the Kc values for the individual crops according to the fraction of area covered by each crop and by the height of the crop: f1 h1 Kc1 + f2 h2 Kc2 Kc field

f1 h1 + f2 h2 where f1 and f2 are the fractions of the field surface planted to crops 1 and 2, h1 and h2 are the heights of crops 1 and 2, and Kc1 and Kc2 are the Kc values for crops 1 and 2. The crop coefficient for the mid-season and the entire field is 1.15. 10.2.2 When adopting the dual crop coefficient for relay intercropping Relay intercropping describes a cropping pattern in which the lifecycle of one crop may only partially overlap that of the other crop. As a result, the timing of the midseason periods can be displaced between the crops, as shown in Figure 10.2, and the Kcb values may need to be calculated daily. The A&P approach (Chapter 9) can be used to estimate Kcb for relay intercropped crops by following the progression in the density of vegetation for each crop. The daily evolution of Kcb is estimated using the density coefficient, Kd , however modified from Equation 9.13. Since the intercropped crops will usually have different characteristics, it is necessary to know the fraction of the soil surface covered by each crop (Frac) and the height (h) of each crop. It is assumed that in the total crop cycle one crop is dominant (subscript dom) while the other is considered subordinate (subscript sub). The Kcb of the intercrop (Kcb inter ) is estimated daily or weekly (Figure 10.2) as: Kcb inter = max [Kcb sub + Kd dom (Kcb dom - Kcb sub ); Kcb dom + Kd sub (Kcb sub - Kcb dom )] (10.2) where Kcb dom Kcb of the dominant crop when grown/cultivated alone [-], Kcb sub Kcb of the subordinated crop when grown/cultivated alone [-],335 10. This is the case, for example in southern California, where citrus trees are planted in tall date palm groves. Where a normal dense spacing is used for both the dates and for the citrus trees, the Kc may increase as the density of the combined vegetation increases, proportional to the increase in LAI (Example 10.3), with maximum Kc constrained by either Kc max (Equation 7.4 or 9.8) or by Kcb full (Equations 9.11) unless the total field area is small so that there is an additional clothesline or oasis effect. EXAMPLE 10.3 Overlapping vegetation A 20 ha date palm grove in Palm Desert, California, the United States has a tree spacing of 6 m. Interplanted among the rows of palms are small orange trees (50 percent canopy) on a 6 m spacing. The palm and citrus trees are 3 m from one another in the rows. Height of the palms is 10 m and height of the citrus is 3 m. The canopy foliage of the palms is well above that of the citrus so that the canopies cannot be considered as contiguous. Mean average RHmin during the mid-season is 20 percent and u2 = 2 m s-1 . The Kc mid from Table 6.3 for dates is 0.95 and when adjusted for humidity and wind using Eq. 6.16 is Kc mid = 1.09. The Kc mid from Table 6.3 for citrus having 50 percent canopy with no ground cover is 0.70 and when adjusted for humidity and wind using Eq. 6.16 is Kc mid = 0.80. The interplanting of citrus among the date palms has increased the total leaf area of the orchard so that ETc for the combined planting (palms and citrus together) will be greater than for either planting alone. The estimated combined Kc mid will be estimated as a function of the increase in total LAI. First the LAI values of the individual plantings are estimated by inverting Eq. 9.4 to solve for LAI: Kcb -Kc min LAI = - 1.4 ln 1 - −−−−−−−−−−− Kcb full -Kc min where Kc min is the minimum basal Kc for bare soil (≈ 0.15 to 0.20) and Kcb full is the maximum mid-season Kc expected for the crop if there were complete ground cover, calculated using Eq. 9.4. Based on Eq. 9.4, with h = 10 m for the date palms and h = 3 m for the citrus, the Kcb full values for the two crops, assuming complete ground cover for each, are Kcb full = 1.34 for palms and Kcb full = 1.30 for citrus (using RHmin = 20% and u2 = 2 m s-1 ). These estimates ignore effects of any unique stomatal control (Fr = 1.0). Under these conditions, the Kc is weighted according to the areas for each crop. However, prior to the weighting, the Kc for the border crop, if taller than the field (interior) crop, should be adjusted for a potential clothesline impact. 10.5 Kc FROM SMALL AREAS OF VEGETATION Natural vegetation and some subsistence agriculture frequently occurs as small groups or stands of plants. The value for Kc for these small stands depends on the type and condition of other vegetation surrounding the small stand. Inthemajorityofcasesfornaturalvegetationorfornon-pristineagriculturalvegetation, thevalueforKc mustadheretoupperlimitsforKc ofapproximately1.20-1.30,whenthe areal expanse of the vegetation is larger than about 2 000 m2 . This is required because ET from large areas of vegetation is governed by one-dimensional energy exchange principles and by the principle of conservation of energy. ET from small stands (< 2 000 m2 ) may be impacted by advected energy from beyond the stand and will adhere to these same principles and limits only where the vegetation height, leaf area, and soil water availability are similar to that of the surrounding vegetation. 10.6 Kc DURING NON-GROWING PERIODS This section describes procedures for estimating ETc and crop coefficients during non-growing periods. Non-growing periods are the periods during which the crop is dormant.Generally,thenon-growingperiodsrefertoperiodsoffrostandcontinuously frozen conditions in case of annual crops and to the dormancy periods in the case of perennials, namely the evergreen trees and shrubs.340 Crop evapotranspiration 10.6.1 Annual winter crops The type and condition of the ground surface during non-growing periods largely influences the range for ETc . Where the surface is covered by nearly dead vegetation, or some type of organic mulch or crop residue, then the Kc will be similar to that for agriculture that uses a surface mulch. Where the surface is covered by weed growth or growth of “volunteer” plants, then the Kc will vary according to the leaf area or fraction of ground covered by the vegetation and by the availability of soil water. Where the surface is snow covered or frozen, then the Kc is difficult to predict and a constant value for Kc may have to be assumed (Box 10.4). Where the ground is left mostly bare following harvest, then the Kc following harvest will be strongly influenced by the frequency and amount of precipitation or irrigation when a post-harvest leaching irrigation is applied. Kc for bare soil may be estimated as Kc = Kc ini where Kc ini is computed using the procedure proposed in Chapter 6. However, as for the wheat case in North China Plain (Box 10.4), if the post-harvest period is wet and Kc is then high, a Kc n-gro of around 0.25-0.50 may be selected. Where a daily soil water balance is applied, the user may elect to apply the dual Kcb approach (Chapter 7), preferably using a simulation computer model. If the topsoil layer dries to very low water contents during periods having no precipitation, the daily water balance calculation with Kcb not far from 0 will generally provide accurate estimates of ETc during the non-growing periods. BOX 10.3 Case study of the estimation of basal and single crop coefficients for the non-growing period of a winter wheat crop under frozen soil conditions at Daxing, North China Plain South of Beijing Results of applying the dual crop coefficient approach (Kcb + Ke ) to a winter wheat crop cultivated at Daxing, North China Plain, are briefly analyzed. The observed ETc act data calculated with the soil water balance model SIMDualKc using soil water content measurements, and soil evaporation data measured with micro-lysimeters. Figure 10.4 shows the Kcb and Ke curves as well as the mean averaged Kc act for the entire crop season. Kcb act coincided with potential because the crop was not stressed. Crop sowing was performed by early Fall, the 9th October, when the soil was wet by a previous large irrigation, and the non-growing period started when soil frozen initiated, by the 8th December. The development of the crop was quite limited due to weather conditions typical of a cold autumn. The soil kept frozen, with a few quite small snow events, and subject to freezing and thawing early December and late February. By 26 February, freezing in the root zone ended and the crop started to revive. The crop growth stage initiated then until the start of the mid-season in April. The Kcb curve resulting from the calibration of the model shows a constant value equal to Kcb ini until reviving, i.e. a Kcb n-grow = Kcb ini . Differently, the actual single crop coefficient before the non-growing period, Kc act ini , was 0.95 due to stored soil water after the winter leaching irrigation. Resulting from averaging Kc n-grow computed in various years of observations and from the simulate Ke curve, the value Kc n-grow = 0.50 was assumed. However, this value could be different if the Kc act curve did not fall vertically but was an inclined straight line for the transition between the antecedent and the non-growing period.341 10. ETc act under various crop management practices FIGURE 10.4 Basal crop coefficient curve (full green line), evaporation coefficient curve (dotted brown line), and average actual single crop coefficient (thick full red line) for a basin irrigated winter wheat crop at Daxing, North China Plain 09/10 29/10 18/11 08/12 28/12 17/01 06/02 26/02 18/03 07/04 27/04 17/05 06/06 Crop coefficients 1.2 1.1 1.0 0.9 0.8 0.7 0.6 0.5 0.4 0.3 0.2 0.1 0.0 200 180 160 140 120 100 80 60 40 20 0 Precipitation, irrigation (mm) Non-growing period (frozen soil) Source: Authors’ own elaboration Where the ground surface has a plant residue or other dead organic mulch cover, or where part of the unharvested crop remains on the surface in a dead or senesced condition, then the surface will respond similarly to a surface covered by mulch. In this case, Kc can be set equal to Kc ini but the value for Kc ini can be reduced by about 5 percent for each 10 percent of soil surface that is effectively covered by an organic mulch. Evaporation from dead, wet vegetation can be substantial for a few days following a precipitation event. Therefore, in the dual Kc approach, the value for fc should be set close to zero to reflect the lack of green cover and fw should be set equal to 1.0 to reflect the wetting of both soil and mulch cover by precipitation. The dead mulch or vegetation will dry more quickly than would the underlying soil if it were exposed. The soil will be protected somewhat from evaporation by the dead mulch or vegetation cover. Therefore, total evaporation losses will be less than the predicted TEW. This can be accounted for by reducing the value for TEW by 5 percent for each 10 percent of soil surface that is effectively covered by an organic mulch or vegetation residues. The value for REW should be limited to REW ≤ TEW. Where the ground surface is snow covered or frozen, any vegetation will be largely non-responsive and non-contributing to ETc , and the amount of ETc will be closely related to the availability of free water at the surface and to the albedo of the surface. The albedo of snow-covered surfaces can range from 0.40 for old, dirty snow cover, to 0.90 for fresh, dry snow. Therefore, the ETc for snow-cover will be less than ETo for grass, as 25-85 percent less shortwave energy is available. In addition, some energy must be used to melt the snow before evaporation. Given the limited value of ETo (or even ETp ) under snow-covered or frozen conditions, a single, average value may be best used to predict ETc as for the example in Box 10.2 relative to winter wheat in North China Plain. The soil is deeply wet there because a large pre-sowing irrigation is applied for leaching of salts. Therefore, the period342 Crop evapotranspiration antecedent to the non-growing period has Kc much larger than Kc n-gro . The user may select different Kc transitions between previous to and during the non-growing period, decreasing for a few days or abrupt like represented in Box 10.2. For winter periods at Kimberly, Idaho, the United States, it was found that ETc averaged 1 mm day-1 over nearly six month long winters (1 October to 30 March). Over the six-year study period, the ground was 50 percent covered by snow for 25 percent of the time. The ground, when exposed, was frozen about 50 percent of the time. The Kc averaged 0.25 during periods when the soil was not frozen but where frosts were occurring (October and early November). When the ground had 50 percent snow cover or greater, the ETc averaged only 0.4 mm day-1 . Over the six-month non-growing period, total cumulative ETc exceeded precipitation by about 50 mm. The ETc /Rso ratio averaged only 0.17 over the six-month period, and averaged 0.11 from 1 Dec. – 10 Mar. The ETc /Rs ratio averaged 0.23 over the six-month period, and averaged 0.15 from 1 Dec. – 10 Mar. A similar study conducted in Logan, Utah, the United States (latitude 41.6°N, elevation 1 350 m) over an eight-year period showed that ETc varied widely with soil surface wetness and air temperature during the winter months. The “average” Kc from November to March was 0.5 for days having no snow cover. For days with snow cover, “ETc ” ranged from 0 to 1.5 mm day-1 . 10.6.2 Evergreen trees and shrubs The period of dormancy of evergreen trees and shrubs generally has a Kc behaviour similar to annual crops where the soil is not frozen, or snow covered. The use of a soil water balance simulation model eases computations and should increase the accuracy of estimates when LAI and fc inputs are adequate. Snow or soil freezing do not occur there but most ofprecipitationoccursduringthenon-growingperiod.Therefore,theKc andKcb curves show a behaviour resulting from the opposed rainfall distribution in the growing and non-growing periods. The olive orchard is mostly supplied by irrigation since rainfall is very short during the growing period. The occurrence of precipitation and irrigation events is shown in the Figure 10.5, includes the out of season and dormant period. All irrigation wetting events occurred in the mid-season stage when precipitation and soil water storage were not sufficient to maintain the crop transpiration with the actual Kcb curve below the full line representing the potential Kcb curve. The soil evaporation Ke curve peaked after wetting events by rain or irrigation. It is important to note that even though values for Kc may be higher during the dormant period than during the growing period, the ET rates may typically be much lower during the dormant period as compared to during the growing period in mid- to high-latitudes because ETo during the dormant periods is typically much lower than during midseason months.343 10. ETc act under various crop management practices FIGURE 10.5 Potential basal crop coefficient curve (full green line), actual Kcb curve (dashed green line), actual single averaged Kc (thick red full line) and soil evaporation coefficient curve (dotted line), for a drip irrigated hedgerow olive orchard at Viana do Alentejo, Portugal. Vertical full lines denote precipitation or irrigation events Crop coefficients 1.2 1.1 1.0 0.9 0.8 0.7 0.6 0.5 0.4 0.3 0.2 0.1 0.0 25/11 25/12 25/01 25/02 25/03 25/04 25/05 25/06 25/07 25/08 25/09 25/10 25/11 25/12 160 140 120 100 80 60 40 20 0 Precipitation, irrigation (mm) Growing period Non-growing period Non-growing period Source: Authors’ own elaboration It is important that the Kc for vegetation during the non-growing period be limited according to the amount of soil water available to supply evapotranspiration. Otherwise, the law of conservation of mass will be violated. Under all conditions, the integration of Kc ETo over the course of the non-growing period cannot exceed the sum of the precipitation occurring during the period plus any residual soil water in the root zone following harvest that can be depleted by the subsequent vegetation. The root zone in this case is the root zone for the weed or volunteer crops. A daily soil water balance may provide for the best estimate of soil water induced stress and associated reduction in Kc and ETc namely when a simulation model is used. The soil water balances will automatically adhere to the law of conservation of mass, so that total ETc from the weed or volunteer vegetation will not be overestimated. During non-growing periods, weeds may germinate and grow. This vegetation is supplied with water from storage in the soil profile and from rainfall as it is the case of the olive orchard in a Mediterranean climate in Box 10.5. The amount of ground surface covered by vegetation will vary with soil management, e.g. the frequency and extent of soil tillage, and the availability of soil water and rain. If the single crop coefficient approach has to used in water management, the value for Kcb determined using procedures in Chapter 9 can be converted into an equivalent Kc by adding 0.05 to 0.15 according to the frequency of rainfall and surface wetting.344 Crop evapotranspiration 10.7 STRATEGIES, METHODS AND TECHNOLOGIES TO ACHIEVE WATER SAVINGS In recent decades, research has produced a variety of strategies, methods, and technologies to reduce non-beneficial water use on farms for adaptation to the rising occurrence of water scarcity, agricultural droughts, and multi-sectoral rivalry for water, all which are increased by climate change. Adopting consumptive water-saving practices (WSET ), i.e. that refer to ETc act requires accurate determination of crop water requirements using the FAO56 methods (Kc ETo ) under various water availability and management techniques, as described in the previous chapters. However, the non-consumptive water-saving practices (WSDP+RO ), that refer to controlling deep percolation (DP) and runoff (RO) at farm and off-farm, generally originate WSDP+RO > WSET . Both DP and RO refer to excess irrigation water applied relative to ETc and to limitations of the irrigation technologies and of the irrigators. In fact, water-saving practices must base not only on well-known net crop irrigation requirements but also on the availability of appropriate irrigation technologies and management. This section, therefore, outlines ideas and methods for reducing non-beneficial consumptive and non-consumptive water use, mainly referring to ET, DP and RO (NBWUET and NBWUDP+RO ) while maintaining high crop water productivity on farms. These achievements tipically require maintaining high levels of water use for transpiration and managing the irrigation systems targeting ETc . In particular, this section describes water-saving routines linked to irrigation scheduling and management for irrigation systems, crop and soil management, mulching, remote sensing techniques, plant conditioners, and micro-climate adaptation. Controlling non-beneficial off farm water use and non-consumptive farm water use imply different management and technological issues not dealt with in FAO56 but that are closely associated with the on-farm water management techniques and practices previously referred to and described hereafter. Off-farm reductions in water consumption include the adoption of modernized irrigation water collection, transport and distribution to farms and fields, and the use of automation for improving those related functions not described herein. All of these practices require an assessment of the local and regional hydrology to determine their overall impacts on reductions in water use. It is important to distinguish between water “use” and water “consumption” when assessing impacts of irrigation on the fresh water resource. Water consumption is the conversion of liquid water to vapour via the ET process, while the term water use describes the total extraction of water from the environment and used as irrigation water. Wateruseincludesboththeconsumedpartoftheextractedwaterandthenon-consumed part. In other words, water use consists of the consumptive use and the non-consumed part, that remains on the land surface but does not infiltrate into the irrigated field, and that stored below the surface. The first is runoff and the second is deep percolation as referred to before. If the non-consumed component flows back to a surface stream or to an underlying aquifer, then it remains as part of the freshwater system and is available for later reuse. However, if the non-consumed component flows into a saline sink such as the ocean or brackish ground water, then it is no longer part of the freshwater system and is considered to be consumed. What is commonly considered to be “water savings’ is the difference between the common total water use, beneficial or not, and the water use when the irrigator manages water to avoid excess ET, or DP or RO.345 10. ETc act under various crop management practices 10.7.1 Water-saving of consumptive water use (WSET ) Irrigation scheduling and management The main aim of irrigation management and scheduling may differ from an area to another or from a farm to another depending on the WS purpose. The aim of the farmer is to maximize the net economic benefit by increasing yield and/or reducing costs. This may often be achieved by maximizing the beneficial consumption water use (ETc act ) and actual yield (Ya ). As there is a strong relation between crop transpiration (Tc ) and CO2 assimilation (gas exchange through stomata), higher Ya is achieved at the expense of more water transpired, mainly when aiming at little or no water stress. Chapter 8 develops the bases for irrigation scheduling and describes yield-water computations. Practical issues for determining crop coefficients under field practices and management are dealt with in Chapter 9. Under conditions of water scarcity, the objective of irrigation management may be to optimize crop water productivity (WPET ), i.e. the marketable yield per unit of water consumed if such a solution is economically appropriate. This involves knowing the economic water productivity ratio (EWPR), which is the ratio of the value of the marketable yield to the unit cost of water applied. In some instances, the goal of maximizing the economics of production may be achieved by inducing controlled crop water stress to reduce vegetative growth and to optimize the quality of marketable yields, including eustress as previously defined (“good stress”), especially in some fruit tree crops and vineyards. To reduce water consumption and optimize global WP and farm returns, an irrigation schedule must be planned, where such a plan may or not include deficit irrigation, depending upon the crop and water availability. For annual crops, optimizing WP must include selection of the best planting date according to the local climate and/or the foreseen weather conditions ahead of planting. That selection requires knowledge of the relationships between evapotranspiration or transpiration deficit and yield decrease (Equations 8.18 and 8.19, and Chapters 8 and 9). Using irrigation scheduling modelling, irrigation events may be planned using short and medium range weather forecasts, which are produced by various international organizations, e.g. ECMWF, the European Centre for Medium-Range Weather Forecasts, WAMIS, the World AgroMeteorological Information Service, or the Weather Prediction Center, USA National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration. Irrigation scheduling with the FAO56 methodologies requires appropriate selection of Kc and Kcb values tabulated in Chapters 6 and 7, and revised in their application depending on various specific conditions including planting density and water and salinity stressors as discussed in Chapters 8 and 9, as well as in the previous sections of this current chapter. Assuming that the tabulated Kc and Kcb are likely representing the upper maximum or near-maximum values since they refer to pristine conditions, those values may be difficult or unreasonable to achieve in common practice where it is essential to achieve water savings. The same upper limits apply to the field attainment of crop coefficients: Tabulated Kc and Kcb should represent reference values relative to those observed from field data and can be useful to verify data quality and data handling approaches, and thus to question results when locally observed values are well above or well below the tabulated values. Limited deficit irrigation (DI) is specifically introduced into the irrigation practice to allow some degree of crop water stress and Ya reduction, with a goal to reduce soil evaporation (Es ) and water and chemical losses, whilst maintaining transpiration at levels that produce economically viable yields. Deficit irrigation can be considered to346 Crop evapotranspiration be a water-saving strategy where soil water depletion in the root zone goes below the optimum yield threshold and therefore transpiration decreases. Irrigation is applied up to a certain level below field capacity so that occasional rainfall can replenish the soil water storage capacity. Less frequent irrigation can reduce evaporation from the soil surface. However, the uneven distribution of rainfall and variable weather conditions pose a risk that may lead to Ya losses larger than desired or planned. To be a sustainable practice, reduction of water consumption in deficit irrigation should be proportionally higher than the reduction in Ya , so that WPET (kg m-3 ) is optimized, and EWPR is always positive (above the economic feasibility threshold, EWPR>1.0). Published research has provided inconsistent evidence on performance of deficit irrigation as a water-saving and economic strategy. In general, during years of water shortage, farmers may choose to adopt deficit irrigation schedules for their less profitable and more water-stress tolerant crops. To be economically viable, the cost of reduction of water use should be higher than the cost of reduction of the market value of Ya . This may be difficult to achieve or manage, depending on the degree of deficits. Therefore, deficit irrigation is not always the preferred option by farmers due to the increased risk of both Ya and monetary losses. Reducing the irrigated area may be a preferred solution, e.g. decreasing the irrigable area having annual crops in favour of watering of perennial crops. However, exceptions include the adoption of eustress in perennial crops where lower Ya results in better quality fruit and higher profits, as for grapevine where beneficial effects such as decreased berry size or increased sugar content may lead to great benefit since wine quality may be improved. Deficit irrigation can be implemented by applying a fixed water reduction strategy throughout the cropping season or targeted to some non-critical phenological stages in relation to water stress. Two main types of DI may be considered: • regulated deficit irrigation (RDI) that targets certain phenological stages during which plants are less sensitive to water stress; • partial root-zone drying (PRD), mostly used in fruit trees and vine crops, which targets the production of the plant hormone abscisic acid (ABA), which reduces leaf expansion and stomatal conductance by stressing one portion of the roots, whilst the other portion of the roots sustains transpiration. Nevertheless, published research findings on RDI and PRD are not always consistent. Some results suggest that deficit irrigation and associated practices such as RDI and PRD may only be acceptable in reducing capital and operating costs of irrigation and to improve yield quality when carried out with using precision irrigation (timing, amount and placement) based on the knowledge of crop responses to water stress, which vary with crop and, often, with cultivar. Consequently, water-yield models, such as that described in Chapter 8, are often crop and crop cultivar specific. Deficitirrigationisahighlycomplexpracticethatinvolvesmanyvariables.Itsapplication must focus on overcoming the uncertainty of outcomes. In general, the applicability of deficit irrigation depends on site-specific conditions, crop types and related cultivars, and climatic variables (e.g., rainfall distribution and reference ET levels). Given the large variety of conditions and factors affecting crop water demand, yields and economic return, optimization modelling and decision-support tools are useful aids to design the most appropriate strategies for specific sites and for specific crops. Among such models are those used to derive various Kc and Kcb values tabulated in Chapters 6 and 7, e.g. CROPWAT, SIMDualKc and AquaCrop. These tools allow one to consider diverse criteria, primarily economic (maximizing profits), and select the irrigation scheduling and management issues that better lead to water-savings and to control environmental347 10. ETc act under various crop management practices impacts (minimizing leaching of nitrates and chemicals, controlling groundwater depletion and quality degradation). The FAO56 approach is used in such modelling applications and may be able to determine the effects of deficit irrigation on crop yield, non-beneficial crop water use, WPET and EWPR. Crop and soil management The planting date of annual crops likely impacts the amounts of beneficial and non beneficial crop water use, namely the consumptive use, due to differences in responses to climatic factors, mainly the timing and distribution of rainfall. Anticipating or delaying planting dates is commonly done in relation to soil conditions for seeding and planting machines to move on the soil more easily and favouring the emergence of the new plants. But when farmers have predictive information on weather conditions, mainly rainfall, that adjustment of dates refer to the increased use of rainfall during the first periods of crop growth. This adjustment of the planting date is generally done using any kind of simulation tools that are able to predict the soil water content and ETc act , therefore the gaininwateravailability.Adjustingthatplantingdaterequiresthatrainfallpredictionsare available to support farmers decisions. The availability of adequate Kc is also paramount. Research has confirmed that the response of crops to water stress depends on critical periods in their phenological stages. Under conditions of water scarcity, irrigation during sensitive phenological stages (e.g. reproductive stage in wheat and maize) is favoured ahead of irrigation during less sensitive stages (e.g. vegetative stage). Again, this is favoured when SWB models are used based upon weather predictions. Crop and soil monitoring are also required. Optimal crop growth and Tc may be reduced under conditions of limited supply of nutrients, fertilization and/or water, or under salinity conditions. This effect on ETc act can be accounted for in the FAO56 approach either by reducing Kc and Kcb coefficients empirically, or by adding the water stress coefficient (Ks ) as described in previous chapters. Nutrient or other shortage effects manifest through a reduction in plant growth and canopy cover, and therefore a field can experience an increase in Ke due to more soil exposure. When irrigation is insufficient, fertilization should be reduced to avoid decreasing the osmotic potential. Plant density and canopy size of perennials affect the utilization of resources. Reducing stand density makes more resources (e.g. water, nutrients) available to individual plants, in particular under rainfed agriculture. However, reduced plant density and canopy size are likely to increase Es , thus increasing non-beneficial water use, i.e. Es increases in detriment of Tc . In general, drier conditions are more suited to lower planting densities for perennials. Canopy cover is used in FAO56 guidelines to estimate the split of ET into Es and Tc , because it approximates the ratio of radiation intercepted by the canopy and incoming solar radiation. Thus, Chapter 9 provides a guideline for the estimation of Kcb from the effective fraction of ground cover, fc eff , and crop height, i.e. the A&P approach. This approach has been successfully applied to vegetable, field and tree crops and respective indicative parameters are tabulated in Chapter 9 to ease its application. The approach is particularly applicable to tree and shrub crops planted in rows given the large number of combinations of canopy sizes and densities (pruning), plant densities, row distances and orientations, wetted areas of micro-irrigation, etc. This type of modelling may be applied at the stage of planning and design of the cropping and irrigation system in order to provide for minimizing non-beneficial water consumption to assess different combinations of canopy management (pruning) and irrigation systems (e.g. drip vs micro-sprayers). However, the A&P approach, coupled with the dual Kc procedure, is mainly useful when applied to established orchards and vines to support planning and management of irrigation scheduling.348 Crop evapotranspiration Inter-cropping(describedinanearliersectionofthischapter)andagro-forestrysystemsare popular practices to optimize the use of resources (water, nutrients and radiation energy). A higher WPET can be achieved in agro-forestry systems compared to mono-cropping because soil water between tree rows is consumed by inter-crop Tc rather than by Es . Soil management practices that may modify the volume of water infiltrated and stored in the soil, thereby affecting the ratio of Es and Tc , are usually related to tillage (e.g. conservation or no-tillage), land preparation (land levelling or tilling ridge and furrows), or in-field rainwater harvesting. Soil additives and conditioners are materials used to increase the soil water and nutrient retention capacity or to reduce Es . Many of these practices affect particularly the topsoil, which is the most dynamic soil layer in terms of mass and energy exchanges and may therefore impact Kc by decreasing Ke . Less frequent irrigations reduce Es compared to frequent irrigations, but they may increase the risk of crop water stress and favour Ya reduction when ETo is high. The frequency of irrigation wetting depends on various factors, namely the storage capacity of the soil, the type of irrigation system and the ET rate. Soil evaporation is site-specific depending upon climate through ETo , the frequency of soil wetting, the use of mulches, and the fraction of ground shadowed by the canopy, which depends upon the crop density and the crop growth stage. Es can be quantified through the partitioning of ET for the considered crop, namely using the dual Kc approach. Es is limited by the shading of the crop canopy, which corresponds to limitations imposed to the available energy for evaporation at the soil surface. This approach is valid for all irrigation methods that wet the soil surface and is in proportion to the wetted fraction, as occurs for furrow irrigation, particularly, when alternate furrows are adopted. As previously described in the current chapter, mulching is an old practice of covering the soil surface to reduce soil evaporation and surface runoff, regulate soil temperature, suppress weeds, improve soil health, and therefore improve crop growth. Different materialscanbeusedformulching,suchas barkchips,straw,grass or other plantmaterial, stones or plastic sheets, and humic substances. A wide variety of environmental and management factors have been reported in literature relative to the complex crop growth response to various types of mulch. Effects of mulches are greater at earlier crop stages, when the soil is yet bare or only partially covered by the crop canopy. As a result, those areperiodswhencropcoefficientsgreatlycanvarywiththefrequencyofwettingsandsoil wetness. Kc can be estimated according to the previous mulching section of this chapter. Only used in high-tech farming, plant conditioners include anti-transpirants, bio stimulants and plant growth regulators. They are mainly applied to regulate harvesting dates of market produces, and to mitigate effects of environmental stresses. Although plant conditioners do not directly reduce non-beneficial water use, NBWUET , they affect Tc or the Tc /ETc ratio and, therefore, their effects need to be considered when estimating total water consumption and in implementing irrigation scheduling. The use of plant conditioners does not change the standard Kc and Kcb coefficients but may act similarly to the stress coefficients in defining their actual values (Kc act and Kcb act ). They may affect soil evaporation when plant conditioners decrease (or increase) the crop cover fraction due to their effects on plants. Anti-transpirants may act by reducing stomatal conductance and then the reduced Kcb may be assessed using the Fr factor described in the A&P approach (Chapter 9). These types of conditioners may become more and more prevalent in the future as research develops means to reduce transpiration from plants while sustaining inflow of carbon dioxide.349 10. ETc act under various crop management practices Various practices may be used to condition micro-climate to reduce water losses and improve WSET through Tc . A typical case is sheltered or protected cultivation. However, as ithappenswithtunnels,theFAOPM-ETo doesnotapplysincetheaerodynamicconditions are changed and do not respect the Penman-Monteith assumptions. Windbreaks, such as trees, may mitigate extreme winds and affect micro-climatic variables such as net radiation (by increased shading), temperature, humidity, heat and evaporation fluxes resulting in changes in evaporative demands and water vapour fluxes. The effects of tree windbreaks depend on surface roughness, height, orientation and porosity. Warmer, more humid and lesswindyconditionsusuallyoccurintheprotectedcropareas.Lowerwindspeedandmore humid conditions reduce vapor pressure deficit and crop ET, mostly without decreasing dry matter assimilation. Reduction of sensible heat fluxes and surface resistances, on the other hand, would most likely favor transpiration and dry matter assimilation. Higher Yact can therefore be expected in sheltered areas except when crops compete for resources with the windbreak plants (e.g. competition for water and nutrients). Generally, the trees in windbreaks are more tolerant of water shortage than agricultural crops and therefore can be irrigated less or not at all. Partial stomatal closure of the windbreak trees will cause a larger proportion of total available energy to be transformed into sensible heat rather than into evapotranspiration and total water consumption. As a result, total ET over large area of windbreaks and agriculture can be reduced. 10.7.2 Water-saving of non-consumptive water use (WSDP+RO ) Farm irrigation systems Irrigation methods can be broadly classified as surface, sprinkler and micro-irrigation. This section focuses on water-saving practices that are typical for each irrigation method and applicable on farms. It should be born in mind that costs and benefits play a major role when assessing respective water-saving issues because economic returns of irrigation tend to decrease when water-saving practices are adopted. Water consumption and use under different irrigation methods show sometimes contrasting outcomes, depending on the type of crop, canopy cover, soil properties (mainly relative to infiltration), land slope, irrigation wetted area, and irrigation management. Surface irrigation systems can have high performance when precise laser levelling is adopted, inflow rates are adequate and non-erosive, and discharge applications are well controlled. These rules apply to either basin, border or furrow irrigation. Precise land levelling for basin, furrow and border irrigation is usually fundamental for efficient application with surface irrigation methods. When applied water creates ponds on the land surface before infiltrating into the soil, it not only leads to increased evaporation losses but decreases distribution uniformity (DU) of the infiltrated water. As a result, there will be parts of the field receiving less and parts receiving excessive water. If only the average depletion is replaced, then field-average ET will be decreased due to water stress on the under-irrigated portion of the field and field-average yields will decrease. To compensate for the less irrigated zones where the crop experiences stress, the farmer may tend to apply water in excess, thus promoting high deep percolation. Several authorsreportedthatadoptingprecisionlandlevellinginbasinirrigationwithcontrolled inflow rates and respective application time duration, reduced water application by nearly 40 percent relative to using less cared applications. However, the overall water consumption (evaporation and transpiration) may be similar but the cumulative soil evaporation may be less because the land surface is then usually wetted less frequently and transpiration increases because the canopy is increased and Es is reduced.350 Crop evapotranspiration Equipment for precise control of applied flow rates and volumes is rare where surface irrigation is traditional. The use of hand held equipment is not precise in control and largely depends upon the ability of the irrigant, which is another cause of non uniformity of infiltrated volumes and, consequently, another reason why farmers may tend to apply excess water. In fact, the lack of precision control equipment makes it difficult to adopt precise scheduling in terms of managing discharge rates and volumes. For example, findings report a reduction in water application of up to 45% when surge flow on alternate furrows is used, namely due to runoff control due to using automated gated pipes. Adopting alternate furrow irrigation may additionally reduce consumptive water use through reduced water application and less soil evaporation with little reduction in yield and increased WPET as compared to conventional furrow irrigation. Achieving appropriate inflow control requires mechanization of water distribution to basins, borders or furrows, preferably automation and remote control. Thus, changing from traditional surface irrigation to modern practices requires investments that may be economically unsatisfactory. However, research has been declining and automated surface irrigation systems are not available in countries where traditional irrigation is predominant. In many regions, the trend has been to replace surface irrigation with sprinkler or micro-irrigation, which equipment is supported by strong markets as compared to those for surface irrigation equipment. Rice is traditionally cultivated in flooded paddy fields where the basins’ water depth is around 5 to 10 cm. Basins are surrounded by soil bunds that avoid RO. This water plays a large role: providing for crop ET, controlling impacts of temperature on the crop and controlling weed growth. When land levelling is improved, the flooding depth may be reduced to about 5 cm, which may decrease deep percolation (DP). When the climate is favourable, intermittent (nearly daily) flooding may be practiced with the soil water kept near saturation, thereby further decreasing DP. When infrequent irrigation by surface, sprinkler or micro-irrigation is practised with aerobic rice production, both DP and Es may decrease, however depending upon soil and land surface conditions. It is important to note that water-savings primarily refer to a reduction in DP and likely to little reduction to evaporation and the non-beneficial water consumption fraction. In other words, water use is reduced, but water consumption may be only slightly reduced. However, the standard Kc values change with the adopted management of the rice crop as tabulated in Chapter 6. As stated, net crop water requirements decrease from conventional flooding to flooding with dry seeding, flooding with anticipated cutoff and, finally, to aerobic rice. The adoption of different, specific Kc values is a condition for better achieving water saving. Sprinkler irrigation systems may be stationary or continuous moving. The former includes portable and solid set systems and side roll. Continuous moving wheeled laterals are automated and include center-pivots, linear-move systems, travelling gun sprinklers and side-move systems. A primary objective of sprinkler systems is high water distribution uniformity throughout the irrigated field. Thus, potential water saving practices aim at maintaining high DU when adopting sprinkler flow rates that favour water infiltration, spacing among sprinklers that provide for a good distribution of water throughout the field, low variability of pressure and discharge if the field is sloping, system maintenance and design for controlling wind effects. If irrigation scheduling is appropriate in terms of the crop and soil water contents dynamics, these characteristics lead to high ratios of requirement to water applied, i.e., water application efficiencies. Sprinkler systems perform best when the soil infiltration is high, and wind speed is low. Sprinkler system design, mainly referring to the351 10. ETc act under various crop management practices selection of sprinklers sizes and spacing, pressure, flow rates, water application depths and diameters of piping, is fundamental to ensure uniform distribution of pressure and flow rates, and to reduce evaporative losses. Practices and design to improve water infiltration and thereby reduce runoff require appropriate sprinkler selection. Because water applied by large sprinkler systems wets the entire ground surface, non-beneficial consumption of water can be substantial under high wind and high evaporative demand of the atmosphere. Wind-induced evaporation of spray may be highest when drops are small, and when the canopy cover is low and risers are high above the ground surface. Evaporation of spray can be reduced by placing nozzles closer to or even inside the plant canopy, using larger drop sizes and attempting to irrigate when wind speeds are low. Modern sprinkler systems, such as center-pivot and linear moving laterals, are often designed to apply small irrigation depths to avoid runoff. As a result, they must irrigate more frequently, thereby increasing total evaporation losses from soil. The more frequent irrigation reduces the risk of crop stress, but at the expense of increased Es . The dual Kc procedure can be used to estimate the increases in Ke and Es under frequent irrigation. Figures 6.1 and 6.2 can be used to estimate the increase in Kc under low vegetation cover. Micro-irrigationsystemsincludehigh-techmethodshavinglargecapitalandmaintenance investment. Systems are low-pressure and include surface and subsurface drip irrigation for row crops and orchards, micro-sprinklers and micro-sprayers for horticulture and under-tree irrigation, and bubblers for orchards. These irrigation systems wet a limited portion of the ground, targeting the crop root zone, mainly in case of orchards and vines. The limited wetting of the surface reduces the soil evaporation impact due to high frequency irrigation, thus providing for high WSET by reducing NBWUET . Achievinggoodperformancewithmicro-irrigationsystemsrequiresgooddesign,which complexity may be overcome using decision support and design models. WS practices resulting from good design aim at improving water application control, the emission uniformity (EU), and the management of irrigation, as well as automation and control. The benefits of drip irrigation compared to surface and sprinkler irrigation include reduced water application, controlled operational water losses, less labour, low or no runoff and reduced energy consumption and pumping costs per unit area, as well as improved management of water, fertilization and pesticides for a range of high-profit crops. Micro-sprinklers and micro-sprayers have similar benefits, but the wetting pattern is wider and may be affected by wind. However, for under-tree irrigation, wind drift and Es may be less than for field crops. Despite advantages, managing drip or micro-spray irrigation systems for peak performance is very demanding, including when automation is adopted. High-tech micro-irrigation methods may not be economically feasible under all conditions since, for field crops, economic returns tend to decrease when more precision water-saving techniques are adopted. Benefits are generally greater for irrigating tree and vine crops having a better developed root system compared to annual crops. Water can be easily applied near the root zone of tree and vine crops, and it may be easier to apply deficit irrigation, in particular partial root-zone drying. Soil evaporation is controlled because the soil is only partially wetted near the crops, and often under the shadow of the crop canopy. When subsurface drip is applied, ground surface wetting occurs only by capillarity from the buried emitters. Drip irrigation, and border and basin irrigation, have advantages over sprinkler systems in saline environments by avoiding the wetting of the plant canopy. Drip irrigation can have reduced accumulation of salts in the root zone due to near continuous irrigation and therefore better suitability for salt sensitive crops.352 Crop evapotranspiration In summary, the impact of the irrigation system type on consumptive water losses through evaporation and transpiration is controlled by the irrigation frequency, the fraction of soil surfacewetted,thecharacteristicsofspraydropsandtheirevaporationinthecaseofsprinkler irrigation,andthewettingofthecropcanopyinthecaseofsprinklerirrigation.Theseimpacts can be generally quantified using the dual Kc approach. Differently, the processes of deep percolationandrunoff,mainlydependupontheirrigationdesignandmanagement,thusonly indirectlyfromETandKc .However,theappropriateselectionofKc valuespermitstheuseof equipment and management tools that contribute to control DP and RO. Off-farm irrigation systems Water collection, transportation and distribution to farms may be a source of water losses incaseofopenchannelconduits,muchlesswhenpipesystemsareused.Thelatterrespond quickly to management, and farmers may often receive water on demand, thus producing only minimal losses and causing adequate implementation and adoption of irrigation water distribution on demand. Gravity canal systems have a demanding operation and regulation to control discharges to low order canals or to distributors. Then, since it is difficult to operate on-demand, thus often producing supplies with discharges and pressure heads that varyandcausedifficultiestotheirrigationschedulingbythefarmersandconsequentlosses. In addition, gravity systems respond slowly to regulation and management operative orders. Modern canal automation and permanent hydraulic monitoring, as well as distant command of gates, associated with using simulation of aggregated ETc act demands, allow an important water saving and appropriate delivery of water to the farms, thus producing WS at farm and off-farm. 10.7.3 Remote sensing techniques for irrigation crops monitoring Remote sensing (RS) data and methodologies have been increasingly used for estimating parameters related to crop water requirements, namely Kc and Kcb as for many cases collected to build their tabulated values. By providing the spatial distribution of vegetation and ET, RS provides for a better understanding of within- and among fields variability, which is important to support irrigation management ranging from local farmers up to off farm irrigation systems’ managers. The increased number of satellite missions, improved sensor technology, enhanced spatial, temporal and spectral resolutions of data, and easier data access have all encouraged the use of RS for irrigation management. A description of RS-based techniques is provided in Chapter 1. RS estimates are valuable for assessing the occurrenceofwatershortageorpoordistributionorotherimpactscausedbypoornutrient availability or salinity. The vegetation-based and thermal-based RS methods work well together as compliments in estimating both potential (standard) Kcb and actual Kc act . Overall, the accurate estimation of Kcb , Kc act and actual crop ET can be assisted by RS Improved estimation improves irrigation management and contributes both to the convergence of actual Kc and Kcb values towards better knowledge of standard values and to their distribution in space. Improved knowledge of Kc aids in achieving larger WPET and water savings, and minimizing non-beneficial water uses associated with over-irrigation. For successful application of RS, the availability of cloud-free satellite, UAV and/or manned aircraft images with an adequate temporal resolution of at least one clear image every 15 days is needed throughout the irrigation season. An important example of application of RS for irrigation management is SIMS, Satellite Irrigation Management Support. SIMS adopts the A&P approach to compute Kc and Kcb , using the fraction of ground cover estimated from RS. The model is used to support irrigation scheduling of perennials and annuals in California. The model is currently integrated with other RS irrigation management models in OpenET, which is aimed at RS advising on irrigation scheduling in the West of USA and assessing performance of RS viewed fields. Literature refers to several RS models used for monitoring and irrigators advising such as the SAMIR tool (Satellite Monitoring of Irrigation) used in Morocco, the AMSR2 used in Iran, the web-GIS weather data sets Annex 3 Statistical analysis and correction of weather data series A3.1 COMPLETING A DATA SET Quite often data sets containing a weather variable Yi observed at a given station are incomplete due to short interruptions in observations. Interruptions can be due to a large number of causes, the most frequent being the breakage or malfunction of instruments during a certain time period. When data are missing, it may be appropriate to complete these data sets from observations Xi from another nearby and reliable station. However, to use portions of data set Xi to insert in data set Yi , both data sets Xi and Yi must be homogeneous. In other words, they need to represent the same conditions throughout time. The procedure for completing data sets must be applied after a test for homogeneity and any needed correction for non-homogeneity have been performed. The data completion procedure proposed herein consists of using an appropriate regression analysis. The procedure for inserting data of a nearby station into an incomplete data set can be summarized as follows: 1. Select a nearby weather station for which the data set length covers all periods for which data are missing. 2. Characterize the data sets from the nearby station, Xi , and of the station having missing data, Yi , by computing the mean and the standard deviation sx for the nearby weather station data set (Xi ): _ n xi x =∑ − (A3.1) i=1 n _ ( n (xi -x) 2 ) ½ sx

Plotting all points xi and yi and the regression line b for the range of observed values, it can be observed if their deviations from the regression line increase or not when y increases. If deviations increase, the insertion of xi values for completing the yi series should not be performed because the two sites have a different behaviour relative to the particular weather variable under consideration and non-homogeneity may occur. The parameters r2 and b can be used as criteria for selecting the best nearby station. 5. A3.2 ANALYSIS OF THE HOMOGENEITY OF DATA SERIES Weather data collected at a given weather station during a period of several years may be non-homogeneous when the data presents a sudden change in its mean and variance in relation to the original values. This phenomenon may occur due to several causes, some of which are related to changes in instrumentation and observation practices, and others which relate to modification of the environmental conditions of the site, such as rapid urbanization or, on the contrary, perhaps development of irrigation in the area. n _ ∑ (xi -x)2 i=1 ( n _ n _ ∑ (xi -x)2 ∑ (yi -y)2 )1/2 i=1 i=1 n _ _ ∑i=1 (xi-x)(yi-y) sx sy n _ _ ∑ (xi -x)(yi -y) covxy i=1375 Annex 3: Statistical analysis of weather data sets Changes relative to data collection may be caused by: • change in grass vegetation cover, its watering and management; • change in location of the weather station, or in type of shelters; • significant change in type of vegetation surrounding the weather station; • change in type of sensor or instrument; • change of observer and/or the timing of observations; and • deterioration of sensors, or poor management of instruments leading to the malfunctioning of mechanical parts of instruments. These changes cause observations made prior and after the change to belong to statistically different populations. Quite often, the occurrence of such changes and the dates when they occurred are not known, making it necessary to access whether a given data set can be considered homogeneous and, if not, to identify the sub-data series and perform the required corrections. Procedures indicated hereafter are simple, well proven in practice and rely upon the statistical comparison of two data sets, one considered homogeneous and consisting of observations Xi performed in a nearby weather station, the other being the one under analysis and consisting of the observations Yi of the same weather variable (Tmax , Tmin , u2 , RHmax , Rs , etc). Both sets Xi and Yi should be collected at two stations that are in the same climatic region, have the same time length of observations and presenting the same trends in time despite the space variability when short time scales (daily, or 10-day) are utilized. A3.2.1 Method of Cumulative Residuals When relating two weather data sets from two weather stations, where the first is homogeneous, the data set of the second station can be considered homogeneous if their cumulative residuals from its linear regression with the first data set are not biased. The bias hypothesis can be tested for a selected probability p by verifying whether the residuals are contained within an ellipse which axis and depend on the probability p used to test the hypothesis. The magnitudes of and also depend on the size of the data set and on the standard deviation of the sample being tested. This test uses the residuals of the linear regression of Y on X as described herein before. The residuals should follow a normal distribution with mean zero and standard deviation sy,x , i.e. the error Ԑi ∈ N (0,sy,x ). Thus, the residuals εi must be independent random variables, i.e., they should exhibit homoscedasticity. The procedure for analysing the homogeneity of a weather data set Yi of a given weather station can be summarized as follows: 1. Select a reference weather station inside the same climatic region that is known to have a homogeneous data set Xi of the same weather variable. As an alternative, construct a “regional” homogeneous data set by averaging the observations at several weather stations in the same region. 2. Organize both data sets with their values xi and yi (i = 1, 2,..., n) in chronological order, where the initial timing and the time increment are identical for both data sets. 3. For both data sets, compute the mean and standard deviation (Eq. A3.1 to A3.4) for the homogeneous variable (xi ) and for the variable to be tested (yi ).376 Crop evapotranspiration 4. Calculate the regression line between the two variables yi and xi and the associated correlation coefficient (Eq. A3.5 to A3.8). The regression equation among the full data sets (subscript f) is expressed as: ŷi = af + bf xi

Whenever possible, plot xi , yi and the regression line to visually verify whether the homoscedasticity hypothesis can be accepted (see Figure A3.1), i.e., when the deviations of yi to the regression estimates ŷi are within the same range for all xi , thus when those deviations are not increasing (or decreasing) when the values of xi are increasing. Station Y Pi (mm) Station X, Pi (mm) Data in this example were provided by J.L. Teixeira (personal communication, 1995) 5. Compute the residuals of the observed yi values to the regression line (Ԑi ) (Eq. 6. Select a probability p for accepting the hypothesis of homogeneity. The probability p = 80% for non-exceedance is commonly used. When the computed values for Ei fall inside the ellipse(Eq.A3.17)itcanbeconcluded,atthelevelofprobabilityp,thatthereisnobias in the distribution of residuals, i.e., the data set yi may be assumed as homogeneous. 7. Plot the cumulative residuals Ei against time using the time scale (interval) of the variable under analysis (Figure A3.2). 8. Draw the ellipse on the same plot and verify whether all Ei lie inside the ellipse. If theydo,thenthehypothesisofhomogeneityisacceptedattheplevelofconfidence. 9. If the hypothesis of homogeneity cannot be accepted (this is the case in Figure A3.2), then one can select the break point where it appears that Ei ceases to increase (or to decrease) and begins to decrease (or to increase), for example at i = 16 in Figure A3.2. This break point is termed k = i. Then, new regression equations are computed between Y and X for both subsets. If we presume that the second subset is homogeneous but that the first is not, then we have: ŷi = anh + bnh xi

ŷi = ah +bh xi (i = k + 1, k + 2, ...,n) (A3.19) wherethesubscriptshandnhidentifytheregressioncoefficientsofthehomogeneous and the non-homogeneous subsets, respectively (see Figure A3.3). Compute the differences between the two regression lines: Δŷi = (ah +bh xi ) - (anh +bnh xi ) (A3.20) for the non-homogeneous set (i = 1, 2, ..., k) 12. Correct the non-homogeneous subset portion of data set Y: yc,i = yi +Δŷi (i = 1, 2, ...,k) (A3.21)379 Annex 3: Statistical analysis of weather data sets where the subscript c identifies the corrected values. Thus, the corrected, homogeneous full set for weather variable Y consists of: Yi = yc,i for i = 1, 2, …., k and (A3.22) Yi = yi for i = k + 1, k + 2, …., n A similar procedure would be utilized if it was presumed that the second sub-set requires correction, rather than the first one. Note that the variables Yi are still considered to be random variables despite that the mean and the variance have been modified due to the correction introduced. To confirm the results of the correction of data set Y for homogeneity, the homogeneity test methodology can be applied again to the corrected variable Y to provide evidence of homogeneity in the graph of residuals. This has been done in Figure A3.4.
