It was therefore presumed that prior to time k, the readings were biased by instrument calibration, different location of the station or the instrument within the station, change in type or manufacturer of the instrument, or change in general environment of the station. It appears in Figure A3.3 that the data prior to i = k were biased downward by approximately 100 mm of annual precipitation. A3.2.2 Double-Mass technique The double-mass technique is also useful for assessing homogeneity in a weather data set. As with the method of cumulative residuals discussed above, the double-mass technique requires data sets from two weather stations, where Xi (i = 1, 2,...,n) is a380 Crop evapotranspiration chronologic data set for a given weather variable observed for a certain time length at a “reference” station which is considered to be homogeneous, and where Yi is a data set of the same variable, with the same time length, observed at another station and for which homogeneity needs to be analysed. In the double-mass technique, starting with the first observed pair of values X1 and Y1 , cumulative data sets are created by progressively summing values of Xi and Yi to verify whether the long-term trends in variation of Xi and Yi are the same. Thus, the following cumulative variables are obtained: i - 1 xi = Xi ∑ Xj (A3.23) j = 1 i - 1 yi =Yi ∑ Yj (A3.24) j = 1 with i = 1, ..., n and j = 1,..., i − 1. FIGURE A3.5 Double mass analysis applied to two series of precipitation when data from station Y are not homogeneous

32000 28000 24000 20000 16000 12000 8000 4000 0 Station X,∑ Pi (mm) Station Y ∑ Pi (mm) Source: FAO, 1998 Thesevariablesxi andyi arestillconsideredtoberandomvariablesandarecharacterized by the mean and the standard deviation (Eq. A3.1 to A3.4). The yi and xi variables can be related through linear regression (Eq. A3.5 to A3.8). However, the double mass technique is typically applied as a graphical procedure.381 Annex 3: Statistical analysis of weather data sets The graphical application of the double-mass analysis is done by plotting all coordinate points xi and yi (Figure A3.5). The plot is then visually analysed to determine whether successive points of xi and yi follow a unique straight line, indicating the homogeneity of the data set Yi relative to data set Xi . If there appears to be a break (or more than one break) in the plot of yi to xi , then there is a visual indication that the data series Yi (or perhaps Xi ) is not homogeneous (Figure A3.5). The break at coordinates xk and yk can be used to separate two subsets (i = 1, 2, ..., k) and (k + 1, k + 2, ..., n). One of the subsets is to be corrected. The appropriate one to correct needs to be identified by consulting the records of the weather station, when available. i Residuals ɛi (mm) Source: FAO, 1998 Often, visual interpretation of the double-mass balance is difficult. Thus, the following numerical regression procedure is recommended. 1. Compute the regression line through the origin for the full set of data xi and yi ŷi = bxi

with b=−−−−−−−−−−−−− (A3.25a) ∑(xi -x̄)2 2. Compute the residuals to the regression line: Ԑi = yi − bxi (A3.26) 3. Analyse the distribution of residuals: If the residuals plot as independent, random variables, then the set can be considered to be homogeneous. However, if the distribution of residuals is biased over i = k, then the homogeneity hypothesis _ ∑(xi -x̄)(yi - y)382 Crop evapotranspiration is rejected. The bias can be visually assessed by plotting (Ԑi , i). The example in Figure A3.6 shows that residuals follow a trend of decreasing Ԑi until i = k (= 16). Following that, the trend is to increase. This plot demonstrates a bias indicating that the data set Y is not homogeneous. 4. The break point at i = k defines two subsets (i = 1, 2, ..., k) and (i = k + 1, k + 2, ..., n). Using local information on data collection, the user must decide which subset requires correction. 5. When the first subset is homogeneous: a. compute the two regression lines, the first through the origin ŷi = bh xi

ŷnh,i = anh + bnh xi (i = k + 1, k + 2, ..., n) (A3.28) where subscripts h and nh identify respectively the homogenous and non homogeneous subsets. b. compute the differences between both regression lines for i = k + 1, k + 2, ..., n Δŷi = bh xi -(anh + bnh xi ) (A3.29) 6. When the second subset is homogeneous a. compute the regression line for the homogeneous subset (i = k +1, k + 2, ..., n) after correcting the coordinates (xi , yi ) using the coordinates of the break point (xk , yk ), i.e. moving the origin of coordinates from (0, 0) to (xk , yk ). This regression is therefore: yi – yk = bh (xi – xk ) (A3.30) thus ŷi = (yk - bh xk ) +bn xi (i = k + 1, k + 2, ..., n) (A3.31) b. compute the regression line for the non-homogeneous (nh) subset forced to the origin: ŷi = bnh xi

c. compute the differences between the regression lines (A3.31) and (A3.32): Δŷi = [(yk - bh xk ) + bh xi ] - bnh xi (A3.33) 7. For both cases, correct the variables yi corresponding to the non-homogeneous subset as: yc,i = yi + Δŷi (A3.34) with Δŷi given by Eq. A3.29 or A3.33. 8. ComputethecorrectedestimatesoftheweathervariablesYi bysolvingEq.A3.24forYi . Figure A3.8 is a plot of the corresponding residuals, which now follow a normal distribution. Responses of drip irrigated tomato (Solanum lycopersicum L.) yield, quality and water productivity to various soil matric potential thresholds in an arid region of Northwest China. Agric. Water Manage. 129, 181–193.Crop evapotranspiration As global agricultural systems face increasing pressure from climate variability, water scarcity, and the growing demand for food, there is a critical need for updated, science-based tools that support efficient and sustainable water use in agriculture. The original FAO Irrigation and Drainage Paper No. 56, published in 1998, set a global standard for computing crop evapotranspiration and estimating crop water requirements. Its methodologies have become integral to irrigation planning, agricultural water management, and policy development worldwide. However, advancements in data science, sensor technology, and climate modeling—coupled with emerging challenges such as prolonged droughts and changing weather patterns—have made it essential to revisit and strengthen the technical foundations of this cornerstone publication. The revised FAO 56 responds directly to this need, integrating modern tools such as remote sensing, IoT applications, and enhanced energy balance methods to improve the precision, relevance, and accessibility of crop water estimation under current and future climate conditions. This updated edition not only builds on the scientific rigor and widespread adoption of the original guidelines but also introduces practical innovations tailored to regional realities, particularly in water-stressed environments. By refining crop coefficient estimation, optimizing irrigation scheduling, and incorporating adaptive strategies, the revised FAO 56 provides a comprehensive framework for addressing the water–food–climate nexus. This publication will serve as a critical technical reference for governments, development agencies, researchers, and practitioners committed to enhancing water productivity, ensuring food security, and promoting climate-resilient agriculture. Second edition, revised 2025 Guidelines for computing crop water requirements 56 Rev.1 CD6621EN/1/12.25
